COUNTRY: Burundi
TITLE: Burundi — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/burundi
META DESCRIPTION: Burundi is a majority-Christian country where most believers worship freely. However, Jehovah’s Witnesses remain banned, some Protestant groups face registration barriers, and churches that engaged in the 2015 political crisis faced government pressure.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Burundi, East Africa, Christian Majority, Jehovah’s Witnesses, Political Persecution, Religious Restrictions
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Burundi is a small, densely populated East African nation that is approximately 90% Christian — one of the highest proportions in Africa. The Catholic Church has deep roots and significant institutional presence; Protestant denominations, including evangelicals and Pentecostals, have grown substantially. The vast majority of Burundians worship freely. However, Burundi is not without religious freedom concerns: Jehovah’s Witnesses have been banned since 2019, some evangelical groups face registration difficulties, and the political crisis that began in 2015 created an environment in which churches that spoke critically of the government faced pressure, surveillance, and intimidation.

## The 2015 Crisis and Church Suppression

When President Nkurunziza sought a disputed third term in 2015, mass protests erupted. Security forces violently suppressed the protests; an estimated 1,200 people were killed, 400,000 fled the country. Several Catholic bishops issued public statements opposing the third term. The government responded angrily, accusing the church of political interference. Some pastors and church leaders who sheltered protesters or spoke out publicly were threatened.

## Jehovah’s Witnesses and Minority Groups

In 2019, the government banned Jehovah’s Witnesses, ordering their dissolution and prohibiting their meetings. The stated reason was failure to comply with registration requirements, but independent observers agreed the government’s real concern was the group’s refusal to participate in political structures. Witnesses were arrested for holding meetings; their properties were seized. As of early 2025, the ban remained in effect. This represents a genuine and ongoing violation of religious freedom, regardless of the theological distinctions between the Witnesses and mainstream Christianity.

## Current Status

The overwhelming majority of Christians in Burundi — Catholic and mainstream Protestant — worship without significant interference. The concerns are real but targeted. Burundi is not in the Open Doors World Watch List’s top tiers.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90% (Catholic majority)
- **Primary Concerns:** Jehovah’s Witnesses ban (2019–present); political pressure on churches since 2015
- **Current Status:** Majority Christian community worships freely; specific groups face real restrictions
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not in top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Burundi* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/burundi/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Burundi — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/burundi/
- Human Rights Watch, *Burundi country reports* — hrw.org/africa/burundi
- Amnesty International, *Burundi* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/burundi/

COUNTRY: Cambodia
TITLE: Cambodia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/cambodia
META DESCRIPTION: Cambodia’s Christian community was nearly annihilated by the Khmer Rouge between 1975 and 1979. The church has been rebuilt from almost nothing over the past three decades, now numbering 250,000–350,000 in a Buddhist-majority nation where legal freedom exists but social discrimination persists.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Cambodia, Southeast Asia, Buddhism, Khmer Rouge, Church Rebuilding, House Churches, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Cambodia’s Christianity carries one of the most dramatic survival stories in the modern church. The Khmer Rouge regime of 1975–1979 — one of the most ideologically extreme and brutally violent governments in human history — systematically dismantled every dimension of Cambodian civil and religious life. By 1979, organized Christianity in Cambodia had been essentially destroyed. What has happened since — the slow, painful rebuilding of a church from near zero — is a story of extraordinary resilience. Today, Cambodia’s Christian community numbers an estimated 250,000–350,000 in a nation of 17 million.

## Colonial-Era Christianity

Catholic missionaries arrived in Cambodia during the French colonial period. The Christian and Missionary Alliance established work in Cambodia beginning in 1923, making significant progress in translating scripture and training Cambodian church leaders. By the early 1970s, Cambodia had an estimated 60,000 Christians and a functioning institutional church.

## The Khmer Rouge Genocide (1975–1979)

The Khmer Rouge took power on April 17, 1975. Their revolution sought to construct “Year Zero” — a completely new society that rejected everything connected to the ancien régime. The genocide killed an estimated 1.7–2.2 million Cambodians — roughly a quarter of the population. Christianity was identified as a foreign religion connected to the French colonizers and Western imperialism. Churches were closed immediately. Buildings were demolished, repurposed, or burned. Clergy and church leaders were executed. By 1979, the church that had taken decades to build had been essentially eliminated.

## Rebuilding from Zero (1979–Present)

When the United Nations Transitional Authority in Cambodia (UNTAC) oversaw a democratic transition in 1991–1993, Cambodia opened to the outside world. Christian organizations entered the country, provided humanitarian assistance, and began supporting the rebuilding of the church. From near zero in 1979, Cambodia’s Christian population has grown to an estimated 250,000–350,000 today — representing perhaps 2–3% of the population.

## Legal Status and Social Challenges

Cambodia’s constitution guarantees religious freedom. Buddhism is recognized as the state religion and is deeply woven into Cambodian cultural identity, but the constitution does not prohibit other faiths. In some rural communities, where Buddhist identity and village social structure are tightly integrated, conversion to Christianity is viewed as a rejection of community and ancestors. Converts face family pressure, social ostracism, and occasionally more formal community-level sanctions. Pastors and church leaders occasionally report harassment from local officials.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** 250,000–350,000 (~2–3% of population)
- **Primary Faith Context:** Theravada Buddhist majority (~96%)
- **Primary Challenges:** Social pressure on converts, local-level harassment, Buddhist nationalist sentiment
- **Legal Status:** Formally free; constitution guarantees religious freedom
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not in top tier
- **Historical Trauma:** Khmer Rouge (1975–1979) systematically destroyed organized Cambodian Christianity; church rebuilt from near zero

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Cambodia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/cambodia/
- CIA World Factbook: Cambodia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/cambodia/
- Open Doors USA, *Cambodia Country Profile* — opendoorsusa.org
- Yale Cambodian Genocide Program — gsp.yale.edu/cambodian-genocide-program
- Kiernan, Ben, *The Pol Pot Regime: Race, Power, and Genocide in Cambodia under the Khmer Rouge, 1975–79* (Yale University Press, 2008)
- Joshua Project, *Cambodia* — joshuaproject.net

COUNTRY: Cameroon
TITLE: Cameroon — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/cameroon
META DESCRIPTION: Cameroon is a majority-Christian nation where believers in the predominantly Muslim north face Boko Haram violence and Islamic pressure. The Anglophone crisis in the west has also devastated Christian communities caught between separatist militias and the military.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Cameroon, Central Africa, Boko Haram, Anglophone Crisis, Islam, Christianity, Religious Violence
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Cameroon is often called “Africa in miniature” for the diversity compressed within its borders — savanna and rainforest, Francophone and Anglophone regions, Muslim north and Christian south. Approximately 70% of Cameroonians identify as Christian, 20% as Muslim, and 10% as adherents of traditional religions. The Christian majority worships largely freely, and the country has historically been one of West-Central Africa’s more stable and pluralistic environments. But two converging crises have made Cameroon increasingly dangerous for Christians in specific regions: the Boko Haram insurgency in the Far North region, which has attacked churches and Christian villages near the Lake Chad basin; and the Anglophone crisis in the Northwest and Southwest regions, where armed separatist conflict has created a humanitarian disaster that has ravaged Christian communities.

## Historical Roots of Christianity

Christianity arrived in Cameroon through multiple channels. Catholic missionaries — primarily German and Swiss — came in the late 19th century under the German colonial administration, and the Basel Mission established Protestant work in the coastal region. British Baptist missionaries had already been active on the coast from the 1840s, with Alfred Saker founding the first Baptist mission in Douala in 1845. When Germany lost its African colonies after World War I, Cameroon was divided between British and French administration — a division that produced the French-speaking majority and English-speaking minority whose tensions would resurface catastrophically in the 21st century.

Christianity spread deeply through both colonial halves of the country. Catholic institutions — schools, hospitals, and churches — became foundational to social infrastructure in the south. Presbyterian, Baptist, and other Protestant missions created a strong tradition of educated, organized Anglophone Protestant communities in the west.

## Islam in the North and Boko Haram

Cameroon’s Far North region borders Nigeria and Lake Chad and has historically been Muslim under the influence of the Fulani-led Sokoto Caliphate and successor states. From approximately 2014 onward, Boko Haram and its splinter faction ISWAP conducted raids into Cameroon’s Far North, targeting military posts, civilian villages, and churches. Kidnappings for ransom became common — including Christian aid workers, pastors, and civilians. Churches in border communities have been burned. Christians have been killed in village raids in which attackers distinguished between Muslim and Christian residents.

## The Anglophone Crisis

The Anglophone crisis began in 2016 when Anglophone teachers and lawyers protested the imposition of French-language courts and curricula in the English-speaking Northwest and Southwest regions. The government’s heavy-handed crackdown on protesters radicalized a segment of the population, and armed separatist groups coalesced around the goal of creating an independent state called Ambazonia. The Cameroonian military launched a counterinsurgency campaign that has been documented — by Amnesty International, Human Rights Watch, and others — to include extrajudicial killings, burning of villages, and torture of detainees.

The crisis is not religious in its origins — it is linguistic, constitutional, and ethnic. But its victims are overwhelmingly Christian, because the Anglophone regions are predominantly Christian (Presbyterian, Baptist, Catholic). Churches have been caught between armed separatist groups, who demand boycotts on pain of violence, and the military, which has at times fired on civilian targets. Pastors have been abducted by separatists demanding money; others have been killed. Church buildings have been burned. An estimated 600,000 people are internally displaced in the Anglophone regions, with hundreds of thousands more having fled to Nigeria.

## Current Status

Cameroon is not in the Open Doors World Watch List top tier but sits on its boundary. The majority Christian population in the south and center worships freely. The pressures are regional and specific — Boko Haram violence in the Far North, and the Anglophone crisis in the northwest and southwest — but in those regions, the effects on Christian communities are severe.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~70%
- **Primary Threats:** Boko Haram violence (Far North); Anglophone crisis displacing and killing Christians in Northwest/Southwest
- **Current Status:** Majority worships freely; specific regions experiencing serious violence
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not in top tier; Anglophone crisis and Boko Haram activity are monitored concerns
- **Displaced:** ~600,000+ in Anglophone regions alone

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Cameroon* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/cameroon/
- CIA World Factbook: Cameroon — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/cameroon/
- Human Rights Watch, *Cameroon: Anglophone Crisis reporting* — hrw.org/africa/cameroon
- Amnesty International, *Cameroon* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/cameroon/
- International Crisis Group, *Cameroon reporting* — crisisgroup.org
- UN OCHA, *Cameroon Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/cameroon
- UNHCR, *Cameroon* — unhcr.org
- UN Security Council, *Lake Chad Basin reporting*
- Aid to the Church in Need, *Cameroon reporting* — acn-intl.org

COUNTRY: Canada
TITLE: Canada — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/canada
META DESCRIPTION: Canada is a constitutionally free, majority-Christian nation. Christians worship and speak freely, though recent years have brought court challenges over religious expression, pandemic-related church restrictions, and arson attacks on Catholic churches following the residential schools crisis.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Canada, North America, Religious Freedom, Catholic, Protestant, Residential Schools, Church Arson
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Canada is a majority-Christian country — approximately 63% of Canadians identified as Christian in the 2021 census — with full constitutional protection of religious freedom under the Canadian Charter of Rights and Freedoms. Christians in Canada are not persecuted by any international standard. The country ranks among the world’s strongest guarantors of religious liberty and has no history of state-directed violence or systematic oppression of Christians.

Canada is not on the Open Doors World Watch List, nor should it be. However, the country’s recent Christian history includes several notable developments that GWM users may encounter: the residential schools reckoning and the wave of church arsons that followed, pandemic-related court battles over church closures, and ongoing legal disputes over the limits of religious expression in public life.

## Christianity in Canada

Christianity came to Canada through French Catholic and British Protestant colonization. The Catholic Church dominated French Canada — Quebec in particular — from the colonial era through the early 20th century. The Quiet Revolution of the 1960s produced one of the most dramatic and rapid secularizations in modern history: Quebec went from being among the most observant Catholic societies in the West to among the least in under two generations.

English Canada developed a Protestant-dominated civic culture through the 19th and early 20th centuries, shaped by Anglican, Methodist, Presbyterian, and later Baptist and Pentecostal communities. Mass immigration in the 20th century diversified the religious landscape substantially.

## The Residential Schools Crisis and Church Arsons

In May 2021, ground-penetrating radar surveys of former residential school sites in British Columbia identified what were initially reported as the unmarked remains of 215 children at the former Kamloops Indian Residential School. The story generated national and international attention and reopened profound wounds about the residential school system — a network of church-run (primarily Catholic, Anglican, and United Church), government-funded schools that separated Indigenous children from their families from the 1880s through the 1990s, with the explicit goal of cultural assimilation. Canada’s Truth and Reconciliation Commission documented its devastating impact, including physical and sexual abuse, cultural destruction, and thousands of deaths.

In the months following the Kamloops announcement, more than 80 Catholic and Protestant churches across Canada were burned or vandalized. The arsons were widely understood as acts of anger directed at institutions associated with the residential school system. Pope Francis visited Canada in July 2022 and issued a formal apology on Canadian soil.

## Legal and Cultural Tensions

Pandemic church closures (2020–2022) generated significant legal conflict, including extended battles in Alberta involving GraceLife Church. Legal debates over religious expression continue in areas including religious adoption agencies, religious education, and street preaching. These cases are real, important for the church’s public witness, and legally contested — but they do not constitute persecution by any globally comparative standard.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~63% (declining; active attendance significantly lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no systematic persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable Issues:** 80+ church arsons (2021) following residential schools news; pandemic-era church closure conflicts; ongoing religious expression legal debates

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Canada* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/canada/
- Statistics Canada, *2021 Census: Religion* — statcan.gc.ca
- Truth and Reconciliation Commission of Canada, *Final Report* (2015) — nctr.ca/records/reports/
- CIA World Factbook: Canada — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/canada/

COUNTRY: Central African Republic
TITLE: Central African Republic — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/central-african-republic
META DESCRIPTION: The Central African Republic has endured a decade of sectarian catastrophe. Muslim-majority armed groups have massacred Christian civilians, burned churches, and seized territory in one of Africa’s most severe humanitarian emergencies. The church has suffered immensely and continues to suffer.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Central African Republic, CAR, Central Africa, Sectarian Violence, Muslim Militias, Seleka, Anti-Balaka, WWL Top 50, Church Attacks
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #32 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

The Central African Republic — one of the world’s poorest and most fragile states — has experienced a decade of catastrophic sectarian violence that has left the church scarred and the country in ruins. The crisis began in 2013 when a predominantly Muslim coalition of armed groups called Séléka seized the capital Bangui and overthrew the government. What followed was a spiral of atrocity: Séléka fighters targeted Christian communities across the country, killing civilians, burning churches, and driving hundreds of thousands from their homes. A counter-force of predominantly Christian and animist militias, the Anti-Balaka, formed in response and committed their own atrocities against Muslim communities. The violence fractured the country along religious lines in ways it has never fully recovered from. CAR ranked #32 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Christianity in the CAR

Christianity arrived in the Central African Republic through French colonial missions in the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Catholic and Protestant missions established churches, schools, and medical work that became foundational to social life. By independence in 1960, Christianity was the dominant faith in the south and center of the country, and the church had genuine institutional depth through its educational and medical networks.

## The Séléka Crisis (2013–Ongoing)

Séléka — a coalition of predominantly Muslim armed groups that included fighters from Chad and Sudan as well as Central African rebels — swept into Bangui in March 2013, forcing President François Bozizé into exile. In the areas they controlled, Séléka fighters conducted a campaign of systematic violence against Christian populations. Churches were burned — in some areas systematically, not opportunistically. Priests and pastors were killed. Christian villages were looted and their inhabitants killed or driven out. By 2014, an estimated one million people — roughly a quarter of the country’s population — had been displaced.

The Anti-Balaka militias that arose in opposition to Séléka committed their own atrocities, primarily against Muslim civilians and communities. They drove most of the Muslim population out of Bangui and from significant areas of the south and west. The conflict became self-reinforcing: each atrocity generated counter-violence, and the category of “Christian” and “Muslim” became lines of survival or death regardless of what any individual actually believed.

## The Humanitarian Scale

The United Nations Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs has consistently listed the CAR among the world’s top humanitarian emergencies. Approximately 3 million people — out of a population of around 5 million — are in need of humanitarian assistance; over 700,000 are internally displaced; over 600,000 are refugees in neighboring countries. For the Christian community specifically: hundreds of churches have been destroyed, damaged, or seized. Catholic missions that had functioned for over a century were looted and burned.

Cardinal Dieudonné Nzapalainga — Archbishop of Bangui — became internationally recognized for his courage in mediating between communities and sheltering civilians during the worst violence. He is both a witness to and a symbol of the church’s suffering and resilience.

## Russian Wagner Influence and Continued Instability

Since approximately 2018, the Russian Wagner Group has been the CAR government’s primary military partner, displacing French forces and United Nations peacekeepers. Wagner forces have been documented by UN investigators as committing atrocities against civilians — including Christian communities — in their operations against armed groups.

## The Church Today

In Bangui, Catholic and Protestant congregations meet and worship. The church has been among the most active voices for peace and reconciliation — Cardinal Nzapalainga, along with Muslim and Protestant leaders, formed a platform for interreligious dialogue that has been internationally recognized. In the interior, the situation is harder. Pastors in armed-group-controlled areas practice at risk. Congregations have been scattered by displacement. Churches have been burned.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #32
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~89%
- **Primary Threats:** Armed groups (Séléka factions, CPC coalition), ongoing sectarian violence, state collapse
- **Displacement:** 700,000+ internally displaced; 600,000+ refugees in neighboring countries
- **Churches:** Hundreds destroyed, damaged, or seized since 2013
- **Notable:** Cardinal Nzapalainga — Archbishop of Bangui — internationally recognized voice for peace and interreligious dialogue

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Central African Republic* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/central-african-republic/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Central African Republic* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/central-african-republic/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Chad
TITLE: Chad — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/chad
META DESCRIPTION: Chad is a majority-Muslim nation where Christians — concentrated in the south — face Boko Haram violence, Islamic law in the north, and social pressure on converts. The state is fragile and the church operates in a complex, often dangerous environment.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Chad, Central Africa, Islam, Boko Haram, Christian South, Religious Restrictions, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Chad is divided roughly in half between a predominantly Muslim north and a predominantly Christian and animist south — a geographic and cultural split that has shaped the country’s politics, conflicts, and religious life since independence in 1960. Christians represent approximately 44% of the population; Muslims approximately 52%. The two communities have coexisted with periodic tension, but Chad has not descended into the kind of systematic sectarian violence seen in the Central African Republic or Sudan. The primary threats facing Christians are Boko Haram violence in the Lake Chad basin, Islamic law applied by custom in northern communities, social pressure on converts from Islam, and the profound fragility of a state that has been governed under military-linked regimes for most of its history.

## Christianity in Chad

Catholic and Protestant missionaries established the foundation of Chadian Christianity during the French colonial period — the White Fathers in the south, and Protestant missions in the center. Christianity took strong root among the Sara and other southern ethnic groups, who constitute the demographic base of the country’s Christian community. Since independence, Chad has been governed almost continuously by northern Muslim leaders, creating a political dynamic in which the Christian south has often felt politically marginalized.

## Boko Haram and Lake Chad Basin Violence

Chad’s Lake Chad basin has been a theater of Boko Haram and ISWAP violence since approximately 2015. Chadian military forces have been among the more effective in the Multinational Joint Task Force countering Boko Haram, and Chad has suffered significant military casualties. Civilian communities in the lake basin — including Christian fishing communities — have been attacked, displaced, and killed. Churches in this region have been burned and Christians have been specifically targeted in some raids.

## Converts and Social Pressure

Chad has no formal apostasy law in its penal code, but Islamic customary practice in northern communities treats apostasy as a serious offense. Converts risk family rejection, loss of inheritance, loss of housing, and in some cases physical violence. Pastors who disciple converts from Muslim backgrounds are targeted with threats.

## Current Status

Chad is not in the Open Doors top tier but is monitored. The southern Christian community practices largely freely in its own territory. The threats are concentrated in the Lake Chad basin (Boko Haram violence), the north (Islamic customary pressure on converts), and in the social consequences for individuals who leave Islam.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~44%
- **Primary Threats:** Boko Haram violence (Lake Chad basin); social persecution of converts from Islam; political marginalization of the Christian south
- **Current Status:** Southern Christians largely free; northern Christians and converts face serious pressure
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Chad* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/chad/
- CIA World Factbook: Chad — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/chad/
- UN OCHA, *Lake Chad Basin Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org
- International Crisis Group, *Chad and Lake Chad Basin reporting* — crisisgroup.org
- Human Rights Watch, *Chad country reports* — hrw.org/africa/chad
- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Chile
TITLE: Chile — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/chile
META DESCRIPTION: Chile is a majority-Christian nation with full religious freedom. The Catholic Church has faced serious institutional reckoning over sexual abuse scandals, but Christians face no state persecution or systematic discrimination.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Chile, South America, Catholic, Evangelical, Religious Freedom, Abuse Scandal
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Chile is overwhelmingly Christian — approximately 65% Catholic and 17% evangelical Protestant, with a growing segment identifying as non-religious. The country has full constitutional religious freedom; no denomination faces persecution, and the state is formally secular. The most significant challenge facing the Chilean church in recent decades has not been external persecution but internal reckoning: the Catholic Church’s clergy sexual abuse scandal reached catastrophic proportions in Chile, leading to mass resignations, papal intervention, and a crisis of institutional trust that dramatically accelerated secularization.

Christianity came to Chile through Spanish colonization in the 16th century. The Franciscan, Dominican, and Jesuit orders established missions throughout the country, and Catholicism became the cultural and legal foundation of colonial Chilean society. Pentecostalism arrived in the early 20th century and has grown dramatically; Chile is home to some of the largest Pentecostal congregations in Latin America.

In 2018, Pope Francis accepted the resignations of three Chilean bishops following a Vatican investigation into clerical abuse — a landmark moment in the global accountability crisis facing the Catholic Church. Chile is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~82%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable:** Catholic clergy abuse scandal produced significant institutional crisis and accelerated secularization

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Chile* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/chile/
- CIA World Factbook: Chile — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/chile/
- Pew Research Center, *Religion in Latin America* (2014) — pewresearch.org/religion/2014/11/13/religion-in-latin-america/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: China
TITLE: China — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/china
META DESCRIPTION: China is home to one of the world’s largest and fastest-growing Christian populations — and one of its most comprehensive systems of state religious control. House churches are raided, pastors imprisoned, crosses demolished, and Bibles restricted. The church grows despite everything.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: China, East Asia, CCP, House Church, Three-Self Church, Persecution, WWL Top 20, Religious Control
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #15 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

China presents one of the most complex and consequential Christian situations in the world. The country is home to an estimated 38–97 million Christians — a number that, if accurate, makes China’s Christian population larger than the population of most European nations. It is also a country with one of the most comprehensive state systems for controlling, monitoring, and suppressing unsanctioned religious expression in the world. The Chinese Communist Party (CCP) does not oppose Christianity as such — it tolerates and manages approved, state-controlled Christianity through the Three-Self Patriotic Movement (TSPM) and the Chinese Patriotic Catholic Association (CPCA). What it cannot tolerate is Christianity — or any institution — that operates outside its control, grows beyond its visibility, or expresses loyalty to any authority above the Party. China ranked #15 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## The Historical Arc of Christianity in China

Christianity has reached China multiple times across history. Nestorian Christianity arrived via the Silk Road in the 7th century AD — the famous Nestorian Stele, erected in Xian in 781 AD and rediscovered by missionaries in the 17th century, records the presence of a functioning Nestorian Christian community during the Tang Dynasty. Matteo Ricci’s Jesuit mission approach in the 16th and 17th centuries — learning Chinese, adopting Confucian intellectual frameworks, engaging the emperor’s court — produced a sophisticated and initially successful Catholic mission. Robert Morrison of the London Missionary Society arrived in 1807 and produced the first Chinese Protestant Bible translation. Hudson Taylor founded the China Inland Mission in 1865 with the explicit goal of reaching China’s interior.

## The Communist Revolution and the Three-Self Church

The CCP’s victory in 1949 ended the era of foreign missionary presence. Missionaries were expelled; Chinese Christians who had worked with foreign missions were pressured to sever ties. The Three-Self Patriotic Movement was established in 1954 as the official Protestant body — “three-self” referring to self-governance, self-support, and self-propagation, principles repurposed by the CCP to mean state-controlled and independent of foreign influence.

The Cultural Revolution (1966–1976) was catastrophic for all religion. Churches were closed, ransacked, or destroyed. Bibles were burned. Clergy were persecuted, imprisoned, and in some cases killed. Public religious practice of any kind was banned. The period produced enormous suffering — and, paradoxically, a deepening of faith among those who maintained it under pressure.

## The House Church Movement

China’s house churches are the heart of what is likely the world’s fastest-growing major Christian community. These are unregistered congregations ranging from small family prayer groups to sophisticated urban networks with thousands of members, trained pastoral leadership, and theological depth. House churches exist across a spectrum. Some are theologically conservative, tracing their lineage through the underground networks of the Cultural Revolution. Others are charismatic and evangelical, shaped by Korean and Western influence.

The CCP’s fundamental objection to house churches is not theological but institutional: they represent organized civil society outside Party control, with loyalty structures that compete with loyalty to the Party and state.

## Xi Jinping’s “Sinicization” Campaign

Under President Xi Jinping, religious policy has significantly tightened. The concept of “Sinicization of religion” — articulated clearly from 2015 onward — requires that all religious practice in China be adapted to “Chinese culture” and “socialist core values” as defined by the Party. In practice, this has meant:

Regulations on Religious Affairs revised in 2018 tightening registration requirements, banning religious education of minors, restricting online religious content, and requiring religious personnel to demonstrate political loyalty.

A cross-removal campaign that began in Zhejiang Province in 2014 and spread nationally — affecting an estimated 1,500–2,000 churches in Zhejiang alone.

Increased surveillance of church activities, including facial recognition at church entrances, CCTV monitoring, and requirements that churches submit member lists to authorities.

Closure and demolition of churches in some areas, including officially registered Three-Self buildings deemed too prominent.

Pastor Wang Yi of Early Rain Covenant Church in Chengdu was arrested in December 2018, along with approximately 100 members of his congregation, in a coordinated raid. He was sentenced to nine years in prison on charges including “inciting subversion of state power.” He remained imprisoned as of early 2025.

## The Catholic Situation

The Vatican’s 2018 provisional agreement with China — which gave Beijing a formal role in the appointment of Catholic bishops — was deeply controversial within the Catholic community. The underground Catholic Church, which had maintained loyalty to Rome through decades of persecution, felt abandoned. Implementation has been contested, and the underground church faces ongoing uncertainty.

## Bibles and Scriptures

Bibles can only be printed in China by state-approved publishers in limited quantities and distributed through registered churches. Importing Bibles is controlled; large-scale imports are intercepted and confiscated. In 2019, reports emerged that the government had removed Bible sale listings from major Chinese e-commerce platforms.

## The Church That Cannot Be Stopped

Despite the comprehensive nature of CCP control, the Chinese church continues to grow. Even the lower estimates represent extraordinary growth from near-zero after the Cultural Revolution. The house church movement is decentralized in ways that make it structurally difficult to fully suppress. Its leadership is trained, its theology is serious, and its members understand that their faith has a cost.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #15
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 38–97 million (wide range reflects measurement difficulty)
- **Primary Threats:** State registration requirements, surveillance, cross removals, church demolitions, imprisonment of pastors, Sinicization policy, Bible distribution restrictions
- **Legal Status:** Christianity tolerated only within state-approved TSPM/CPCA framework; unregistered practice increasingly criminalized
- **Notable:** Pastor Wang Yi (Early Rain Covenant Church) sentenced to 9 years; hundreds of pastors imprisoned; thousands of churches closed or demolished since 2014

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: China* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/china/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: China (includes Tibet, Xinjiang, and Hong Kong)* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/china-includes-tibet-xinjiang-and-hong-kong/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: China* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: China — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/china/
- Human Rights Watch, *China: Freedom of Religion* — hrw.org/asia/china
- ChinaSource — chinasource.org
- Pew Research Center, *Global Christianity* (2011) — pewresearch.org/religion/2011/12/19/global-christianity-exec/
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Nestorian Stele and early church history
- Spence, Jonathan D., *The Memory Palace of Matteo Ricci* (Viking Penguin, 1984)
- Fulton, Brent, *China’s Urban Christians: A Light That Cannot Be Hidden* (Pickwick Publications, 2015)

COUNTRY: Colombia
TITLE: Colombia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/colombia
META DESCRIPTION: Colombia is a deeply Christian nation where believers in rural conflict zones face a specific and lethal threat: guerrilla and criminal organizations that target pastors, missionaries, and church leaders who challenge their social control.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Colombia, South America, FARC, ELN, Persecution, Pastors Killed, Rural Church, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Colombia is approximately 90% Christian — the vast majority Catholic, with a significant and rapidly growing evangelical Protestant community. In major cities, Christians worship freely and without threat. Colombia is not a persecution country in the sense that the state restricts religious freedom. However, Colombia presents a distinctive and serious threat for Christians in rural conflict zones: the guerrilla and organized crime groups — particularly the ELN (National Liberation Army), FARC dissident factions, and criminal networks — that control significant portions of the Colombian countryside specifically target pastors, missionaries, and church leaders who challenge their authority, refuse to cooperate with their economic activities, or provide moral community leadership that competes with their control.

## Christianity in Colombia

Spain established Catholicism as the foundational religion of Colombia through three centuries of colonial rule. A 1991 constitution formally established religious pluralism and ended the Catholic Church’s privileged constitutional status. Since then, evangelical Protestantism — particularly Pentecostal movements — has grown dramatically.

One of Latin America’s most significant 20th-century Christian martyrs is Salvadoran Archbishop Óscar Romero — canonized by Pope Francis in 2018 — whose witness to justice in the face of state violence shapes how Colombian and regional Christian leaders understand their own calling in conflict zones.

## Armed Groups and Targeted Violence

In communities where gangs control territory, pastors occupy a specifically vulnerable position. They are known community figures who interact with youth the gangs want to recruit, who preach ethics and redemption that challenge the criminal worldview, who provide trusted community leadership that undermines gang authority, and who may provide sanctuary to those fleeing recruitment. For all of these reasons, pastors are targeted. The threat typically takes the form of extortion demands, death threats, and assassination. International Christian Concern and other monitoring organizations document Colombia as having among the highest rates of pastor killings in Latin America.

Colombia sits in the lower tier of the Open Doors World Watch List not for state persecution but for non-state criminal violence against church leaders.

## Current Status

Urban Christians in Colombia’s major cities — Bogotá, Medellín, Cali — practice freely and without threat. The issue is geographically concentrated in rural conflict zones: the Pacific coast region, the Amazon borderlands, Norte de Santander, and other areas with high ELN or FARC dissident presence.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90%
- **Primary Threat:** ELN, FARC dissidents, and criminal organizations targeting rural pastors and church leaders
- **Current Status:** Urban Christians fully free; rural church leaders in conflict zones at serious risk
- **Open Doors WWL:** Ranked (lower-mid tier)
- **Notable:** Among the highest rates of pastor killings in Latin America

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Colombia* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/colombia/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Colombia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/colombia/
- CIA World Factbook: Colombia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/colombia/
- International Christian Concern, *Colombia Country Profile* — persecution.org/country/colombia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Colombia country reports* — hrw.org/americas/colombia
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Colombia* — csw.org.uk

COUNTRY: Comoros
TITLE: Comoros — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/comoros
META DESCRIPTION: The Comoros Islands are a small, deeply Islamic nation where Christianity is virtually nonexistent. Proselytism is illegal, conversion from Islam carries criminal penalties, and the few known Christians face severe social and legal consequences. It is among the most restrictive environments for Christianity in the world.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Comoros, Indian Ocean, Islam, Apostasy, Conversion Illegal, WWL Top 10
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #5 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

The Comoros — a small archipelago nation of approximately 850,000 people in the Indian Ocean between Madagascar and the East African coast — is one of the most Islamically uniform countries on earth. Approximately 98% of the population is Sunni Muslim. Islam is enshrined in the constitution as the state religion, and the legal system integrates Sharia principles alongside civil law. There are effectively no indigenous Comorian Christians — the handful of known believers are isolated individuals who practice in complete secrecy, at serious personal risk. Proselytism is illegal. Conversion from Islam is a criminal offense. Foreigners who attempt Christian ministry are expelled. The Comoros ranked #5 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Historical Background

The Comoros were settled by Bantu-speaking Africans, Arab traders, and later Malagasy and Persian migrants. Islam arrived through Arab merchant networks in the early medieval period and took deep root as the cultural and religious foundation of Comorian identity. French colonization (from the mid-19th century through 1975) introduced a small Catholic presence serving French personnel, but no significant indigenous Christianization occurred. When the Comoros declared independence in 1975, the new state confirmed Islam as the foundation of national life.

The island of Mayotte, though geographically part of the Comorian archipelago and claimed by the Comoros, has been a French territory since 1975 and voted to become a French department in 2011. Its population is predominantly Christian — a striking contrast to the three Comorian islands that illustrates how geopolitical status shaped entirely different religious freedom realities within a few miles of each other.

## Legal and Social Framework for Non-Muslims

The Comorian Penal Code criminalizes the exercise of any act of worship contrary to Islam with penalties including up to five years in prison. Proselytism directed at Muslims is illegal and subject to imprisonment and deportation. Importation of non-Islamic religious literature — including Bibles — is banned. Foreigners who are discovered conducting Christian activity are expelled.

For a Comorian national who converts to Christianity, the consequences are severe: family rejection, social exclusion, loss of employment, potential legal jeopardy, and physical danger. The social enforcement of Islamic identity is total.

## The Church in Comoros

There is no organized Christian church in the Comoros. The only regular Christian worship occurs among the small number of expatriate workers who are permitted to worship privately within their homes or residential compounds. Indigenous Comorian Christians — estimated to number in the dozens, possibly low hundreds — are entirely secret. They have no community structures, no pastoral support from visible churches, and no legal recourse if they face persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #5
- **Indigenous Christian Population:** Effectively zero; possibly tiny number of secret converts
- **Legal Framework:** Apostasy criminalized; proselytism criminal; Christian literature illegal
- **Churches:** Zero public churches
- **Expatriate Christians:** Private worship only in diplomatic or residential compound settings

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Comoros* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/comoros/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Comoros* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/comoros/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Comoros — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/comoros/
- Joshua Project, *Comoros* — joshuaproject.net

COUNTRY: Democratic Republic of Congo
TITLE: Democratic Republic of Congo — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/democratic-republic-of-congo
META DESCRIPTION: The DRC is one of Africa’s most Christian nations, but Christians in the volatile east — particularly in North and South Kivu — face systematic violence from dozens of armed groups, including the ADF, which has targeted churches and pastors with deadly regularity.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: DRC, Congo, Central Africa, ADF, Allied Democratic Forces, Church Attacks, Armed Groups, M23, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

The Democratic Republic of Congo is extraordinary in its Christian identity and its suffering. Approximately 95% of the DRC’s population of nearly 100 million people identifies as Christian — among the highest Christian percentages of any large nation on earth. The Catholic Church, Protestant denominations, and the Kimbanguist Church (an indigenous African Christian movement founded in the DRC) have deep institutional roots and enormous social influence. In most of the vast country, Christians worship freely. But the DRC’s eastern provinces have been in a state of armed conflict for three decades, and Christians in those regions face violence from a complex web of armed groups, most notably the Allied Democratic Forces (ADF), which has conducted one of the continent’s most sustained campaigns of anti-Christian terror.

## Christianity in the DRC

Belgian colonization established Catholicism as the dominant religious and educational framework in the Congo from the late 19th century through independence in 1960. Catholic missions built the country’s first schools and hospitals; the church became foundational to Congolese social infrastructure. The Kimbanguist Church, founded by Simon Kimbangu in 1921 after a revival movement the Belgian colonial authorities tried to suppress, became one of Africa’s most significant indigenous Christian movements and is now a member of the World Council of Churches.

## The Eastern Congo Crisis and the ADF

The eastern DRC has been in near-continuous conflict since the mid-1990s, following the aftermath of the Rwandan genocide, which sent millions of refugees into eastern Congo and triggered a spiral of violence. The conflict has never fully resolved; it involves more than 100 armed groups active in eastern Congo.

Among these groups, the Allied Democratic Forces (ADF) stand out for their specifically anti-Christian violence. The ADF was founded in Uganda in the early 1990s as an Islamist insurgency. Driven out of Uganda by the Ugandan military, it relocated to the jungles of eastern DRC near the Rwenzori Mountains. Beginning approximately 2019, following the ADF’s formal affiliation with the Islamic State, the nature and scale of their anti-Christian violence intensified dramatically: churches were burned, pastors and priests were beheaded, and Christian congregants were massacred in attacks that follow a recognizable pattern. The UN Group of Experts and human rights organizations have documented hundreds of deaths specifically in church-related attacks.

The broader M23 rebel group — backed by Rwanda — and other armed factions also contribute to civilian displacement and violence affecting Christians. The eastern DRC humanitarian crisis is among the world’s largest: an estimated 7 million people are internally displaced.

## The Church’s Response

Despite the violence, the Congolese church has not retreated. The Catholic Bishop of Goma and other church leaders have confronted armed groups publicly, sheltered displaced civilians in church compounds, and provided testimony to international bodies documenting atrocities. Several priests and pastors have been killed specifically because of this advocacy. Church-based humanitarian organizations — Caritas Congo, Protestant medical and educational networks — provide the majority of healthcare and education in many parts of the east where the state has no effective presence.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~95% (~90 million)
- **Primary Threat:** ADF (Allied Democratic Forces) sustained anti-Christian massacres in eastern Congo; general armed conflict
- **Displacement:** 7 million+ internally displaced (largest in Africa)
- **Current Status:** Most of the country safe; eastern provinces in acute crisis
- **Open Doors WWL:** Mid-tier

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Democratic Republic of the Congo* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/democratic-republic-of-the-congo/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Democratic Republic of the Congo* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/democratic-republic-of-the-congo/
- CIA World Factbook: Democratic Republic of the Congo — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/congo-democratic-republic-of-the/
- UN Group of Experts on the DRC, *Final Reports* — un.org/securitycouncil
- UN OCHA, *Democratic Republic of the Congo Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/democratic-republic-congo
- Human Rights Watch, *DRC: ADF and armed group reporting* — hrw.org/africa/democratic-republic-congo
- Aid to the Church in Need, *DRC reporting* — acn-intl.org
- World Council of Churches, *Kimbanguist Church* — oikoumene.org

COUNTRY: Republic of Congo
TITLE: Republic of Congo — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/republic-of-congo
META DESCRIPTION: The Republic of Congo (Congo-Brazzaville) is a majority-Christian nation with full legal religious freedom. Christians worship freely with no systematic persecution or state restriction.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Congo-Brazzaville, Central Africa, Catholic, Evangelical, Religious Freedom, Marxist History
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

The Republic of Congo — also called Congo-Brazzaville — is a small Central African nation of approximately 6 million people. Roughly 85% of the population identifies as Christian, with the Catholic Church and various Protestant and evangelical denominations constituting the religious landscape. Christians worship freely; the state guarantees religious liberty and does not systematically restrict any Christian denomination.

French colonial missions established Catholicism in the region from the late 19th century. The country underwent a period of Marxist governance from 1969 to 1991 during which religious institutions faced some restrictions and nationalization of church schools. However, as in many Marxist African states, the suppression was never total, and the church maintained significant presence throughout. Since democratization in 1991, the Republic of Congo has maintained legal religious freedom. Congo-Brazzaville is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~85%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Republic of the Congo* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/republic-of-the-congo/

COUNTRY: Costa Rica
TITLE: Costa Rica — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/costa-rica
META DESCRIPTION: Costa Rica is a peaceful, majority-Christian democracy with full religious freedom. The Catholic Church is the official state church, but all denominations worship freely and face no persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Costa Rica, Central America, Catholic, Evangelical, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Costa Rica is approximately 70% Christian — roughly 52% Catholic and 18% evangelical Protestant — and has full constitutional religious freedom. Costa Rica is among Latin America’s most stable democracies. Catholicism is formally named the state religion in Costa Rica’s constitution, though in practice all faiths operate freely and there is no meaningful privilege attached to this status for purposes of everyday religious life. Costa Rica is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. Christians face no persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~70%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; Catholicism constitutionally named as state religion but without restrictive application
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Côte d’Ivoire (Ivory Coast)
TITLE: Côte d’Ivoire — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/cote-divoire
META DESCRIPTION: Côte d’Ivoire is roughly equally divided between Christians in the south and Muslims in the north. A devastating civil war (2002–2011) strained religious relations, but the country has largely recovered and Christians practice freely. Jihadist spillover from the Sahel is an emerging concern.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Côte d’Ivoire, Ivory Coast, West Africa, Islam, Christianity, Civil War, Sahel Spillover, Religious Relations
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Côte d’Ivoire is a West African nation of approximately 27 million people, roughly divided between a predominantly Christian south and a predominantly Muslim north — with a significant population following traditional religions throughout. The country endured a devastating political and military crisis between 2002 and 2011 that had religious dimensions, though the conflict was primarily ethnic and political rather than sectarian. The country has substantially recovered, and Christians in the south practice freely. An emerging threat from Sahelian jihadist groups expanding southward from Burkina Faso and Mali is a serious concern for the future.

## Christianity in Côte d’Ivoire

French colonial missionaries established Christianity in the coastal south beginning in the late 19th century. President Félix Houphouët-Boigny — himself a Catholic — built the country’s relative stability partly on a policy of deliberate interreligious harmony. He built the Basilica of Our Lady of Peace in Yamoussoukro in the late 1980s — one of the largest churches in the world — consecrated by Pope John Paul II in 1990.

## The Civil War and Religious Dimensions

The civil war that began in 2002 was triggered by a military coup and revolved primarily around ethnic and political tensions. The rebel movement holding the north was predominantly Muslim; the government holding the south was led by a Christian president. The war was not a religious war, but religious identity became a social marker of political allegiance. Post-war reconciliation explicitly addressed the need to prevent the conflict from hardening along religious lines.

## Jihadist Spillover

Since approximately 2020, jihadist groups from Burkina Faso — primarily JNIM — have conducted attacks in northern Côte d’Ivoire near the Burkinabé border. These attacks have been primarily against security forces rather than Christians specifically, but the pattern of jihadist expansion into the region raises serious concerns about the potential replication of the Burkinabé model of anti-Christian violence.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~45%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no systematic persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Emerging Concern:** Jihadist spillover from Burkina Faso into northern border areas

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Côte d’Ivoire* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/cote-divoire/
- CIA World Factbook: Côte d’Ivoire — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/cote-divoire/
- International Crisis Group, *Côte d’Ivoire reporting* — crisisgroup.org
- ACLED (Armed Conflict Location and Event Data Project), *Côte d’Ivoire* — acleddata.com
- UNESCO, Basilica of Our Lady of Peace documentation — whc.unesco.org

COUNTRY: Croatia
TITLE: Croatia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/croatia
META DESCRIPTION: Croatia is a strongly Catholic nation with full religious freedom. Christians face no systematic persecution. The Catholic faith is deeply tied to Croatian national identity.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Croatia, Eastern Europe, Catholic, Religious Freedom, Balkan Wars, National Identity
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Croatia is approximately 86% Roman Catholic and among the most overtly Catholic nations in Europe. Catholicism is woven into Croatian national identity in a way that blurs the line between religious and ethnic-national belonging. The country has full constitutional religious freedom; all denominations operate without interference. Croatia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

The Croats were Christianized in the 7th–9th centuries, and their association with Rome became a defining marker distinguishing them from Orthodox Serbs — a divide that was a significant fault line in the violent dissolution of Yugoslavia in the 1990s. Since independence and European Union membership (2013), Croatia has been a stable democratic state.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90% (overwhelmingly Catholic)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Croatia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/croatia/
- CIA World Factbook: Croatia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/croatia/

COUNTRY: Cuba
TITLE: Cuba — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/cuba
META DESCRIPTION: Cuba’s communist government has surveilled, harassed, and imprisoned Christian leaders for decades. Since 2021, the crackdown on independent churches — particularly those connected to the protest movement — has intensified significantly. The church is growing but paying a high price.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Cuba, Caribbean, Communism, House Church, State Persecution, Imprisonment, WWL, Protestant
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Cuba is a communist state governed by the Cuban Communist Party, which has maintained power since Fidel Castro’s 1959 revolution. The relationship between the Cuban state and the church has been one of the most consequential in the Western Hemisphere — marked by decades of suppression, uneasy accommodation, and periodic crackdowns. Christianity has survived, and by some measures grown, under communist rule. But the Cuban church — particularly its Protestant and evangelical wing — pays a significant price for that survival. Independent churches that operate outside state-approved structures are surveilled, harassed, and shut down. Pastors who speak publicly about injustice or who connect with the democracy movement face imprisonment. Since the July 2021 protests — the largest public demonstrations in Cuba in decades — the government has intensified pressure on churches with connections to the protest movement, imprisoning at least a dozen Christian leaders.

## Historical Background

Cuba’s colonial Catholic heritage was deep — the island was under Spanish rule from 1492 through 1898. The 1959 revolution brought immediate conflict: the government nationalized Catholic schools, expelled hundreds of foreign priests and sisters, and created a hostile climate for religious practice. An estimated 135 Catholic clergy were expelled in one mass expulsion in 1961. The Cuban constitution’s formal prohibition on religious belief as a condition for Communist Party membership was removed in 1992. Pope John Paul II’s visit in 1998 opened a new phase of engagement between the Catholic Church and the Cuban state.

## The Protestant and Evangelical Church

Evangelical and Pentecostal Christianity in Cuba has grown significantly, particularly since the 1990s — fueled by the economic crisis of the “Special Period” following Soviet collapse. The house church movement expanded. Church buildings that couldn’t be officially registered multiplied informally. The Cuban government manages legal Christianity through registered institutions; unregistered churches operate illegally and at risk.

## The 2021 Crackdown

Cuba’s July 11–12, 2021 protests were the largest demonstrations since the early revolutionary period. The government’s response included mass arrests, long sentences for demonstrators, and systematic targeting of anyone associated with the protests. Pastor Lorenzo Rosales Fajardo of Palmarito de Cauto was sentenced to eight years in prison for participating in protests. Multiple house church leaders and evangelical pastors were arrested on charges of “public disorder,” “contempt of authority,” or “enemy propaganda.” The Cuban Prisoners Defenders organization documented the systematic nature of the crackdown on religious voices connected to the protest movement.

## Church Buildings and Registration

Cuba’s government has a policy of refusing to authorize new church construction in most instances. Churches that operate in unregistered spaces face fines and potential demolition of their facilities. Some congregations have had buildings seized or demolished on pretextual grounds.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~60%
- **Primary Threats:** State surveillance, registration denial for independent churches, imprisonment of pastors connected to democracy movement, church building restrictions
- **Current Status:** State-controlled Christianity tolerated; independent churches under growing pressure; significant crackdown since 2021
- **Open Doors WWL:** Mid-tier ranked
- **Notable:** Pastor Lorenzo Rosales Fajardo sentenced to 8 years following 2021 protests; dozens of Christian leaders imprisoned

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Cuba* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/cuba/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Cuba* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/cuba/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Cuba* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Cuba — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/cuba/
- Human Rights Watch, *Cuba country reports* — hrw.org/americas/cuba
- Cuban Prisoners Defenders — cubanprisioneros.es
- Amnesty International, *Cuba* — amnesty.org/en/countries/americas/cuba/

COUNTRY: Cyprus
TITLE: Cyprus — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/cyprus
META DESCRIPTION: Cyprus is a predominantly Orthodox Christian island divided since 1974 between the Greek Cypriot south and the Turkish Cypriot north. Christians in the north face restricted access to historic churches under Turkish administration.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Cyprus, Eastern Mediterranean, Eastern Orthodox, Divided Island, Turkey, Church Heritage
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Cyprus has been politically divided since 1974, when Turkey invaded the northern third of the island following a coup supported by the Greek military junta. The internationally recognized Republic of Cyprus — controlling the southern two-thirds — is approximately 78% Greek Cypriot, predominantly Eastern Orthodox. Christians in the Republic of Cyprus worship entirely freely. In the north, the historic issue is not active persecution of Christians but restricted access to Orthodox churches, monasteries, and Christian heritage sites — some of which have been converted to mosques or allowed to deteriorate.

## Christianity in Cyprus

Christianity on Cyprus has deep apostolic roots: the Apostle Paul and Barnabas (a Cypriot himself) preached on the island during the first missionary journey recorded in Acts 13. The Church of Cyprus — one of the autocephalous churches of Eastern Orthodoxy — traces its origin to this apostolic foundation and received formal recognition of its independence at the Council of Ephesus in 431 AD, making it one of the oldest independent churches in the world.

## The Divided Island and Heritage Sites

The 1974 division displaced approximately 200,000 Greek Cypriots from the north. Historic churches, monasteries, and cemeteries in the north have faced varying fates under Turkish administration: some have been converted to mosques (including the Cathedral of St. Sophia in Nicosia, now Selimiye Mosque); others have deteriorated through neglect. Icons and artifacts from many northern churches were removed and sold on the international art market — an act of cultural looting documented in extensive legal proceedings.

Christians in the Republic of Cyprus worship entirely freely and face no persecution. The Christian heritage of the north is a genuine cultural and religious rights concern, though it does not constitute active persecution of a living Christian community.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~78% (in the Republic of Cyprus)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom in the Republic of Cyprus; no active persecution
- **Heritage Concern:** Historic Orthodox churches in northern Cyprus converted, deteriorated, or stripped of artifacts
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Cyprus* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/cyprus/
- CIA World Factbook: Cyprus — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/cyprus/
- Council of Europe, *Cultural Heritage in the Northern Part of Cyprus* — coe.int
- UNESCO, documentation of Cypriot monuments — whc.unesco.org
- Church of Cyprus, official history — churchofcyprus.org.cy

COUNTRY: Czechia (Czech Republic)
TITLE: Czechia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/czechia
META DESCRIPTION: Czechia is one of the most secular nations in the world. Christians are a minority and face no persecution, but Christianity’s sharp decline since communism reflects deep historical wounds and a culture of skepticism toward institutional religion.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Czechia, Czech Republic, Central Europe, Secularism, Catholic, Protestant, Communist Legacy
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Czechia — the Czech Republic — is one of the most secular countries on earth. Only about 14% of Czechs identify as Christian by active practice, and roughly 65–70% of the population identifies as non-religious. Christians face no persecution whatsoever; the country has full religious freedom. Czechia’s extraordinary secularism is itself a consequence of Christian history — specifically the trauma of forced re-Catholicization after the defeat of the Protestant Bohemian forces at the Battle of White Mountain in 1620, and the subsequent association of the Catholic Church with Habsburg oppression, which generated an anti-clerical tradition that deepened further under communist rule.

## A Complicated Christian History

Bohemia and Moravia — the historical regions composing Czechia — were at the center of the European Reformation before the Reformation. Jan Hus, rector of Charles University in Prague, preached church reform a full century before Luther, was condemned at the Council of Constance, and was burned at the stake in 1415. The Hussite movement anticipated many Protestant themes and produced a remarkable tradition of vernacular scripture and democratic church governance. The Battle of White Mountain in 1620, in which Catholic Habsburg forces decisively defeated the Protestant Bohemian uprising, ended this period — mass exile of Protestant nobility and clergy followed; forced re-Catholicization was imposed. This trauma was formative: Czechs associated Catholicism with foreign domination, and the seeds of deep anti-clericalism were planted. The communist period (1948–1989) further suppressed religious life.

## Current Status

Czech Christians — Catholic, Protestant, and others — worship freely. The challenge for the Czech church is not persecution but indifference — ministering in a culture where religion is widely regarded as irrelevant or associated with historical oppression.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Active Christian Population:** ~14%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Jan Hus burned at the Council of Constance (1415); White Mountain trauma (1620) deeply shaped Czech secularism

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Czech Republic* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/czech-republic/
- CIA World Factbook: Czechia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/czechia/
- Pew Research Center, *Religious Belief and National Belonging in Central and Eastern Europe* (2017) — pewresearch.org/religion/2017/05/10/religious-belief-and-national-belonging-in-central-and-eastern-europe/
- Fudge, Thomas A., *Jan Hus: Religious Reform and Social Revolution in Bohemia* (I.B. Tauris, 2010)

COUNTRY: Denmark
TITLE: Denmark — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/denmark
META DESCRIPTION: Denmark is a historically Lutheran nation with full religious freedom. The Church of Denmark retains a formal state church status, but all denominations worship freely and Christians face no persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Denmark, Scandinavia, Lutheran, State Church, Secular, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Denmark is a Nordic welfare state with full religious freedom and a historically Lutheran Christian heritage. The Church of Denmark (Folkekirken) is the official state church — supported by a church tax and formally linked to the Danish state — but all denominations and religions operate freely, and membership in the state church is voluntary. Approximately 72% of Danes are members of the Church of Denmark, though active weekly attendance is estimated at under 5% — Denmark is one of the most secular societies in the world by practice.

Christianity arrived in Denmark through Viking-era missionary activity, with King Harald Bluetooth baptized around 960 AD. The Reformation came to Denmark in 1536 when King Christian III established Lutheranism as the state religion. Denmark is not on the Open Doors World Watch List and has no pattern of Christian persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~78% (nominal; active practice much lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; Church of Denmark is state church
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Denmark* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/denmark/
- CIA World Factbook: Denmark — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/denmark/
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/
- Church of Denmark official statistics — km.dk

COUNTRY: Djibouti
TITLE: Djibouti — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/djibouti
META DESCRIPTION: Djibouti is a small, strategically located Horn of Africa nation that is 94% Muslim. Christians — almost entirely foreign workers — can worship privately, but proselytism is illegal, indigenous Christians are nearly nonexistent, and converts from Islam face severe social and family consequences.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Djibouti, Horn of Africa, Islam, Expat Christians, Converts, Religious Restrictions
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Djibouti is a small country of approximately one million people at the strategic junction of the Red Sea and the Gulf of Aden, bordering Somalia, Ethiopia, and Eritrea. It is 94% Muslim. Christians represent approximately 6% of the population — a figure composed almost entirely of expatriate workers, Ethiopian and Eritrean immigrants, and a tiny number of indigenous Djiboutian converts who practice in extreme secrecy. Islam is the state religion under the constitution; proselytism directed at Muslims is illegal; and a Djiboutian who converts from Islam to Christianity faces severe social consequences. Djibouti is not in the top tier of the Open Doors World Watch List but is monitored.

## Christianity in Djibouti

There is no ancient tradition of Christianity in Djibouti. The territory was populated by the Afar and Issa Somali peoples, both Muslim, long before French colonization established French Somaliland in the late 19th century. The small Catholic church built for French administrators remains essentially the character of the Catholic presence today — serving expatriates and immigrants with no significant indigenous Djiboutian congregation.

Ethiopian and Eritrean immigrants — many of whom are Orthodox Christian — form the largest indigenous-African Christian presence in Djibouti and can worship in their own communities. Djibouti also hosts foreign military bases from the United States, France, China, Japan, and others, with Christian service members who worship in base chapels.

## Converts and Restrictions

A Djiboutian who converts from Islam to Christianity faces family rejection, loss of employment, community exclusion, and physical danger from family members. The law against proselytism carries penalties including imprisonment and deportation.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~6% (almost entirely expatriate and immigrant)
- **Primary Restrictions:** Proselytism illegal; converts from Islam face severe social consequences; no indigenous church community
- **Current Status:** Private worship permitted for foreigners; indigenous Christians practice in extreme secrecy
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Djibouti* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/djibouti/
- CIA World Factbook: Djibouti — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/djibouti/
- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/
