COUNTRY: Dominican Republic
TITLE: Dominican Republic — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/dominican-republic
META DESCRIPTION: The Dominican Republic is a deeply Christian Caribbean nation with full religious freedom. Catholics and evangelicals both have strong community roots, and Christians face no systematic persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Dominican Republic, Caribbean, Catholic, Evangelical, Religious Freedom, Latin America
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

The Dominican Republic is a majority-Christian nation — approximately 68% Catholic and 18% Protestant — occupying the eastern two-thirds of the island of Hispaniola. It has full religious freedom under its constitution, and Christians of all denominations practice without legal restriction or systematic social persecution.

Columbus’s first permanent settlement in the Americas was established on Hispaniola, and the Catholic Church arrived with the earliest Spanish colonists. The island was the base of Catholic missionary expansion throughout the Americas in the 16th century; the first cathedral in the Americas was built in Santo Domingo and is now a UNESCO World Heritage Site. That deep colonial Catholic heritage remains visible in Dominican culture, public life, and family practice, even as evangelical Pentecostalism has grown substantially over the past several decades.

The Dominican Republic is not on the Open Doors World Watch List and is not a country of concern for Christian persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~86%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** First cathedral in the Americas built in Santo Domingo (16th century); UNESCO World Heritage Site

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Dominican Republic* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/dominican-republic/
- CIA World Factbook: Dominican Republic — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/dominican-republic/
- UNESCO World Heritage List, *Colonial City of Santo Domingo* — whc.unesco.org/en/list/526/

COUNTRY: Ecuador
TITLE: Ecuador — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/ecuador
META DESCRIPTION: Ecuador is a predominantly Catholic South American nation with full constitutional religious freedom. Evangelical churches are growing rapidly, and Christians of all backgrounds practice without persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Ecuador, South America, Catholic, Evangelical, Religious Freedom, Indigenous Christianity, Jim Elliot
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Ecuador is approximately 80% Christian — with a Catholic majority and a growing evangelical minority — and has full constitutional religious freedom. Christians face no systematic persecution in Ecuador; all denominations operate freely, including within indigenous communities where Christianity has blended in complex ways with Andean spiritual traditions.

Spanish colonization brought Catholicism to Ecuador in the 16th century. The Jesuit, Dominican, and Franciscan orders established missions throughout the country. Protestant and evangelical missions intensified from the mid-20th century; the martyrdom of five American missionaries — Jim Elliot, Nate Saint, Roger Youderian, Ed McCully, and Pete Fleming — who were killed while attempting to contact the Huaorani (Auca) people in 1956 became one of the most well-known events in modern Protestant mission history. The subsequent peaceful contact made by their widows and the eventual widespread conversion of the Huaorani people is among the most remarkable mission stories of the 20th century.

Ecuador is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable:** 1956 martyrdom of Jim Elliot and four fellow missionaries attempting to reach the Huaorani; subsequent peaceful mission became one of the defining stories of 20th-century evangelical missions

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Ecuador* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/ecuador/
- CIA World Factbook: Ecuador — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/ecuador/
- Pew Research Center, *Religion in Latin America* (2014) — pewresearch.org/religion/2014/11/13/religion-in-latin-america/
- Elliot, Elisabeth, *Through Gates of Splendor* (Harper & Row, 1957) — primary account of the Huaorani mission and martyrdoms

COUNTRY: Egypt
TITLE: Egypt — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/egypt
META DESCRIPTION: Egypt is home to the Coptic Church — one of the oldest Christian communities on earth — whose 10–12 million members face ISIS bombings, church construction barriers, social discrimination, and severe consequences for those who leave Islam. The church of the martyrs endures.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Egypt, North Africa, Coptic Church, Coptic Christians, ISIS, Persecution, WWL Top 40, Apostasy, Church Construction
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #35 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Egypt is home to one of the oldest continuous Christian communities in the world. The Coptic Orthodox Church traces its founding to the Apostle and Evangelist Mark, who according to tradition brought the gospel to Alexandria in the first century AD. From Alexandria, Christianity spread throughout Egypt and across the Roman world. This is not a peripheral branch of Christian history — Egypt was at the center of early Christian theology, monasticism, and biblical scholarship. It is the land of Athanasius and Cyril, of Anthony the Great and Pachomius, of the Desert Fathers who shaped Christian spirituality for all subsequent centuries.

Today, Coptic Christians number between 10 and 12 million — roughly 10% of Egypt’s population of 105 million. They are the largest indigenous Christian community in the Middle East and North Africa. They are also a community under sustained pressure: from laws that restrict church construction, from social discrimination in employment and public life, from the devastating consequences of leaving Islam for those who were born Muslim, and from periodic outbreaks of violent extremism that have bombed their churches, burned their villages, and killed their people. Egypt’s ranking at #35 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List reflects this accumulated and ongoing pressure on a community whose roots in their land predate Islam by seven centuries.

## The Apostolic Foundation: Mark in Alexandria

The tradition that Mark the Evangelist founded the church in Alexandria is both ancient and deeply embedded in Coptic identity. Alexandria was one of the greatest cities of the ancient world — a center of learning, commerce, and intellectual diversity. The Catechetical School of Alexandria produced figures of permanent importance: Clement of Alexandria, Origen, and later Athanasius, whose defense of the full divinity of Christ against Arianism at the Council of Nicaea (325 AD) shaped the Trinitarian orthodoxy that defines Christian faith to this day.

Egyptian Christianity also gave the world Christian monasticism. Anthony the Great (c. 251–356 AD), who retreated to the Egyptian desert to live in prayer and ascetic discipline, is considered the father of Christian monasticism. Pachomius developed communal monastic life — the organized monastery — in the 4th century. The traditions Anthony and Pachomius established spread throughout the church and remain foundational to monastic life in both East and West.

## The Council of Chalcedon and the Coptic Separation

The Council of Chalcedon in 451 AD was the pivotal moment of Coptic divergence from what would become the Greek Orthodox and Roman Catholic traditions. The council defined Christ as having two complete natures — divine and human — in one person. The Coptic Church rejected this formulation and held to miaphysitism — the belief that Christ’s divine and human natures are united in one nature without separation, confusion, or mixture. This theological difference kept the Coptic Church in a separate communion for sixteen centuries, though modern ecumenical dialogue has significantly narrowed the perceived gap.

## The Arab Conquest and Dhimmi Status

Arab Muslim forces conquered Egypt between 639 and 642 AD. Under the dhimmi system, Copts were permitted to practice their faith but were subject to a range of disabilities: the jizya (a poll tax levied on non-Muslims), restrictions on building or repairing churches, prohibitions on proselytizing to Muslims, and exclusion from senior government and military positions. Over the following millennium, Egypt’s Christian majority gradually became a minority as conversion to Islam reduced the Coptic population from the majority to somewhere between 10 and 15% by the modern era.

## Modern Egypt: Discrimination and Violence

The 20th century was complex for Egyptian Christians. President Sadat’s assassination in 1981 was carried out by Islamic Jihad members connected to networks that also targeted Copts. President Mubarak’s long rule maintained a degree of state protection for Copts while tolerating social Islamization and failing to prosecute anti-Coptic violence reliably.

The January 2011 uprising that removed Mubarak opened a period of acute danger for Copts. The Muslim Brotherhood, which won the presidency in 2012 under Mohammed Morsi, created an environment in which anti-Coptic violence intensified. The July 2013 military coup that removed Morsi — led by General Abdel Fattah el-Sisi — was broadly welcomed by Coptic leadership, who had experienced the Brotherhood year as deeply threatening.

Under el-Sisi, the government has positioned itself as a protector of Copts. El-Sisi attended Christmas Mass at the Coptic Cathedral — the first Egyptian president to do so — and has made symbolic gestures of solidarity with the Coptic community.

## ISIS and the Martyrs

The most devastating violence against Egypt’s Christians in modern times came from the Islamic State. Between 2015 and 2018, ISIS in Egypt’s Sinai Peninsula conducted a sustained campaign of terror against the Coptic community there, forcing mass displacement of thousands of Coptic families. On Palm Sunday 2017, ISIS suicide bombers struck two Coptic churches simultaneously: St. George’s Church in Tanta and St. Mark’s Cathedral in Alexandria — the seat of the Coptic papacy. The attacks killed 45 people and wounded more than 100. In May 2017, gunmen attacked buses carrying Coptic Christians on a pilgrimage to the Monastery of St. Samuel the Confessor in Minya, killing 29 people — including children.

The 21 Coptic migrant workers beheaded on a Libyan beach by ISIS in February 2015 — dressed in orange jumpsuits, each speaking the name of Jesus in their final moments — were canonized by the Coptic Church as the “Martyrs of Libya.” Their deaths became for many Copts a modern icon of the ancient reality: that following Jesus in Egypt has always had a cost.

## Church Construction and Repair

One of the most persistent structural problems facing Egypt’s Copts is the extreme difficulty of building or repairing church buildings. A 2016 law was intended to rationalize the process, and it has made some improvement, but the construction of new churches remains significantly more difficult in practice than the construction of new mosques, and local resistance — sometimes violent — to new church projects is common in Upper Egypt.

## Converts from Islam

Christians who were born Muslim face a specific and acute danger. Apostasy from Islam is not explicitly criminalized in the Egyptian Penal Code, but a convert faces the loss of civil status as a Muslim under personal status law — which in Egypt means Islamic law governs their marriage, divorce, inheritance, and child custody. A convert cannot officially change the religion listed on their national identity card without an extraordinary legal process that is rarely successful. Converts face severe family and community consequences: rejection, violence, threats, and in some documented cases killing by family members.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #35
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 10–12 million (~10%; predominantly Coptic Orthodox)
- **Primary Threats:** Church construction barriers, discrimination in employment and civil life, violence from Islamist extremists, severe consequences for converts from Islam
- **Notable:** Coptic Church founded by St. Mark; Palm Sunday bombings 2017; 21 Martyrs of Libya canonized 2015; Monastery of St. Samuel attacks 2017

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Egypt* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/egypt/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Egypt* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/egypt/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Egypt* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Egypt — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/egypt/
- Human Rights Watch, *Egypt country reports* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/egypt

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: El Salvador
TITLE: El Salvador — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/el-salvador
META DESCRIPTION: El Salvador is a deeply Christian nation where the primary threat to pastors and church leaders comes not from the state or Islam, but from violent criminal gangs that have systematically targeted churches challenging their territorial control.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: El Salvador, Central America, Catholic, Evangelical, Gang Violence, MS-13, Pastoral Persecution, Archbishop Romero
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

El Salvador is one of the most densely Christian countries in Latin America — approximately 50% Catholic and 36% Protestant, with the majority of the Protestant community belonging to evangelical and Pentecostal denominations. The state fully guarantees religious freedom, and Christians face no government persecution. El Salvador’s challenge for the church is different: for decades, criminal gangs — principally MS-13 (Mara Salvatrucha) and Barrio 18 — controlled significant territory, extorted businesses and individuals, and specifically targeted pastors and church leaders who provided moral community leadership or whose churches offered sanctuary to youth the gangs wanted to recruit. President Nayib Bukele’s aggressive anti-gang crackdown, beginning in 2022, has dramatically reduced gang violence — though questions about its civil liberties impact remain.

## Christianity in El Salvador

Spanish colonization established Catholicism as the dominant faith from the 16th century. El Salvador’s 1979–1992 civil war — between a U.S.-backed military government and the FMLN — produced one of the most significant Christian martyrs of the 20th century: Archbishop Óscar Romero of San Salvador. Romero, initially regarded as a conservative appointment, was transformed by direct witness of military atrocities against civilians. He became the most prominent voice in El Salvador calling for peace and justice. On March 24, 1980, he was shot dead while celebrating Mass — the assassination carried out by death squads linked to the military establishment. He was beatified by Pope Francis in 2015 and canonized in 2018.

## Gang Violence and the Church

The post-war period produced the gang crisis through a convergence of poverty, displacement, and the deportation of gang members from the United States who brought MS-13 and Barrio 18 structures back to Central America. Pastors whose churches operated in gang-controlled neighborhoods faced extortion. Those who preached against the gang lifestyle, offered rehabilitation programs, or refused gang demands for cooperation were targeted with threats and assassination.

## Bukele’s Crackdown and the Current Moment

President Bukele’s declaration of a state of emergency in March 2022 and the mass incarceration of gang members — over 75,000 arrested in less than two years — produced a dramatic reduction in violence. El Salvador’s murder rate fell to among the lowest in Latin America. The physical security environment for pastors in previously gang-controlled areas improved significantly. The crackdown’s civil liberties implications — mass arrests, credible reports of innocent people detained, suspension of due process rights — are significant and widely documented.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~86%
- **Primary Historical Threat:** Gang violence targeting pastors and churches in territorial control zones
- **Current Status:** Security dramatically improved under Bukele crackdown; civil liberties concerns remain
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored but not top tier
- **Notable:** Archbishop Óscar Romero, martyred 1980; canonized 2018 — one of the 20th century’s most significant Christian martyrs

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: El Salvador* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/el-salvador/
- CIA World Factbook: El Salvador — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/el-salvador/
- Human Rights Watch, *El Salvador country reports* — hrw.org/americas/el-salvador
- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/
- Vatican News, *Canonization of Oscar Romero* (October 14, 2018) — vaticannews.va

COUNTRY: Equatorial Guinea
TITLE: Equatorial Guinea — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/equatorial-guinea
META DESCRIPTION: Equatorial Guinea is a majority-Christian nation under a long-ruling dictatorship. Christians worship freely in formal terms, but the government’s authoritarian character limits civil society — including religious institutions — that challenge its power.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Equatorial Guinea, Central Africa, Catholic, Protestant, Dictatorship, Religious Freedom, Authoritarian
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Equatorial Guinea is a small oil-rich Central African nation of approximately 1.5 million people that is approximately 93% Christian, with the Catholic Church dominant and Protestant communities also present. The country is formally free for Christian practice; no denomination is banned, and no systematic persecution of Christians exists. However, Equatorial Guinea has been ruled by President Teodoro Obiang Nguema Mbasogo since 1979 — one of the world’s longest-serving heads of state — and the authoritarian character of his government limits all civil society, including religious institutions that might challenge his authority. Equatorial Guinea is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~93%
- **Current Status:** Formal religious freedom; authoritarian political context limits civil society broadly
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Eritrea
TITLE: Eritrea — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/eritrea
META DESCRIPTION: Eritrea is one of the most repressive nations on earth for Christians. Thousands of believers — including members of registered denominations — have been imprisoned in horrific conditions, some for over twenty years, for nothing more than their faith. The underground church endures in darkness.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Eritrea, Horn of Africa, WWL Top 10, Imprisonment, Underground Church, Isaias Afwerki, Evangelical, Orthodox, Persecution
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #6 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Eritrea is a small, isolated East African nation of approximately 3.5 million people that ranks among the world’s most severe persecutors of Christians. The government of President Isaias Afwerki — who has ruled since independence in 1993 and has never held an election — controls every dimension of public life with totalitarian intensity. Since 2002, all religious groups except four recognized denominations have been banned outright, and even members of those recognized groups have been imprisoned for activities the government deems threatening. Christians outside the approved list — evangelical, Pentecostal, Baptist, charismatic, Adventist, and others — face arrest, imprisonment without charge or trial, and detention in conditions that constitute torture: metal shipping containers baking in the desert heat, underground cells, and military camps where prisoners are subjected to pressure to recant their faith. Thousands have been imprisoned. Some have been held for over two decades. Some have died in custody. Eritrea ranked #6 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Historical Foundation: Christianity Before Eritrea Was Eritrea

The Kingdom of Aksum adopted Christianity as its official religion approximately 330 AD, when King Ezana was converted through the ministry of Frumentius, a Syrian scholar who had come to Aksum as a slave and eventually became its first bishop — consecrated by Athanasius of Alexandria himself. This ancient connection between Alexandria and the Aksumite church is the origin of the historical relationship between the Coptic and Ethiopian/Eritrean Orthodox traditions.

The Nine Saints, a group of Syrian Christian monks who arrived in the 5th and 6th centuries, established monasteries throughout the highlands and translated the Scriptures into Ge’ez, the classical language of the church in this region. The Debre Bizen monastery in Eritrea, founded in the 14th century, is among the oldest continuously inhabited Christian monasteries in Africa.

## The Religious Landscape

Eritrea’s population is roughly equally divided between Christians and Muslims — approximately 50% of each. The Christians are predominantly Eritrean Orthodox (the ancient church of the highland Tigrinya people), with smaller communities of Catholics, Lutherans (introduced by Swedish missionaries in the 19th century), and evangelical and Pentecostal Christians.

## The 2002 Crackdown

In May 2002, the government announced that only four religious groups were officially recognized: the Eritrean Orthodox Church, the Roman Catholic Church, the Evangelical Church of Eritrea (Lutheran), and Islam. All other religious groups were required to register through a process that has effectively never been completed. Unregistered religious activity became illegal overnight.

Within weeks of the May 2002 announcement, arrests began. Police and military intelligence agents raided house church meetings and evangelical gatherings, arresting participants en masse. Those arrested were taken to military camps, prisons, and detention facilities — most without formal charges, legal representation, or any semblance of legal process.

## Conditions of Imprisonment

The conditions in which Eritrean Christian prisoners have been held are among the most severe documented anywhere in the world. The most notorious are the metal shipping containers — standard cargo containers — used as cells in facilities including Wi’a and Dahlak Kebir Island. Temperatures inside these containers in the Eritrean lowland heat reach life-threatening levels. Prisoners are crowded together with minimal food, water, and sanitation. Medical care is absent or grossly inadequate.

Many prisoners are given explicit offers: sign a document renouncing their faith and agree not to practice Christianity, and be released. Those who refuse continue to be held. Some have refused for years — for decades.

## Even the Recognized Churches Are Not Free

The most dramatic demonstration of this was the government’s treatment of the Eritrean Orthodox Church. In January 2006, the government forcibly removed Patriarch Antonios — the head of the Eritrean Orthodox Church — from office, after he refused to excommunicate members of a renewal movement within the church. Patriarch Antonios was placed under house arrest, denied contact with church members and visitors, and stripped of his patriarchal authority. He remained under house arrest for over fifteen years into his 90s. He died in March 2024, never having been formally restored to his position — one of the most egregious documented cases of government interference with a recognized church leader in the world.

## The Diaspora as the Voice of the Silenced

Because the Eritrean government controls all domestic media and severely restricts outside communication, the Eritrean Christian diaspora — concentrated in Europe, the United States, Canada, and Australia — serves as the primary voice for the imprisoned church. Organizations including Release Eritrea, Open Doors, Jubilee Campaign, and Christian Solidarity Worldwide maintain databases of imprisoned believers and advocate before governments and international bodies. The United Nations Special Rapporteur on the situation of human rights in Eritrea has repeatedly documented the religious persecution in reports to the Human Rights Council.

## The Church Underground

Despite two decades of severe persecution, the underground evangelical church in Eritrea has not collapsed. Reports from those who have left the country describe small groups meeting in extreme secrecy, rotating locations, keeping no written records, and trusting only the most vetted members. Young Eritrean Christians fleeing military conscription and persecution have been among the largest groups of Eritrean refugees in Sudan, Ethiopia, Libya, and Europe.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #6
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~50% of 3.5 million (~1.75 million; predominantly Orthodox, with Catholic, Lutheran, and evangelical communities)
- **Primary Threats:** Imprisonment without trial for unregistered denomination members; metal container detention; government removal of recognized church leadership
- **Legal Status:** Only 4 groups recognized; all evangelical, Pentecostal, and independent Christian groups illegal since 2002
- **Prisoners:** Thousands documented; many held for 10–20+ years; death in custody documented
- **Notable:** Patriarch Antonios held under house arrest for 15+ years; died 2024 without formal restoration

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Eritrea* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/eritrea/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Eritrea* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/eritrea/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Eritrea* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Eritrea — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/eritrea/
- Human Rights Watch, *Eritrea country reports* — hrw.org/africa/eritrea
- Amnesty International, *Eritrea* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/eritrea/
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Eritrea* — csw.org.uk
- Release Eritrea — releaseeritrea.org
- UN Human Rights Council, *Reports of the Special Rapporteur on the Situation of Human Rights in Eritrea* — ohchr.org
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Aksumite Christian history

COUNTRY: Estonia
TITLE: Estonia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/estonia
META DESCRIPTION: Estonia is one of the most secular nations in the world. Christians practice freely but are a minority. The Lutheran heritage is historically significant but today functions more as cultural identity than active faith.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Estonia, Baltic, Lutheran, Secular, Religious Freedom, Post-Soviet
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Estonia is among the least religious countries in the world by survey — only around 20–25% of Estonians identify with any religious tradition, and active church attendance is far lower. The historical faith is Lutheranism, established during the German Reformation of the 16th century. The Orthodox Church has a significant presence among the ethnic Russian minority. Christians of all traditions worship freely; Estonia has full constitutional religious freedom. The Soviet occupation (1940–1991) suppressed religious life severely — closing churches, imprisoning clergy, and promoting atheism aggressively. Estonia’s post-Soviet secularism reflects both Soviet suppression and a broader Nordic-influenced cultural skepticism toward institutional religion. Estonia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~20–25% (nominal; active practice very low)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

COUNTRY: Eswatini
TITLE: Eswatini — Christianity & Persecution Profile

CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Eswatini, Swaziland, Southern Africa, Christian Majority, Religious Freedom, Monarchy
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Eswatini — formerly Swaziland, renamed in 2018 by King Mswati III — is a small, landlocked kingdom in Southern Africa with a population of approximately 1.2 million. Roughly 90% of the population identifies as Christian, with a blend of Protestant denominations, Zionist and Apostolic African Independent Churches, and Catholic communities. The king is himself Christian and supports Christianity as part of the national cultural fabric. Christians practice freely and without persecution. The country’s serious challenges — among the world’s highest HIV/AIDS prevalence rates, significant poverty, and political repression — affect the general population but are not specifically directed at Christians as a religious group. Eswatini is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Primary National Challenges:** HIV/AIDS prevalence, poverty, absolute monarchical governance — not religious persecution

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Ethiopia
TITLE: Ethiopia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/ethiopia
META DESCRIPTION: Ethiopia is home to one of the world’s oldest Christian churches. But since 2020, the Tigray war has devastated ancient Orthodox communities, Islamist attacks in Oromia have burned churches and killed Christians, and the church itself has experienced a painful institutional schism. A Christian nation in deep crisis.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Ethiopia, Horn of Africa, Ethiopian Orthodox, Tigray War, Islamist Violence, Oromia, Church Schism, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Ethiopia holds a singular place in global Christian history. The Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church is one of the oldest Christian institutions in the world — Aksum adopted Christianity as the state religion approximately 330 AD. The church has maintained an unbroken presence for seventeen centuries. Its liturgy, in the ancient Ge’ez language, preserves a world of biblical and theological richness largely unknown to Western Christians. Its monasteries, including the ancient island monasteries of Lake Tana, are among the most extraordinary in the world.

Today, Ethiopia’s Christian story is marked by both this ancient depth and serious, contemporary suffering. The Tigray war (2020–2022) produced one of the world’s worst humanitarian crises and devastated the ancient Orthodox communities of Tigray, including deliberate attacks on churches, monasteries, and priests. Islamist violence in the Oromia and other regions has burned churches and killed Christians with increasing regularity. And the Ethiopian Orthodox Church itself has undergone a painful institutional schism whose resolution remains incomplete.

## The Ancient Foundation

As in Eritrea’s profile, the Aksumite kingdom’s adoption of Christianity traces to the ministry of Frumentius (Abba Selama) and his consecration by Athanasius of Alexandria. The Ethiopian church developed its own monastic tradition, its own scriptural canon — extraordinarily rich, with over 180 feast days annually — and its own theological scholarship. The rock-hewn churches in Lalibela, built in the late 12th and early 13th centuries, are among the architectural wonders of the world — eleven churches carved entirely from living rock, still in active liturgical use today. The Ethiopian church’s claim to house the Ark of the Covenant in Aksum is a living article of faith for Ethiopian Christians.

## The Modern Period: Protestant Growth and Orthodox Dominance

Ethiopia today is approximately 44% Ethiopian Orthodox and 21% Protestant — with the Protestant community having grown dramatically in the 20th century. The Mekane Yesus Church, an Evangelical Lutheran denomination, is among the largest Lutheran bodies in the world. There is also a significant Muslim population — approximately 34% — concentrated in the east and south.

## The Tigray War (2020–2022) and Its Aftermath

In November 2020, Prime Minister Abiy Ahmed launched a military operation against the Tigray People’s Liberation Front (TPLF). What followed was one of the world’s worst humanitarian crises: an estimated 300,000–500,000 deaths (including violence, famine, and disease), mass displacement of millions, and systematic atrocities documented by the United Nations and human rights organizations.

For the Ethiopian Orthodox Church — and for Christianity specifically — the Tigray war was catastrophic. Tigray is the historic heartland of Ethiopian Orthodoxy, the region of Aksum, Lalibela, and Debre Damo — among the most sacred sites in the entire Christian world. Churches and monasteries were specifically targeted by federal forces and their Eritrean allies: the ancient Maryam Dengelat Church, where civilians sheltering inside were massacred, is among the most documented. Priests were killed; monks were expelled; church property was looted; sacred manuscripts were destroyed.

A ceasefire agreement was reached in November 2022. Reconstruction — physical and communal — is ongoing.

## Islamist Violence in Oromia and Beyond

Separately from the Tigray war, Ethiopia has experienced a significant pattern of Islamist violence against Christians, particularly in the Oromia region. Churches have been burned — hundreds in some reports — in mass burnings that reached particular intensity in 2019 and again in 2022–2023, displacing tens of thousands of Christians and destroying communities that had existed for generations.

## The Orthodox Schism

In 2023, a significant portion of the Ethiopian Orthodox Church’s Oromo leadership declared a separate synod — the Holy Synod of the Oromia Ethiopian Orthodox Tewahedo Church — citing decades of marginalization of Oromo Christians within the church’s historically Amhara-dominated hierarchy. The official Holy Synod excommunicated the breakaway leadership. The schism remains unresolved.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~65% (Orthodox ~44%, Protestant ~21%)
- **Primary Threats:** Tigray war devastation of ancient Orthodox communities; Islamist violence against churches in Oromia; institutional Orthodox schism
- **Legal Status:** Constitutional religious freedom; no state anti-Christian policy
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not in formal top tier but significant suffering documented
- **Ancient Heritage:** Aksumite Christianity (330 AD); rock-hewn churches of Lalibela; claimed location of the Ark of the Covenant

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Ethiopia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/ethiopia/
- CIA World Factbook: Ethiopia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/ethiopia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Ethiopia: Tigray War reporting* — hrw.org/africa/ethiopia
- Amnesty International, *Ethiopia* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/ethiopia/
- UN Human Rights Office, *Ethiopia reporting* — ohchr.org
- Open Doors USA, *Ethiopia Country Profile* — opendoorsusa.org
- International Christian Concern, *Ethiopia* — persecution.org/country/ethiopia/
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Aksumite church history

COUNTRY: Fiji
TITLE: Fiji — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/fiji
META DESCRIPTION: Fiji is a majority-Christian Pacific island nation where believers practice freely. Ethnic and religious tensions between indigenous Fijians (predominantly Christian) and Indo-Fijians (predominantly Hindu and Muslim) have shaped the country’s politics but have not produced systematic Christian persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Fiji, Pacific Islands, Methodist, Catholic, Religious Freedom, Ethnic Tensions, Hindu
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Fiji is a Pacific archipelago nation of approximately 930,000 people, split demographically between indigenous Fijians (approximately 57%) — who are predominantly Christian, mostly Methodist — and Indo-Fijians (approximately 38%) — descendants of laborers brought from India by the British, who are predominantly Hindu and Muslim. Christianity arrived in Fiji through Methodist missionaries in the 1830s and spread rapidly; by 1854, the paramount chief Ratu Seru Epenisa Cakobau converted to Christianity in a moment that effectively Christianized the islands at the highest social level.

Fiji has experienced political instability — four coups since 1987 — that has often had ethnic and religious dimensions. These tensions reflect ethnic and political conflict more than religious persecution, and they have moderated significantly since the 2013 constitution under the Bainimarama government, which imposed formal secularism and equal rights regardless of ethnicity or religion. Christians in Fiji are not persecuted. Fiji is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~65%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; ethnic tensions present but not systematic persecution of Christians
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Fiji* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/fiji/
- CIA World Factbook: Fiji — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/fiji/

COUNTRY: Finland
TITLE: Finland — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/finland
META DESCRIPTION: Finland is a historically Lutheran nation with full religious freedom. The Evangelical Lutheran Church retains a special constitutional status, but Christians of all traditions worship without restriction.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Finland, Scandinavia, Lutheran, Religious Freedom, State Church, Nordic
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Finland is a Nordic nation where the Evangelical Lutheran Church of Finland holds a special status — alongside the Finnish Orthodox Church — as a national church with constitutional recognition. Both national churches receive state support and have formal public roles. Despite this formal establishment, Finland has full religious freedom; all denominations operate freely. Approximately 70% of Finns are nominally Lutheran members, though active weekly attendance is very low.

Finland is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. A 2021 criminal prosecution of Member of Parliament Päivi Räsänen and Bishop Juhana Pohjola — charged with “hate speech” for a 2004 church pamphlet on sexual ethics — drew significant international attention from Christian freedom advocates. Both were acquitted by Finnish courts at multiple levels, but the case highlighted ongoing European tensions around the limits of religious speech in secular legal frameworks.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~75%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; Päivi Räsänen hate speech case acquitted
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: France
TITLE: France — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/france
META DESCRIPTION: France is the birthplace of secular republicanism and a majority-Catholic cultural heritage. Christians worship freely, but France has experienced a striking rise in anti-Christian acts — church vandalism and arson — while legal restrictions on religious expression in public spaces affect all faiths including Christianity.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: France, Western Europe, Catholic, Protestant, Secularism, Church Vandalism, Laïcité, Anti-Christian Acts
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

France is a nation of paradoxes in religious life. It is culturally the most Catholic country in Western Europe — the land of Clovis and Charlemagne, of Chartres and Notre-Dame, of Jeanne d’Arc and the Curé d’Ars — whose official ideology of laïcité (strict secularism) is among the most rigorous in the world. Christians worship freely; France has no anti-Christian legislation and no state persecution of Christianity. But France is also a country where anti-Christian acts — particularly the vandalism and arson of church buildings — have occurred at striking frequency, and where the legal framework of strict secularism creates real tensions around religious expression in public life.

## Christianity’s Deep French Roots

Christianity arrived in Gaul during the Roman period. The martyrs of Lyon (177 AD), including Bishop Pothinus and the slave woman Blandina, are among the early church’s most celebrated martyrs. Clovis I, king of the Franks, converted to Catholic Christianity around 498 AD — a politically decisive moment that shaped the religious character of what would become France. The French Revolution’s violent anti-clericalism — the dechristianization campaigns of the 1790s, the execution and exile of priests, the transformation of Notre-Dame into a “Temple of Reason” — created a tradition of radical secularism that has never fully dissolved. The 1905 Law on the Separation of the Churches and the State formalized laïcité.

## Anti-Christian Acts and Church Attacks

A consistent and troubling pattern has emerged in France over the past decade: a high rate of vandalism, desecration, and arson targeting Catholic churches. The French Interior Ministry has documented hundreds of anti-Christian acts annually — ranging from graffiti and desecration of statues to arson. The April 2019 fire at Notre-Dame de Paris, while determined to be accidental, focused international attention on the vulnerability of France’s religious heritage. The rate of documented anti-Christian acts in France is significantly higher than the rate of anti-Islamic or anti-Semitic acts, though those also occur.

## Laïcité and Religious Expression

France’s strict application of laïcité — which prohibits conspicuous religious symbols in state schools and public institutions for employees — affects Christians alongside other faiths. France is not a persecution country by any measure that applies globally. The concerns are about cultural trends and legal frameworks — important, but categorically different from what Christians in Eritrea, North Korea, or Afghanistan face.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~51% (nominal Catholic majority; active practice much lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; hundreds of anti-Christian acts annually; strict laïcité limits public religious expression
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: France* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/france/
- CIA World Factbook: France — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/france/
- French Interior Ministry, annual reports on anti-religious acts — interieur.gouv.fr
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/

COUNTRY: Gabon
TITLE: Gabon — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/gabon
META DESCRIPTION: Gabon is a majority-Christian Central African nation with legal religious freedom. Christians worship freely under a long-ruling political dynasty, now ended by a 2023 military coup. No systematic persecution exists.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Gabon, Central Africa, Catholic, Protestant, Religious Freedom, Coup, Political Transition
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Gabon is a small, oil-rich Central African nation of approximately 2.3 million people that is approximately 80% Christian — predominantly Catholic, with Protestant and evangelical communities also present. Christians practice freely; religious freedom is constitutionally guaranteed. The country was governed for 56 years by the Bongo family dynasty until a military coup in August 2023 ended Bongo rule following disputed elections. The transitional military government has not indicated any change in policy toward religious communities. Gabon is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; political transition ongoing after 2023 coup
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Gabon* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/gabon/
- CIA World Factbook: Gabon — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/gabon/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Gambia
TITLE: Gambia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/gambia
META DESCRIPTION: Gambia is a small, majority-Muslim West African nation where Christians practice with relative freedom compared to more restrictive Islamic states, but where an Islamic identity law proposed under former dictator Jammeh and ongoing social pressure on converts remain concerns.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Gambia, West Africa, Islam, Christian Minority, Converts, Religious Restrictions, Post-Jammeh
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Gambia is a tiny country — the smallest on mainland Africa — of approximately 2.5 million people. Approximately 96% of Gambians are Muslim; Christians represent around 4%, concentrated primarily in the western coastal region around Banjul and among certain ethnic groups including the Jola and Manjago. Christians can worship in their churches, maintain schools, and practice openly. However, the legacy of Yahya Jammeh’s 22-year dictatorship (1994–2017) — which declared Gambia an “Islamic Republic” in 2015 and created an environment of acute pressure on religious minorities — continues to shape the country’s religious climate. Converts from Islam face severe social consequences.

## Christianity in Gambia

Christianity came to Gambia through British colonial presence — the Gambia was the earliest British territorial claim in Africa — and through missionaries, particularly Methodist and Catholic, who established churches, schools, and medical work from the 19th century. Interreligious relations in Gambia have historically been relatively cordial by regional standards.

## The Jammeh Era and Its Legacy

Yahya Jammeh declared Gambia an “Islamic Republic” in 2015, alarming the Christian minority and creating immediate concern about whether the framework of legal religious pluralism would be maintained. Jammeh made additional statements threatening Christians and expressing support for a more overtly Islamist social order. His electoral defeat in December 2016 and subsequent exile to Equatorial Guinea ended the immediate crisis. President Adama Barrow reversed the “Islamic Republic” declaration, restoring Gambia’s formally secular constitutional framework.

## Converts and Social Pressure

For Gambians who leave Islam for Christianity, the consequences are significant: social ostracism, family rejection, and community exclusion. The government does not actively prosecute converts, and apostasy is not criminalized in the penal code. But the informal social framework is powerful, and pastors who disciple converts from Muslim backgrounds sometimes face threats.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~4%
- **Primary Concerns:** Social pressure on converts from Islam; Jammeh-era Islamist legacy; Islamic Republic declaration (2015, reversed 2017)
- **Current Status:** Formal religious freedom restored; social environment challenging for converts
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Gambia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/gambia/
- CIA World Factbook: Gambia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/gambia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Gambia* — hrw.org/africa/gambia
- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Georgia
TITLE: Georgia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/georgia
META DESCRIPTION: Georgia is a fiercely Orthodox Christian nation where the church has been central to national identity and survival for seventeen centuries. Minority denominations — particularly Jehovah’s Witnesses and evangelical groups — faced violent mob persecution in the early 2000s; the situation has improved but social pressure on non-Orthodox Christians remains.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Georgia, Caucasus, Georgian Orthodox, Christian Nation, Minority Denominations, Historical Persecution, Religious Identity
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Georgia has been a Christian nation since 327 AD — according to tradition, when St. Nino of Cappadocia evangelized the kingdom of Iberia and converted King Mirian III. That seventeen-century-old Christian identity has been the anchor of Georgian national survival through Persian invasions, Mongol devastation, Ottoman domination, and Soviet suppression. To be Georgian is, in the deepest cultural sense, to be Orthodox — and the Georgian Orthodox Patriarchate commands a degree of public trust and national reverence that is extraordinary even by Eastern Orthodox standards. Approximately 84% of Georgians identify as Orthodox.

The complexity in Georgia’s religious freedom picture is not state persecution of Christians — it is the treatment of minority Christian denominations by a society whose dominant religious institution has, at times, been complicit in or tolerant of violence against those who deviate from Orthodox Christianity.

## Seventeen Centuries of Christian Identity

Georgia’s conversion is closely tied to the story of St. Nino, who arrived in what is now eastern Georgia around 320 AD. The Georgian church received its own catholicos in the 5th century and has maintained autocephalous (self-governing) status. The martyrdom of the 6,000 at the Cathedral of Svetitskhoveli — killed by Shah Abbas of Persia in 1616 rather than convert to Islam — is among the defining moments of Georgian Christian martyrology. The Soviet period suppressed the Georgian church severely, but it survived. After independence in 1991, the church recovered rapidly.

## Mob Violence Against Minority Denominations

The early 2000s produced a serious and well-documented pattern of organized religious violence against minority Christian groups — specifically Jehovah’s Witnesses, Baptists, and evangelical Christians — carried out by mobs led by defrocked Orthodox priest Basil Mkalavishvili. These mobs, sometimes numbering in the hundreds, attacked prayer meetings, burned Bibles and religious literature, physically assaulted worshipers, and destroyed property. Police initially failed to intervene. Mkalavishvili was eventually arrested in 2004 and imprisoned; the organized mob violence ended.

As a reference point for GWM users: Jehovah’s Witnesses were among the primary victims of this mob violence in Georgia — which functions as a “canary” revealing the degree to which non-Orthodox Christian expression could be targeted with social tolerance and initial state inaction. Baptists and evangelicals were also attacked, illustrating that the violence was directed at anyone perceived as competing with or departing from Orthodox Christianity.

## Current Status

The situation has improved substantially since the Mkalavishvili period. The Georgian Patriarchate has more clearly condemned religiously motivated violence, and the physical attacks on minority denominations have ended as an organized phenomenon. However, social pressure on converts from Orthodoxy and on minority Christian communities persists. Georgia is not in the Open Doors top tier but is monitored.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~89% (predominantly Georgian Orthodox)
- **Primary Historical Concern:** Organized mob violence against minority Christian groups (2000–2004); Mkalavishvili convicted 2004
- **Current Status:** Physical attacks ended; social pressure on non-Orthodox Christians persists; formal religious freedom in law
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not in top tier; historically monitored

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Georgia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/georgia/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- Forum 18 News Service, *Georgia* — forum18.org/archive.php?country=25
- CIA World Factbook: Georgia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/georgia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Georgia: Religious Violence reporting* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/georgia
- Amnesty International, *Georgia* — amnesty.org/en/countries/europe-and-central-asia/georgia/

COUNTRY: Germany
TITLE: Germany — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/germany
META DESCRIPTION: Germany is a majority-Christian nation with full religious freedom and a complex Christian history — including the Reformation, two world wars, and the Holocaust, in which the churches’ response remains a source of deep theological reckoning.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Germany, Western Europe, Lutheran, Catholic, Religious Freedom, Reformation, Holocaust, Church Tax
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Germany is a majority-Christian nation — approximately 53% of the population identifies as Christian, split roughly equally between Catholic and Protestant — with full constitutional religious freedom. Germany is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

Germany’s significance in Christian history is disproportionate to its size. Martin Luther’s nailing of the 95 Theses to the Wittenberg church door in 1517 launched the Protestant Reformation, permanently dividing Western Christianity. The Peace of Westphalia (1648) — which ended the devastating Thirty Years’ War — shaped the development of European religious pluralism.

The 20th century brought catastrophic chapters. German churches’ response to Nazism was deeply divided: the German Christians movement accommodated National Socialism; the Confessing Church — led by figures including Dietrich Bonhoeffer, Martin Niemöller, and Karl Barth — resisted. Bonhoeffer was executed by the Nazis in April 1945, days before the end of the war; his theology and martyrdom have made him one of the most influential Christian thinkers of the modern era. The Holocaust — the systematic murder of six million Jews — occurred in a historically Christian country and raises questions about Christian anti-Semitism and institutional complicity that the German churches have spent decades reckoning with.

Germany today has a church tax (Kirchensteuer) system through which registered church members contribute to their denomination. Secularization is advancing, particularly in the former East Germany.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~53%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Significance:** Birthplace of the Protestant Reformation; Dietrich Bonhoeffer martyred 1945; Holocaust reckoning shapes ongoing theological reflection

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Germany* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/germany/
- CIA World Factbook: Germany — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/germany/
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/
- Bethge, Eberhard, *Dietrich Bonhoeffer: A Biography* (Fortress Press, 2000)

COUNTRY: Ghana
TITLE: Ghana — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/ghana
META DESCRIPTION: Ghana is one of West Africa’s most stable democracies and a majority-Christian nation where believers practice freely. Muslims and Christians coexist with generally positive relations, though Islamic pressure on converts in the northern regions is a quiet concern.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Ghana, West Africa, Christian Majority, Catholic, Pentecostal, Religious Freedom, Religious Harmony
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Ghana is a West African nation of approximately 33 million people that is approximately 71% Christian — with an exceptionally diverse range of denominations including Catholic, Methodist, Presbyterian, Baptist, Pentecostal, charismatic, and a thriving independent church movement. Approximately 20% of Ghanaians are Muslim, concentrated primarily in the northern regions. The country is a multi-party democracy and has been praised as one of West Africa’s most stable governance models. Christians practice freely; interreligious relations are generally described as positive and cooperative.

Christianity arrived in Ghana through Portuguese Catholic missionaries at Elmina in the 15th century. British colonial missions established significant Protestant work in the 19th century. Since independence in 1957, Christianity has grown dramatically, and Ghana has become a significant exporter of Christian mission — Ghanaian churches have established congregations across Africa and in the diaspora.

The primary mild concern is social pressure on converts from Islam in the northern regions. Ghana is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~71%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable:** One of West Africa’s most stable democracies; active Christian mission-sending nation

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Ghana* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/ghana/
- CIA World Factbook: Ghana — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/ghana/
- Pew Research Center, *The Global Religious Landscape* (2012) — pewresearch.org/religion/2012/12/18/global-religious-landscape-exec/

COUNTRY: Greece
TITLE: Greece — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/greece
META DESCRIPTION: Greece is a deeply Orthodox Christian nation with a constitutionally privileged national church and full religious freedom for all. Christians face no persecution, though proselytism laws have historically created friction for minority denominations.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Greece, Southern Europe, Greek Orthodox, Religious Freedom, Proselytism, Minority Denominations
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Greece is approximately 90% Eastern Orthodox Christian, and the Greek Orthodox Church holds a privileged constitutional status as the “prevailing religion.” Christianity arrived in Greece through the Apostle Paul himself — his famous visit to Athens (Acts 17), his letter to the Corinthians and Philippians, and his founding of churches in multiple Greek cities make the Pauline epistles partly a correspondence about the original Greek Christian communities.

Greece has full religious freedom in law. Catholic, Protestant, and evangelical denominations operate freely. However, Greece’s proselytism law — which prohibits “aggressive” proselytism — has historically been used against evangelical and Jehovah’s Witness missionaries, and several cases reached the European Court of Human Rights (notably Kokkinakis v. Greece, 1993), where Greece lost. Enforcement of this law has eased considerably in recent decades.

Minority Christian denominations sometimes encounter bureaucratic obstacles in registering as religious organizations and face social friction in a culture where Orthodox identity is deeply embedded in national self-understanding. But systematic persecution does not exist. Greece is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90% (predominantly Orthodox)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no systematic persecution; historical proselytism law friction eased
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Paul the Apostle founded churches in Athens, Corinth, Philippi, Thessalonica, and other Greek cities — among the earliest Christian communities in the world

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Greece* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/greece/
- CIA World Factbook: Greece — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/greece/
- European Court of Human Rights, *Kokkinakis v. Greece*, Application No. 14307/88 (1993) — echr.coe.int

COUNTRY: Guatemala
TITLE: Guatemala — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/guatemala
META DESCRIPTION: Guatemala is a majority-evangelical Protestant nation where pastors and church leaders in rural indigenous communities face targeted violence from drug trafficking organizations and criminal networks that see the church as a threat to their control.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Guatemala, Central America, Evangelical, Catholic, Gang Violence, Pastoral Persecution, Indigenous Christianity, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Guatemala is one of the most Christian countries in Latin America and, strikingly, one of the most evangelical. Approximately 40% of Guatemalans identify as Catholic and 40% as Protestant — the vast majority of the Protestant community belonging to evangelical and Pentecostal denominations. The state poses no threat to Christians in Guatemala. Guatemala’s challenge for the church is similar to Colombia’s: drug trafficking organizations, criminal networks, and gang structures that control rural territory specifically target pastors and church leaders who challenge their authority, recruit youth away from the criminal economy, or provide community moral leadership that competes with criminal power.

## Christianity in Guatemala

Spanish colonization established Catholicism through the Dominican, Franciscan, and Jesuit orders. The evangelization of indigenous populations was both genuine and coercive, and the syncretic blending of Christian and indigenous religious practice in some Mayan communities is a documented and complex phenomenon. The devastating civil war (1960–1996) — which killed an estimated 200,000 people and included documented massacres of indigenous Mayan communities — was a defining trauma in which the Catholic Church’s human rights advocacy cost lives. Bishop Juan Gerardi of Guatemala City was murdered in 1998, two days after releasing a major human rights report on the war. Evangelical and Pentecostal Christianity has grown dramatically since the mid-20th century.

## Criminal Violence and the Church

In communities controlled by drug trafficking organizations or gangs, pastors who preach against drug use, run rehabilitation programs, or provide moral community leadership are targeted with threats, extortion, and assassination. International Christian Concern and Open Doors document Guatemala as a country where pastors are regularly killed by non-state criminal actors, particularly in rural indigenous communities.

## Current Status

Guatemala sits in the lower-to-middle tier of the Open Doors World Watch List, not for state persecution but for non-state criminal violence against church leaders. The majority of Guatemalan Christians worship freely. The specific risk is geographically concentrated in criminal-controlled rural areas.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80%
- **Primary Threat:** Criminal organizations and drug trafficking networks targeting rural pastors and church leaders
- **Current Status:** Full legal religious freedom; non-state criminal violence against church leaders in rural areas documented
- **Open Doors WWL:** Lower-mid tier
- **Historical Note:** Guatemala is among the most Protestant-evangelical countries in Latin America

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Guatemala* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/guatemala/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Guatemala* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/guatemala/
- CIA World Factbook: Guatemala — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/guatemala/
- International Christian Concern, *Guatemala Country Profile* — persecution.org/country/guatemala/
- Human Rights Watch, *Guatemala country reports* — hrw.org/americas/guatemala

COUNTRY: Guinea
TITLE: Guinea — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/guinea
META DESCRIPTION: Guinea is a predominantly Muslim West African nation where Christians — about 10% of the population — practice with relative freedom in urban areas but face social discrimination and pressure on converts in Muslim-majority communities.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Guinea, West Africa, Islam, Christian Minority, Converts, Discrimination, Coup, Political Instability
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Guinea is a West African nation of approximately 14 million people, of whom roughly 85% are Muslim and approximately 10% are Christian (mainly Catholic and evangelical Protestant). Christians can practice openly in urban centers and in regions where they form a significant presence. In Muslim-majority communities — particularly among the Fula (Peul) and Mandinka peoples — converts from Islam face severe social consequences, and the environment for Christian witness is significantly restricted.

Guinea has experienced significant political instability. Alpha Condé was overthrown in a military coup in September 2021 by Colonel Mamadi Doumbouya. The transitional government has not signaled hostility toward Christians, but political instability always creates uncertainty for minority communities.

The Catholic Church has maintained hospitals, schools, and social services in Guinea that have given it a presence and reputation beyond its numerical weight. In urban areas, interreligious relations are generally civil. In deeply rural Muslim communities, conversion from Islam to Christianity is treated as a profound social rupture — family rejection, community ostracism, and physical danger are documented consequences.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~10%
- **Primary Concerns:** Social and family pressure on converts from Islam; discrimination in Muslim-majority communities; political instability
- **Current Status:** Urban Christians practice freely; rural converts face serious social consequences
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Guinea* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/guinea/

COUNTRY: Guinea-Bissau
TITLE: Guinea-Bissau — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/guinea-bissau
META DESCRIPTION: Guinea-Bissau is a religiously diverse West African nation with a Muslim majority, Christian minority, and large traditional religion community. Christians practice freely in a largely tolerant environment.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Guinea-Bissau, West Africa, Islam, Christian Minority, Traditional Religions, Religious Tolerance, Poverty
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Guinea-Bissau is a small, extremely poor West African nation of approximately 2 million people with a diverse religious landscape: approximately 45% Muslim, 22% Christian (predominantly Catholic, with evangelical communities), and 33% practicing traditional African religions. The country has a remarkable degree of religious syncretism and tolerance. Christians practice freely in Guinea-Bissau. There is no pattern of state persecution, no significant Islamist pressure, and no notable violence against Christians. Guinea-Bissau is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~22%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no systematic persecution; notable religious tolerance
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Guyana
TITLE: Guyana — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/guyana
META DESCRIPTION: Guyana is a religiously diverse South American nation where Christians form the majority and practice freely alongside significant Hindu and Muslim communities. No persecution exists; interreligious relations are generally positive.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Guyana, South America, Christian Majority, Hindu, Muslim, Religious Freedom, Caribbean
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Guyana is a small South American nation of approximately 800,000 people with an extraordinarily diverse religious landscape inherited from its colonial and post-colonial history. Approximately 57% of Guyanese are Christian — including Anglican, Catholic, Methodist, Pentecostal, and various evangelical denominations. Approximately 25% are Hindu and approximately 7% are Muslim — both communities descended from Indian indentured laborers brought by the British. Interreligious relations in Guyana are generally described as harmonious. There is no pattern of Christian persecution; all communities practice freely.

Guyana is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. The country’s challenges are primarily political and economic — though recent offshore oil discoveries have dramatically changed the economic outlook.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~57%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; notable interreligious harmony
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Haiti
TITLE: Haiti — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/haiti
META DESCRIPTION: Haiti is overwhelmingly Christian on paper, but the collapse of state authority, epidemic gang violence, and extreme poverty have created one of the Western Hemisphere’s worst humanitarian crises — one that has devastated the church alongside every other institution in the country.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Haiti, Caribbean, Catholic, Evangelical, Gang Violence, State Collapse, Vodou, Poverty, Church Attacks
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Haiti is approximately 80% Christian — roughly 55% Catholic and 25% Protestant, primarily Pentecostal and evangelical — with a significant population that blends Christianity with Vodou, Haiti’s indigenous spiritual tradition. Christians in Haiti face no state persecution and no anti-Christian ideology in government. But Haiti presents a different kind of threat to the church: the near-total collapse of state authority, the takeover of large portions of the country by violent criminal gangs, and a humanitarian crisis of staggering proportions that has left the church — one of the few functioning institutions in the country — physically targeted, financially depleted, and socially overwhelmed.

## The Haitian Church: Deep Roots and Hard History

Haiti was the first Black republic in the world, established through the only successful slave revolution in history when former enslaved Africans defeated Napoleon’s army and declared independence in 1804. Vodou — practiced widely among the enslaved population — was not merely a spiritual practice but a form of cultural resistance and community solidarity under slavery.

After independence, the Catholic Church’s formal presence in Haiti was interrupted — the Vatican did not recognize the Haitian republic for decades — and the church that functioned was largely Haitian-administered and syncretic in practice. Protestant missions entered Haiti in the 19th century, and evangelical and Pentecostal Christianity has grown dramatically in the past fifty years. Today, evangelical and Pentecostal Haitian Christianity is dynamic, culturally rooted, and deeply committed.

## The 2010 Earthquake and Its Aftermath

The January 12, 2010 earthquake — magnitude 7.0, epicenter near Port-au-Prince — killed an estimated 160,000–316,000 people, injured 300,000, and left 1.5 million homeless. It was one of the deadliest natural disasters in recorded history. Churches, schools, and hospitals were destroyed alongside homes and government buildings. The Catholic Cathedral of Port-au-Prince collapsed, killing the Archbishop. Hundreds of pastors and priests died.

The years following saw significant church rebuilding. But Haiti’s fragility was structural, and the decade after 2010 brought serial crises: a cholera epidemic, political instability, devastating hurricanes, and the assassination of President Jovenel Moïse in July 2021 — a killing that accelerated the country’s descent into gang-dominated chaos.

## Gang Control and Attacks on the Church

Since 2021, criminal gangs have seized control of an estimated 80% of Port-au-Prince and significant portions of the surrounding region. For the church, the gang takeover has been devastating. Pastors and priests have been kidnapped for ransom. Church buildings in gang-controlled areas have been looted, vandalized, and in some cases seized and used by gangs.

The most dramatic single incident was the kidnapping in October 2021 of 17 Christian Aid Ministries missionaries — 12 Americans and 5 Haitians, including five children — by the 400 Mawozo gang near Port-au-Prince. The group was held for two months before members escaped or were released through negotiations.

Church-run schools and hospitals — which provide a substantial portion of Haiti’s social services given the effective absence of a functioning state — have been forced to close in many areas under gang control.

## The Church as a Remaining Institution

Despite everything, the Haitian church remains one of the few functioning civil institutions in the country. Pastors in many communities are the de facto community leaders — conflict mediators, relief distributors, record-keepers, and moral authorities. The church is not just spiritually present in Haiti’s crisis — it is practically essential.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80%
- **Primary Threats:** Gang violence, kidnapping of church leaders and missionaries, destruction of church property, collapse of state protection
- **Current Status:** No state persecution; extreme non-state violence and humanitarian collapse affect the church severely
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored for non-state actor violence
- **Notable:** 17 Christian Aid Ministries missionaries kidnapped October 2021; multiple pastors killed or held for ransom

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Haiti* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/haiti/
- CIA World Factbook: Haiti — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/haiti/
- UN OCHA, *Haiti Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/haiti
- Human Rights Watch, *Haiti country reports* — hrw.org/americas/haiti
- Open Doors USA, *Haiti monitoring* — opendoorsusa.org
- Christian Aid Ministries, *Haiti missionary kidnapping statement* — christianaidministries.org
