COUNTRY: Honduras
TITLE: Honduras — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/honduras
META DESCRIPTION: Honduras is a majority-Christian nation where the primary threat to the church comes from the world’s most powerful criminal organizations targeting pastors and community leaders whose moral authority competes with gang control.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Honduras, Central America, Catholic, Evangelical, Gang Violence, MS-13, Pastoral Persecution, Narco Trafficking
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Honduras is approximately 46% Catholic and 41% Protestant — the Protestant majority being largely evangelical and Pentecostal in character. The Honduran state guarantees religious freedom, and Christians face no government persecution. Honduras’s documented threat to church leaders mirrors that of Colombia and Guatemala: drug trafficking organizations, MS-13, Barrio 18, and related criminal networks operating in communities where pastors and church leaders represent moral authority that competes with criminal power — and paying for that competition with threats, displacement, and assassination.

## Christianity in Honduras

Spanish colonization established Catholicism through the Franciscan, Dominican, and Jesuit orders from the 16th century. Protestant missions entered the country in the 19th century, and Pentecostal and evangelical Christianity has grown rapidly from the mid-20th century onward. Today, Honduras has one of the highest proportions of evangelical Christians in Central America.

## Gang Violence and Pastoral Targeting

Honduras has been among the world’s most violent countries by murder rate for most of the past two decades. In communities where gangs control territory, pastors occupy a specifically vulnerable position. They are known community figures who interact with youth the gangs want to recruit, who preach ethics and redemption that challenge the criminal worldview, and who provide trusted community leadership that undermines gang authority. The threat is typically delivered as a demand: cooperate, pay extortion, or leave.

The Atlantic coast region (particularly around San Pedro Sula and the Sula Valley) has historically been among the most dangerous in the country and has the highest concentration of documented attacks on church leaders. Honduras sits in the lower tier of the Open Doors World Watch List specifically because of this pattern of non-state criminal violence against church leaders.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~87%
- **Primary Threat:** Gang and criminal organizations targeting pastors and church leaders in controlled territory
- **Current Status:** Full legal religious freedom; non-state criminal violence against church leaders documented
- **Open Doors WWL:** Lower tier

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Honduras* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/honduras/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Honduras* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/honduras/
- CIA World Factbook: Honduras — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/honduras/
- International Christian Concern, *Honduras* — persecution.org/country/honduras/
- Human Rights Watch, *Honduras country reports* — hrw.org/americas/honduras

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Hungary
TITLE: Hungary — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/hungary
META DESCRIPTION: Hungary is a majority-Catholic nation with a constitutionally Christian identity and full religious freedom for traditional denominations, though smaller evangelical and minority religious groups have faced legal restrictions on recognition.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Hungary, Central Europe, Catholic, Reformed, Religious Freedom, Church Law, Orbán
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Hungary is approximately 52% Catholic and 16% Reformed (Calvinist) Protestant. Christians practice freely; Hungary is not a persecution country. However, a 2011 Church Law under Prime Minister Viktor Orbán stripped legal recognition from hundreds of small religious groups and denominations that had been recognized since 1990, requiring re-registration through a parliamentary vote — a process that favored established historic churches and created significant barriers for smaller evangelical and independent communities. Several denominations affected appealed to the European Court of Human Rights and won rulings against Hungary in 2014.

Hungary’s 2011 constitution declares that “we recognize the role of Christianity in preserving the nation” — language that has been criticized as privileging Christianity in a multi-religious democratic state. Hungary is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~68%
- **Current Status:** Traditional denominations fully free; smaller groups faced legal barriers under 2011 Church Law (partially addressed after ECHR rulings)
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Hungary* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/hungary/
- CIA World Factbook: Hungary — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/hungary/
- European Court of Human Rights, *Hungarian Church Recognition Cases* — echr.coe.int
- Freedom House, *Freedom in the World 2024: Hungary* — freedomhouse.org/country/hungary/freedom-world/2024

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Iceland
TITLE: Iceland — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/iceland
META DESCRIPTION: Iceland is one of the world’s most secular nations, with a Lutheran state church and full religious freedom. Christians face no persecution whatsoever.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Iceland, Nordic, Lutheran, Secular, Religious Freedom, State Church
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Iceland was Christianized at the Althing (parliament) in 1000 AD — one of the more remarkable moments in Christian history, when the Icelandic assembly voted collectively to adopt Christianity as the national faith (with the compromise that pagans could continue private practice). The Evangelical Lutheran Church of Iceland has been the national church since the Reformation in the 16th century. Christians of all traditions worship freely in Iceland. Iceland is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~75% (nominal; active practice far lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Iceland* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/iceland/
- CIA World Factbook: Iceland — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/iceland/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: India
TITLE: India — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/india
META DESCRIPTION: India is home to one of the world’s oldest Christian communities — and to a rising tide of Hindu nationalist violence that has burned churches, killed pastors, murdered missionaries, and stripped Christians of legal protections through anti-conversion laws. The world’s largest democracy is in a crisis of religious freedom.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: India, South Asia, Hindu Nationalism, RSS, BJP, Anti-Conversion Laws, Mob Violence, WWL Top 15, Thomas Christians, Dalit Christians
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #11 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

India is the world’s largest democracy, a nation of 1.4 billion people, and home to one of the oldest Christian communities on earth. By tradition, the Apostle Thomas arrived on the Malabar Coast of what is now Kerala in 52 AD, and the Christian community he founded — the St. Thomas Christians, or Nasrani — has maintained an unbroken presence for nearly two thousand years. India’s Christians today number approximately 28–31 million — roughly 2.3% of the total population — belonging to an extraordinary diversity of traditions: ancient Oriental Orthodox, Catholic, Protestant mainline, and a vast evangelical and Pentecostal community that has grown dramatically in the past century.

India also presents one of the world’s most rapidly deteriorating religious freedom environments for Christians. Since the rise of the Bharatiya Janata Party (BJP) under Prime Minister Narendra Modi — first elected in 2014, re-elected in 2019 and 2024 — Hindu nationalist ideology has moved from the political margins to the center of Indian governance. Vigilante violence by organizations linked to the Rashtriya Swayamsevak Sangh (RSS) has targeted Christians with increasing frequency: church burnings, disruption of worship services, beatings of pastors, false accusations under anti-conversion laws, and in some cases murder. Anti-conversion laws now exist in at least eleven Indian states, criminalizing conversion from Hinduism through “force, fraud, or inducement” — categories so broadly defined that they are routinely applied to ordinary evangelism. India ranked #11 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List — the highest it has ever ranked.

## The Apostolic Foundation: Thomas in Kerala

The tradition that the Apostle Thomas reached India is one of the most ancient and geographically specific in Christian history. What is clear from the historical record is that Christianity was firmly established in Kerala by the late first and early second centuries AD. When Portuguese missionaries arrived in Kerala in 1498, they were astonished to find a thriving Christian community that had never been in contact with Rome. The subsequent Coonan Cross Oath of 1653 — in which a large portion of the community vowed never to submit to Portuguese ecclesiastical authority — produced a schism whose effects still shape the many Thomas Christian denominations that exist today.

## Colonial-Era Christianity

British missionaries arrived in the 19th century — William Carey, the Baptist missionary who arrived in Bengal in 1793, established the template for modern Protestant mission: learning the language, translating the Bible, establishing schools, and working at the grassroots of society. The great 19th-century missionary expansion left India with Christian schools, hospitals, and colleges that remain today among the country’s finest educational and medical institutions.

Christianity had particular appeal among Dalit communities — the lowest strata of Hindu society — who converted in large numbers through the 19th and 20th centuries as an act of liberation from a caste system that denied them full humanity.

## The Northeast India Phenomenon

India’s northeastern states — particularly Nagaland, Mizoram, and Meghalaya — are approximately 75–90% Christian, converted through 19th and 20th-century Baptist and Welsh Calvinist Methodist missions. These states, inhabited primarily by tribal communities with distinct ethnic identities from the Hindu mainstream, maintain some of the most theologically serious and missionally active churches in Asia. Nagaland sends more missionaries per capita than almost any other region in the world.

## The Rise of Hindu Nationalism

The RSS, founded in 1925, holds that India belongs to Hindus and that conversion away from Hinduism is an act of cultural betrayal. The BJP’s rise to national power in 2014 under Narendra Modi emboldened the grassroots Hindu nationalist network. Anti-conversion laws now exist in at least eleven states. These laws criminalize religious conversion through “force, fraud, or inducement” — in practice applied through a consistent pattern: Hindu nationalist activists file a police complaint accusing a pastor of conducting a “forced” or “fraudulent” conversion; police arrest the accused; the arrest serves to disrupt church activity, intimidate the congregation, and generate public accusations of criminality.

## Documented Violence: From Graham Staines to the Present

The killing of Australian missionary Graham Staines and his two young sons, Philip and Timothy, in January 1999 — burned alive in their vehicle by a Hindu nationalist mob while they slept — established the deadly potential of anti-Christian violence in India. The Kandhamal Massacres of 2007 and 2008 in Odisha killed approximately 100 Christians, displaced over 50,000, and burned more than 300 churches. In 2022 alone, Open Doors documented over 1,500 specific incidents of anti-Christian violence in India. Dalit Christians face both religious persecution and legal discrimination simultaneously — they are denied the Scheduled Caste reservation benefits afforded to Dalit Hindus, Buddhists, and Sikhs under a 1950 presidential order.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #11 (highest ranking in India’s history)
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 28–31 million (~2.3%)
- **Primary Threats:** Anti-conversion laws (11+ states), Hindu nationalist mob violence, false police complaints, beatings of pastors, church arson, Dalit Christian legal discrimination
- **Historic Community:** St. Thomas Christians of Kerala — among the oldest continuous Christian communities in the world
- **Notable Events:** Graham Staines murder (1999); Kandhamal massacres (2007–2008); 1,500+ documented anti-Christian incidents (2022)

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: India* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/india/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: India* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/india/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: India* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: India — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/india/
- Human Rights Watch, *India: Hindu Nationalist Violence against Minorities* — hrw.org/asia/india
- Amnesty International, *India* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/india/
- International Christian Concern, *India Country Profile* — persecution.org/country/india/
- Evangelical Fellowship of India (EFI) — efionline.org
- United Christian Forum (UCF) — unitedchristianforum.org
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for St. Thomas tradition

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Indonesia
TITLE: Indonesia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/indonesia
META DESCRIPTION: Indonesia is the world’s most populous Muslim country and home to 26 million Christians — the third-largest Christian population in Asia. Christians face church closure campaigns, Islamist violence, blasphemy prosecutions, and severe restrictions in Aceh Province, but most of the archipelago’s Christians worship with relative freedom.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Indonesia, Southeast Asia, Islam, Church Closures, Blasphemy Law, Islamist Violence, Maluku, Aceh, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Indonesia is simultaneously home to the world’s largest Muslim population (approximately 231 million) and one of Asia’s most significant Christian communities (approximately 26 million, roughly 10% of the population). Its national motto — Bhinneka Tunggal Ika, “Unity in Diversity” — reflects a founding commitment to pluralism that has been tested repeatedly since independence in 1945. The Indonesian experience of Christianity spans the full spectrum: from peaceful, well-established communities in majority-Christian provinces like North Sulawesi, Papua, and North Sumatra, to communities in Java, West Papua, and the Maluku Islands that have experienced devastating Islamist violence, systematic church closures, and forced displacement.

## Christianity’s Presence

Portuguese Catholic missionaries came to the Indonesian archipelago in the 16th century. Dutch colonial rule from the early 17th century brought Dutch Reformed Christianity. In the 19th and 20th centuries, German and American missions established churches across Sumatra, Kalimantan, and Sulawesi. Today, Indonesian Christianity is geographically concentrated: the provinces of East Nusa Tenggara, North Sulawesi, North Sumatra (Batak Christians), Papua, and West Papua have Christian majorities or significant pluralities.

## Church Closures: The Joint Ministerial Decree

The most persistent structural threat to Indonesian Christians is the 2006 Joint Ministerial Decree on Houses of Worship, which requires that a new church obtain the signatures of 90 local residents of other faiths supporting its construction. In practice, this requirement gives local Muslim communities a veto over church construction in Muslim-majority areas. The Islamic Defenders Front (FPI), before its formal disbanding by the government in 2020, was the primary organization that pressured local governments to close churches.

## Islamist Violence: Maluku and Beyond

The most devastating episode of anti-Christian violence in Indonesia’s modern history occurred in the Maluku Islands between 1999 and 2002, when sectarian conflict between Christian and Muslim communities — fueled by jihadist fighters from outside the region — killed an estimated 5,000–10,000 people and displaced 500,000. Church bombings have occurred throughout Indonesia. The May 2018 Surabaya church bombings — carried out by a family who drove vehicles and walked suicide bombers into three churches simultaneously on a Sunday morning — killed 13 people.

## Blasphemy Laws and Prosecutions

Indonesia’s blasphemy law has been used against Christians in documented cases. The most politically prominent case was the prosecution of Basuki Tjahaja Purnama (“Ahok”), the Christian Chinese-Indonesian governor of Jakarta, in 2016–2017 on blasphemy charges widely regarded as politically motivated. He was sentenced to two years in prison.

## Papua: A Separate Crisis

West Papua and Papua provinces have a predominantly Christian population who have been in conflict with the Indonesian state since Papua’s integration in 1969. Indonesian military operations have affected Christian communities. The religious and indigenous rights dimensions of the Papua situation overlap in complex ways.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~26 million (~10%)
- **Primary Threats:** Joint Ministerial Decree enabling church closures, blasphemy law prosecutions, Islamist violence, Surabaya bombings (2018)
- **Current Status:** Regionally varied; significant structural legal barriers alongside areas of relative freedom
- **Open Doors WWL:** Lower-mid tier ranked
- **Notable:** Maluku conflict (1999–2002) killed ~5,000–10,000; Surabaya church bombings (2018) killed 13; Ahok blasphemy prosecution (2016–2017)

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Indonesia* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/indonesia/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Indonesia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/indonesia/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Indonesia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/indonesia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Indonesia country reports* — hrw.org/asia/indonesia
- Amnesty International, *Indonesia* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/indonesia/
- International Crisis Group, *Indonesia reporting* — crisisgroup.org

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Iran
TITLE: Iran — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/iran
META DESCRIPTION: Iran’s Islamic Revolution turned one of the Middle East’s most historic Christian nations into one of its most dangerous. House church pastors are imprisoned, converts face the death penalty for apostasy, and the underground church has grown to hundreds of thousands despite — or because of — the pressure.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Iran, Middle East, Islamic Republic, House Church, WWL Top 10, Apostasy, Imprisonment, Persian Christians
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #9 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Iran is home to one of the Middle East’s most ancient and historically significant Christian communities — and one of the world’s most dangerous environments for Christians today. The 1979 Islamic Revolution transformed Iran from a constitutional monarchy with legal religious pluralism into an Islamic Republic in which Christianity is tolerated only within strictly defined limits, where conversion from Islam to Christianity is punishable by death under sharia, and where the house church movement that has grown explosively among Persian-background Iranians faces systematic arrest, imprisonment, and torture. Iran ranked #9 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## The Ancient Christian Communities

Christianity reached Persia in the first and second centuries AD. The Church of the East — the Nestorian tradition — became the primary expression of Christianity across the Sassanid Persian Empire. Despite Zoroastrian state religion, Christian communities survived and grew; the great persecution of 340–401 AD under the Sassanids killed an estimated 190,000 Christians but did not eliminate the community. Arab Islamic conquest in the 7th century replaced Zoroastrianism with Islam as the dominant faith, but Christian communities of Persia — primarily Nestorian, Armenian, and later Chaldean Catholic — continued as recognized religious minorities.

## The Islamic Revolution and Its Aftermath

The 1979 revolution imposed a constitutional framework in which Islam is the state religion, sharia is the supreme law, and non-Muslim Iranians are classified as dhimmis with limited legal standing. Recognized religious minorities — Armenian and Assyrian Christians, Zoroastrians, and Jews — are formally permitted to practice their faith within defined boundaries. What they cannot do: evangelize Muslims, publish Christian materials in Persian, hold services in Persian, or in any way engage the Muslim population of Iran with the gospel.

This restriction is the key to understanding Iran’s persecution of Christians. The historic Armenian and Assyrian Christian communities — who worship in their own languages, in their own ancient churches — are tolerated. The Christians who face acute danger are Persian-background believers — ethnic Persians who have converted from Islam to Christianity. Their existence is legally precarious: conversion from Islam is apostasy under Iranian law, potentially punishable by death, and the house churches in which they worship are unregistered and therefore illegal.

## The House Church Explosion

One of the most remarkable developments in global Christianity is the explosive growth of the underground church in Iran since 1979. Christian mission organizations and monitoring groups describe a genuine movement: hundreds of thousands of Iranians — estimates range from 300,000 to 1,000,000 or more — have come to faith in Christ since the revolution, drawn by satellite television broadcasting, social media, and the testimony of other converts. The irony is well-attested: the Islamic Republic, which sought to establish a perfect Islamic society, has instead generated one of the largest church growth movements in the world.

## Martyrs and Prisoners

Pastor Haik Hovsepian Mehr, an Armenian-background Christian leader who advocated publicly for the rights of Iranian converts, was killed in January 1994. Two more Protestant pastors — Tateos Michaelian and Mehdi Dibaj — were killed later in 1994. Dibaj had been sentenced to death for apostasy and released only under international pressure before being killed. Pastor Youcef Nadarkhani — arrested in 2009, sentenced to death for apostasy, and held for three years under intense international pressure — was eventually released on other charges. Pastor Saeed Abedini — an Iranian-American — was imprisoned in 2012 and sentenced to eight years; released in 2016 as part of a prisoner exchange with the United States. Hundreds of house church members and leaders have been arrested in coordinated raids, particularly around Christmas and Easter.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #9
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 300,000–1,000,000 (predominantly Persian-background converts in underground house churches)
- **Primary Threats:** Apostasy law (death penalty), raids on house churches, imprisonment of pastors, pressure to identify other believers
- **Legal Status:** Recognized minority Christians tolerated within limits; Persian-background converts in underground churches illegal
- **Notable:** Pastor Youcef Nadarkhani, Pastor Saeed Abedini — internationally recognized cases; underground church estimated at hundreds of thousands

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Iran* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/iran/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Iran* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/iran/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Iran* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Iran — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/iran/
- Human Rights Watch, *Iran country reports* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/iran
- Amnesty International, *Iran* — amnesty.org/en/countries/middle-east-and-north-africa/iran/
- Elam Ministries, *Iran Church* — elam.com
- Article18 (advocacy for Iranian Christian rights) — article18.org
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Iran* — csw.org.uk
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Nestorian Persian church history

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Iraq
TITLE: Iraq — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/iraq
META DESCRIPTION: Iraq was home to one of the world’s oldest Christian communities — worshiping in the language of Jesus himself. From 1.5 million in 2003, the Christian population has fallen to 150,000–250,000. ISIS’s genocide nearly finished what decades of war, discrimination, and targeted killings began.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Iraq, Middle East, Chaldean, Assyrian, Ancient Church, ISIS, Genocide, Displacement, Nineveh Plains, WWL Top 20
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #17 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Iraq is one of the most tragic stories in the history of Christianity. The Mesopotamian region — modern Iraq — was among the earliest centers of Christian mission. The church that took root there in the first and second centuries AD worshiped in Syriac, a dialect of Aramaic — the language spoken by Jesus himself — and produced theological, missionary, and liturgical traditions of extraordinary depth. In modern Iraq, before 2003, there were an estimated 1.4 to 1.5 million Christians — Chaldean Catholics, Assyrian Orthodox, Syriac Catholics, and others — representing approximately 6% of the population. Today, following twenty years of war, targeted killings, the ISIS genocide, and displacement, there are estimated to be between 150,000 and 250,000 Christians remaining. The church of the Nineveh Plains — which had existed continuously for nineteen centuries — came within an apocalyptic moment of being entirely extinguished.

## The Mesopotamian Foundation

The Book of Acts records that “Parthians, Medes and Elamites” were present in Jerusalem at Pentecost — people from the Mesopotamian sphere. The ancient city of Edessa (modern Urfa in Turkey, near the Iraqi border), according to ancient tradition, was evangelized by the Apostle Thaddaeus himself. The Syriac church — worshiping in a language closely related to what Jesus spoke — spread east through Persia to India and China. The Nestorian Stele, erected in Xian in 781 AD, is evidence of this extraordinary reach.

## The 2003 Invasion and Its Consequences

The U.S.-led invasion of Iraq in March 2003 and the subsequent collapse of the Ba’athist state removed the secular authoritarian framework that had, whatever its other crimes, protected religious minorities from communal violence. Churches were bombed, priests were kidnapped and killed, Christian business owners were extorted, and families were threatened to leave. Our Lady of Salvation Church in Baghdad was attacked in October 2010, killing 52 people in one of the most devastating single attacks on Christians in modern Middle Eastern history. Archbishop Paulos Faraj Rahho of Mosul was kidnapped and killed in 2008. The Christian population of Baghdad, once 250,000 or more, had shrunk to tens of thousands by 2012.

## ISIS and the Genocide of Nineveh’s Christians

In August 2014, ISIS reached the Christian towns of the Nineveh Plains: Qaraqosh (Bakhdida), Tel Keppe, Bartella, Karamles, and others — towns that had been Christian since the earliest centuries of the church, where Syriac was still spoken. ISIS gave the inhabitants an ultimatum: convert to Islam, pay the jizya tax, leave, or be killed. Within days, 100,000–125,000 Christians fled. ISIS occupied these towns for two years. Churches were converted to mosques or prisons; ancient manuscripts and artifacts were destroyed or stolen.

The United States Congress, the UK Parliament, and the European Parliament all passed resolutions declaring what happened to the Yazidis and Christians of Iraq a genocide. The designation was correct.

## Liberation and Partial Return

ISIS was driven out of the Nineveh Plains by Kurdish Peshmerga and Iraqi forces in late 2016. Pope Francis’s historic visit to Iraq in March 2021 — the first papal visit to Iraq — was centered on the Nineveh Plains. He visited Qaraqosh, celebrated Mass in the liberated town’s rebuilt church, and prayed at the ruins of a church ISIS had used as a target range.

Of the 100,000+ who fled in 2014, an estimated 30,000–40,000 had returned to the Nineveh Plains by 2022. The return has been partial and fragile.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #17
- **Christian Population 2003:** ~1.4–1.5 million
- **Christian Population Today:** ~150,000–250,000
- **Primary Historical Threats:** Post-2003 targeted killings and church bombings; ISIS genocide (2014); displacement
- **Current Concerns:** Fragile security in Nineveh Plains, land disputes, militia presence, continued emigration
- **Notable:** Our Lady of Salvation bombing (2010) — 52 killed; Archbishop Rahho kidnapped and killed (2008); Pope Francis visit (2021); Syriac Christianity preserved the language of Jesus

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Iraq* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/iraq/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Iraq* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/iraq/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Iraq* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Iraq — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/iraq/
- Human Rights Watch, *Iraq: ISIS Crimes Against Christians and Yazidis* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/iraq
- Aid to the Church in Need, *Iraq reporting* — acn-intl.org
- UN Assistance Mission for Iraq (UNAMI) — uniraq.org
- Knights of Columbus, documentation of Iraqi Christian persecution — kofc.org
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Syriac/Mesopotamian church history

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Ireland
TITLE: Ireland — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/ireland
META DESCRIPTION: Ireland is a historically Catholic nation experiencing one of Europe’s most dramatic secularizations. Christians practice freely with no persecution, but the institutional church is reckoning with devastating abuse scandals that have transformed its social standing.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Ireland, Western Europe, Catholic, Protestant, Secularization, Abuse Scandal, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Ireland was historically among the most deeply Catholic countries in Europe — the Irish Catholic identity fused faith, national resistance to British Protestant rule, and cultural distinctiveness into a single powerful combination. Today, Ireland is undergoing one of the most dramatic secularizations in the Western world, driven primarily by the Catholic Church’s catastrophic institutional abuse scandals. Christians practice freely in Ireland; no persecution exists.

## The Irish Christian Tradition

Christianity arrived in Ireland primarily through St. Patrick in the 5th century. The Irish church developed a distinctive monastic character that produced some of the most remarkable Christian scholarship of the early medieval period. Irish monks — Columbanus, Columba, and others — became missionaries to pagan continental Europe. The Book of Kells and other illuminated manuscripts produced in Irish monasteries are among the most beautiful objects in Christian cultural history.

The identification of Catholicism with Irish national identity and Protestantism with English colonial rule is the root of the sectarian dynamics that defined Irish history through the 20th century and produced the Troubles in Northern Ireland (1968–1998).

## The Abuse Reckoning

The late 20th and early 21st centuries brought wave after wave of revelations about systemic abuse within the Irish Catholic Church: the clerical sexual abuse of thousands of children, the Magdalene Laundries, and the Mother and Baby Homes. Government commissions — the Ryan Report (2009), the Murphy Report (2009), and the Mother and Baby Homes Commission Report (2021) — documented institutional failures at a scale that shattered the church’s moral authority. In 2018, Ireland voted by a substantial majority to repeal its constitutional abortion ban — widely interpreted as a vote against church influence over public life.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~72% (nominal; active practice significantly lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; massive institutional credibility crisis
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** St. Patrick (5th century); Irish monks re-Christianized continental Europe; Book of Kells; Catholic-Protestant sectarian conflict in Northern Ireland (the Troubles, 1968–1998)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Ireland* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/ireland/
- CIA World Factbook: Ireland — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/ireland/
- Truth and Reconciliation Commission of Ireland equivalents: Ryan Report (2009), Murphy Report (2009), Mother and Baby Homes Commission Report (2021) — available via gov.ie
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Israel and the Palestinian Territories
TITLE: Israel and the Palestinian Territories — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/israel-and-palestinian-territories
META DESCRIPTION: The land where Jesus walked is home to a shrinking but deeply significant Christian community. In Israel, Christians are free but face social friction. In the West Bank, Palestinian Christians navigate between Israeli military control and Palestinian political pressure. In Gaza under Hamas, Christians have been reduced to a tiny, imperiled remnant.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Israel, Palestinian Territories, West Bank, Gaza, Hamas, Holy Land, Palestinian Christians, Holy Sites, Messianic Jews
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

The land of the Bible — the region where Jesus was born, baptized, crucified, and resurrected — today holds a Christian community of profound spiritual significance and fragile demographic reality. Of the Holy Land’s approximately 14 million inhabitants, approximately 175,000 are Christian — a figure that has been declining for decades due to emigration. The Christian situation varies dramatically by geography: Israeli Arabs who are Christian have formal equal rights under Israeli law; Palestinian Christians in the West Bank navigate occupation and restricted movement; the tiny Christian community in Gaza under Hamas control faces an acute and deteriorating crisis.

## The Holy Land Church: Historical Depth

The church in the Holy Land is not a modern transplant — it is the original church, in the land of its birth. The Jerusalem church, led initially by James the brother of Jesus, was the mother church of all Christianity. The Church of the Holy Sepulchre has been a continuous place of Christian pilgrimage and worship for seventeen centuries. The Christian community is ancient and diverse: Greek Orthodox, Armenian Apostolic, Catholic (Roman and Eastern rites), Coptic, Ethiopian, Syrian, Maronite, Anglican, Lutheran, and a growing evangelical and Messianic Jewish community.

## Christians in Israel

Within Israel’s pre-1967 borders, Arab Christians number approximately 130,000–180,000. They are Israeli citizens with formal equal rights. In practice, Arab citizens face structural discrimination in resource allocation compared to Jewish citizens. The Messianic Jewish community in Israel — Jewish people who have come to faith in Jesus as Messiah — numbers approximately 30,000–40,000 and faces specific social friction from rabbinical authorities and social pressure from Jewish communities.

## Christians in the West Bank

Palestinian Christians in the West Bank — approximately 40,000–50,000, concentrated in Bethlehem, Ramallah, and East Jerusalem — face the pressures of Israeli military occupation (movement restrictions, checkpoints, settlement expansion) alongside Palestinian Authority governance dynamics and the social pressure of living as a Christian minority in a society that is approximately 97% Muslim. Bethlehem, the city of Jesus’s birth, was approximately 85% Christian in 1948; today it is approximately 15% Christian — a demographic transformation driven almost entirely by emigration.

## Christians in Gaza Under Hamas

Gaza’s Christian community — an ancient presence — has been reduced to approximately 1,000 people, overwhelmingly Greek Orthodox and Catholic. Under Hamas rule since 2007, Christians cannot evangelize, cannot proselytize, and are expected to live as a quiet minority. The October 7, 2023 Hamas attack on Israel and the subsequent Israeli military campaign devastated the territory comprehensively. The Holy Family Catholic Church compound, which sheltered hundreds of Christian civilians during the bombardment, was struck multiple times. Dozens of Christians sheltering there were killed.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~175,000 (Israel, West Bank, and Gaza combined; declining)
- **Primary Concerns:** Israel — social friction for Arab Christians and Messianic Jews; West Bank — movement restrictions, emigration pressure; Gaza — Hamas rule, war devastation, near-extinction of community
- **Current Status:** Legally free in Israel; restricted in West Bank; acutely threatened in Gaza post-2023
- **Open Doors WWL:** Palestinian Territories ranked (moderate tier)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Israel, West Bank and Gaza* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/israel-west-bank-and-gaza/
- CIA World Factbook: Israel — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/israel/; West Bank — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/west-bank/; Gaza Strip — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/gaza-strip/
- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Palestinian Territories* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/
- Human Rights Watch, *Israel/Palestine reporting* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/israel-palestine
- Amnesty International, *Israel/Palestine* — amnesty.org
- UN Office for the Coordination of Humanitarian Affairs, *Occupied Palestinian Territory* — ochaopt.org

COUNTRY: Italy
TITLE: Italy — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/italy
META DESCRIPTION: Italy is the home of the Roman Catholic Church and one of the world’s most historically Christian nations. Christians practice freely; no persecution exists, though the country is secularizing steadily.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Italy, Southern Europe, Catholic, Vatican, Religious Freedom, Secularization
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Italy is the seat of the Roman Catholic Church — Vatican City, the world’s smallest sovereign state and the headquarters of a church with 1.3 billion members globally, sits within Rome. Approximately 74% of Italians identify as Catholic, though active practice is declining. Christians of all traditions worship freely in Italy; there is no persecution. Italy is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

The Apostles Peter and Paul both died in Rome (by tradition under Nero, c. 64–68 AD), and Rome was the most important church in the Western Roman Empire from the 2nd century onward. The Lateran Treaty of 1929 established Vatican City as a sovereign state and gave the Catholic Church a special constitutional role in Italy — a role modified but maintained in the 1984 revision of the concordat.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80% (overwhelmingly Catholic)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; Vatican City within Rome
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Italy* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/italy/
- CIA World Factbook: Italy — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/italy/
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/

COUNTRY: Jamaica
TITLE: Jamaica — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/jamaica
META DESCRIPTION: Jamaica is a deeply Christian Caribbean nation where believers practice freely and faith is central to cultural identity. No persecution exists.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Jamaica, Caribbean, Christian Majority, Baptist, Pentecostal, Rastafari, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Jamaica is a majority-Christian nation — approximately 67% of Jamaicans identify as Christian, with a rich denominational landscape including Baptist, Pentecostal, Church of God, Anglican, Catholic, Seventh-day Adventist, and Moravian communities — where believers practice freely and faith is woven deeply into cultural life.

The Baptist church has a particularly significant role in Jamaican history. Sam Sharpe — a Baptist deacon whose leadership of the Christmas Rebellion slave revolt in 1831–1832 is credited with accelerating emancipation — is a national hero. Emancipation in 1834 was celebrated in Jamaica as a Christian liberation, and the connection between faith and freedom is embedded in the Jamaican Baptist tradition.

Rastafari — the spiritual movement that arose in Jamaica in the 1930s — is a distinctive Jamaican contribution to the global religious landscape, though it is distinct from mainstream Christianity. Jamaica is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~67%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Sam Sharpe — Baptist deacon — led 1831 slave rebellion; credited with accelerating emancipation

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Jamaica* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/jamaica/
- CIA World Factbook: Jamaica — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/jamaica/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Japan
TITLE: Japan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/japan
META DESCRIPTION: Japan has one of the smallest Christian communities of any major nation — roughly 1–2% — but a remarkable history, including the Kakure Kirishitan who practiced in secret for 250 years after the government banned Christianity under pain of death.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Japan, East Asia, Catholic, Protestant, Kakure Kirishitan, Hidden Christians, Secularism, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Japan is home to approximately 1–2% of the population identifying as Christian — roughly 1.9 million people in a nation of 125 million. Christians practice fully freely; there is no state restriction or social persecution of Christians in modern Japan. Japan is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. But Japan’s Christian history includes one of the most remarkable stories of hidden faith in world history — the Kakure Kirishitan, or Hidden Christians, who practiced a secret, syncretic form of Christianity for over 250 years after the Tokugawa shogunate banned and violently persecuted Christianity in the 17th century.

## Francis Xavier and Early Christianity

Francis Xavier, a Jesuit co-founder, arrived in Japan in 1549 and began one of the most successful early modern mission enterprises in Asia. Within fifty years, Japanese Christianity had grown to an estimated 300,000 believers. The Tokugawa shogunate, which unified Japan in the early 17th century, perceived Christianity as a threat to social order and a potential tool of European colonial power. The 26 Martyrs of Japan — crucified in Nagasaki in 1597 — were canonized by the Catholic Church in 1862.

After the Shimabara Rebellion of 1637–1638, the shogunate sealed Japan to almost all foreign contact. Christianity was banned outright; missionaries were expelled or killed; known Christians faced death.

## The Hidden Christians

Instead of Christianity disappearing, tens of thousands of Japanese Christians in the Nagasaki region went underground — the Kakure Kirishitan. For 250 years, without priests, without Bibles (which they could not read), without any contact with the outside church, these communities maintained a form of Christian practice passed down through oral tradition, secret rituals, and household religious objects disguised as Buddhist or Shinto items. When Japan opened to the West in the 1850s and Catholic missionaries returned, they encountered these communities and were astonished.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~1–2%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; culturally secular and challenging for church growth
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Kakure Kirishitan (Hidden Christians) maintained secret faith for 250 years under ban; 26 Martyrs of Nagasaki (1597) canonized

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Japan* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/japan/
- CIA World Factbook: Japan — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/japan/
- Turnbull, Stephen, *The Kakure Kirishitan of Japan: A Study of Their Development, Beliefs and Rituals to the Present Day* (Routledge, 2016)

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Jordan
TITLE: Jordan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/jordan
META DESCRIPTION: Jordan is one of the Middle East’s most stable environments for Christians. An ancient Christian community maintains active churches under a Hashemite monarchy that has consistently protected religious minorities, though conversion from Islam carries severe social and some legal consequences.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Jordan, Middle East, Hashemite, Christian Minority, Ancient Church, Religious Freedom, Converts
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Jordan is among the most religiously tolerant of the Arab Muslim-majority states. Approximately 2–3% of Jordan’s population is Christian — roughly 170,000–200,000 people, predominantly Greek Orthodox, with Catholic, Anglican, Lutheran, and evangelical communities also present. The Hashemite monarchy has consistently positioned itself as a protector of religious minorities and of Christian access to holy sites. Christians can build churches, operate schools and hospitals, and practice their faith openly. Senior Christians serve in the Jordanian military and civil service.

## An Ancient Community

Christianity in Jordan traces to the earliest decades of the church. The Apostle Philip baptized the Ethiopian eunuch on the road through what is now Jordan (Acts 8); the Decapolis — the region of ten Hellenistic cities that included Jerash and Umm Qais — had Christian communities from the 1st century onward. The Madaba Map — a 6th-century mosaic floor map of the Holy Land and the oldest surviving cartographic representation of the Middle East — was created in a Jordanian church. The ancient Christian community of Madaba continues to maintain its churches today.

## Restrictions and Converts

Jordan’s legal framework prohibits proselytism directed at Muslims. Under Jordanian personal status law, a Muslim who converts to Christianity cannot change their officially registered religion; their marriage, inheritance, and custody arrangements continue to be governed by Islamic law. Socially, Jordanian Muslims who convert to Christianity face family rejection, community ostracism, and potential physical danger.

## Palestinian Christian Refugees

Jordan’s Christian community includes a significant number of Palestinian Christians who arrived as refugees after 1948 and 1967. More recently, Iraqi Christian refugees fleeing the post-2003 violence and Syrian Christian refugees fleeing the civil war have added to the number of displaced Middle Eastern Christians in Jordan. Jordan has been a generally hospitable place of refuge for displaced Christians from neighboring countries.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~2–3%
- **Primary Concerns:** Proselytism to Muslims illegal; converts face legal and social consequences; social pressure on those who leave Islam
- **Current Status:** Relatively stable and protected; among the best environments for Christians in the Arab world
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not in top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Jordan* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/jordan/
- CIA World Factbook: Jordan — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/jordan/
- Open Doors USA, *Jordan monitoring* — opendoorsusa.org
- UNHCR, *Jordan refugee statistics* — unhcr.org/jordan

COUNTRY: Kazakhstan
TITLE: Kazakhstan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/kazakhstan
META DESCRIPTION: Kazakhstan is a majority-Muslim Central Asian state where Christianity has deep roots among the Russian and German minority populations and where the government controls all religious life through strict registration and surveillance. Unregistered churches face raids and fines; minority denominations navigate constant bureaucratic pressure.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Kazakhstan, Central Asia, Islam, Russian Orthodox, Protestant, Registration Restrictions, Post-Soviet, Religious Control
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Kazakhstan is the world’s largest landlocked country — a vast Central Asian state of 19 million people. Approximately 68% of the population is nominally Muslim; approximately 19% is Russian Orthodox Christian; and a variety of Protestant, Catholic, and evangelical denominations serve smaller communities. Kazakhstan is a secular authoritarian state that controls religious life through a comprehensive registration and surveillance framework. Christianity is not the primary target of this control, but unregistered churches face raids, fines, and harassment, and the overall environment for religious minorities is one of managed constraint rather than genuine freedom.

## Christianity in Kazakhstan

Christianity arrived in Kazakhstan through Russian imperial expansion in the 18th and 19th centuries, as Russian Cossack settlers and later deportees — including German-background Christians deported by Stalin — established communities across the steppe. The Russian Orthodox Church built its institutional presence alongside Russian administrative control. The Soviet period suppressed all religion, but Russian Orthodoxy survived in diminished form. Protestant and evangelical Christianity grew after independence in 1991.

## Government Religious Control

Kazakhstan’s Religion Law, revised multiple times and significantly tightened in 2011, requires all religious organizations to register with the state, mandates a minimum membership threshold, prohibits unregistered religious activity, and gives the authorities broad discretion to refuse registration. Protestant and evangelical congregations have been the primary targets of registration-related enforcement. Raids on unregistered prayer meetings, fines for participants, deportation of foreign missionaries, and detention of church leaders for questioning are documented patterns. Jehovah’s Witnesses have faced bans on their literature and significant fines.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~25%
- **Primary Concerns:** Registration requirements, raids on unregistered churches, deportation of foreign missionaries, surveillance
- **Current Status:** Registered denominations largely stable; unregistered churches face ongoing pressure
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Kazakhstan* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/kazakhstan/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- Forum 18 News Service, *Kazakhstan* — forum18.org/archive.php?country=29
- CIA World Factbook: Kazakhstan — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/kazakhstan/
- Human Rights Watch, *Kazakhstan* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/kazakhstan

COUNTRY: Kenya
TITLE: Kenya — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/kenya
META DESCRIPTION: Kenya is one of Africa’s most Christian nations, but Christians in the northeast — near the Somali border — face deadly Al-Shabaab attacks that have massacred bus passengers, university students, and church communities specifically because of their faith.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Kenya, East Africa, Christian Majority, Al-Shabaab, Terrorism, Coastal Attacks, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Kenya is approximately 85% Christian — one of the highest proportions in East Africa — with a diverse denominational landscape including Catholic, Anglican, Presbyterian, Pentecostal, and many independent evangelical and charismatic movements. In most of the country, Christians practice freely with no persecution. The significant and deadly exception is the northeastern region and the coast, where Al-Shabaab — the Somali Islamist militant group — has conducted a sustained campaign of terrorist attacks that has specifically targeted Christians. These attacks are explicitly religiously motivated: Al-Shabaab has repeatedly separated passengers on buses and at checkpoints into Muslims and non-Muslims, killing the non-Muslims.

## Al-Shabaab Violence Against Christians

Key documented attacks include: The April 2015 Garissa University massacre, in which Al-Shabaab militants seized the university campus, separated Christian from Muslim students, and killed 148 people, the vast majority of them Christians. In 2014, attackers stopped a bus in Mandera County, separated passengers by religion, and executed 28 non-Muslim passengers. In 2015, a second Mandera bus attack killed 28 Christian quarry workers. Churches in the coastal and northeastern regions have been bombed or attacked. The targeting is explicit: Al-Shabaab has asked victims to recite Islamic prayers as a survival test, executing those who cannot.

## Broader Context

The vast majority of Kenya’s Christians — in Nairobi, central Kenya, western Kenya, and most of the country — are entirely free. Kenya’s Christian community is active, growing, and globally mission-minded. The challenge is geographically concentrated but within that geography, deadly. Kenya sits in the middle tier of the Open Doors World Watch List specifically because of Al-Shabaab’s targeted anti-Christian violence.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~85%
- **Primary Threat:** Al-Shabaab terrorist attacks targeting Christians in northeastern Kenya and coast
- **Current Status:** Vast majority free; northeastern and coastal regions dangerous for Christians
- **Open Doors WWL:** Mid-tier
- **Notable:** Garissa University massacre (2015) — 148 killed, majority Christians; multiple bus attacks separating Christians for execution

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Kenya* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/kenya/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Kenya* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/kenya/
- CIA World Fatbook: Kenya — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/kenya/
- Human Rights Watch, *Kenya: Al-Shabaab Attacks* — hrw.org/africa/kenya
- Amnesty International, *Kenya* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/kenya/
- UN Security Council, *Somalia/Kenya Al-Shabaab reporting*

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Laos
TITLE: Laos — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/laos
META DESCRIPTION: Laos is a one-party communist state where only three Christian denominations are approved and all others are illegal. Ethnic minority Christians — particularly the Hmong and Khmu — face forced recantation, expulsion from villages, imprisonment, and systematic destruction of churches. The church grows in the face of all of it.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Laos, Southeast Asia, Communism, LPRP, Hmong, Ethnic Minority Christians, House Churches, WWL Top 25, Forced Recantation
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #26 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Laos is a small, landlocked, heavily forested Southeast Asian nation of approximately 7.5 million people governed by the Lao People’s Revolutionary Party (LPRP) — a Marxist-Leninist one-party state that has ruled since 1975. The official state religion is Theravada Buddhism, which is deeply intertwined with Lao ethnic and national identity. The government controls all religious life through the Lao Front for National Construction. Only three Christian bodies are officially recognized: the Lao Evangelical Church, the Seventh-day Adventist Church, and the Roman Catholic Church. All other Christian groups — independent evangelical congregations, charismatic house churches, Baptist missions, and many others — are illegal.

Christians represent approximately 3% of the Laotian population — roughly 225,000 people. The majority belong to ethnic minority communities: the Hmong, Khmu, and other highland tribal peoples who converted to Christianity through 20th-century missionary work and who now face the dual pressure of communist religious control and Lao majority ethnic nationalism. These communities face forced recantation demands, expulsion from villages, destruction of churches and Bibles, imprisonment of church leaders, and denial of access to basic services including burial rights. Despite all of this, the Christian community in Laos has grown steadily — from a small number at the time of the communist takeover in 1975 to an estimated 225,000 today.

## Missionary Roots and the Communist Revolution

Christian missionaries entered Laos in the 19th century. The most significant mission breakthrough came through work among the highland tribal peoples. By the time the Pathet Lao communist movement took control of the country in 1975, there were significant evangelical Christian communities among several highland ethnic groups. The communist government immediately classified these communities as politically suspect — the Hmong in particular had been heavily recruited by the CIA during the Secret War (1964–1973), and Hmong Christianity was associated in the government’s eyes with American imperialism.

## The Legal Framework of Restriction

The Lao government’s control of Christianity operates through several mechanisms: only the three approved denominations are legal; unregistered churches are illegal and subject to closure and prosecution; religious education of children is restricted; all sermons must be consistent with Party guidelines; and all religious literature must be approved before distribution.

In practice, these restrictions are enforced more harshly in rural areas where local officials have unchecked discretion.

## Forced Recantation and Village Expulsion

The most documented pattern of persecution in Laos is the forced recantation of Christians in ethnic minority villages. When a community member converts to Christianity, village authorities pressure the convert to renounce their faith and resume Buddhist practice. Those who refuse face escalating consequences: verbal demands, formal demands to sign recantation documents, and ultimately expulsion from the village — losing their homes, crops, livestock, and access to clean water. Children may be expelled from village schools.

Open Doors, Christian Solidarity Worldwide, and other monitoring organizations document dozens of individual village expulsion cases annually.

## Church Destruction and Bible Confiscation

In villages where Christian communities are identified, local authorities or mob action has destroyed church buildings and confiscated Bibles, hymnals, and Christian literature. In some documented cases, local officials have organized public burnings of confiscated Christian materials.

## Imprisonment of Church Leaders

Pastors and house church leaders face the risk of arrest on charges typically framed in political terms — “disturbing social order,” “spreading false doctrine,” “conducting illegal religious activities.” Prison conditions in Laos are harsh; prisoners report inadequate food, inadequate medical care, and isolation from family.

## The Lao Evangelical Church: Between Legitimacy and Compromise

The Lao Evangelical Church (LEC) — one of the three approved denominations — occupies a complex position. It is the primary legal vehicle for evangelical Christian expression in Laos and serves tens of thousands of registered members. But it operates under real constraints — it cannot advocate publicly for the rights of unregistered Christians and must maintain a posture of political loyalty.

## Growth Despite Persecution

The most remarkable fact about the Laotian church is that it has grown from perhaps 3,000 Christians at the time of the communist takeover in 1975 to an estimated 225,000 today — a 75-fold increase during a period of active persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #26
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~225,000 (~3%)
- **Primary Threats:** Communist one-party state control, only 3 denominations legal, village expulsions and forced recantation of ethnic minority Christians, church demolition, Bible confiscation, imprisonment of church leaders
- **Primary Affected Communities:** Hmong, Khmu, and other highland tribal Christians
- **Legal Status:** Christianity technically legal within approved denominational framework; all other practice illegal
- **Notable:** Church grew from ~3,000 (1975) to ~225,000 (present) — a 75-fold increase despite active persecution

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Laos* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/laos/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Laos* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/laos/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Laos* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Laos — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/laos/
- Human Rights Watch, *Laos country reports* — hrw.org/asia/laos
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Laos* — csw.org.uk
- Forum 18 News Service, *Laos monitoring* — forum18.org
- International Christian Concern, *Laos* — persecution.org/country/laos/

COUNTRY: Latvia
TITLE: Latvia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/latvia
META DESCRIPTION: Latvia is a historically Lutheran and Catholic Baltic nation with full religious freedom. Soviet occupation suppressed religious life severely; the church has been rebuilding for three decades. Christians face no persecution.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Latvia, Baltic, Lutheran, Catholic, Post-Soviet, Religious Freedom, Soviet Suppression
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Latvia is a small Baltic nation of approximately 1.8 million people with a historically divided Christian landscape: Lutheran in the north and center, Catholic in the southeast (Latgale region), and a Russian Orthodox community among the ethnic Russian minority. All denominations practice freely. Latvia restored independence in 1991 after fifty years of Soviet occupation, which suppressed religious life severely — closing churches, imprisoning clergy, and promoting atheism through state institutions. The post-Soviet church has been rebuilding steadily. Latvia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~55%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Soviet occupation severely suppressed religious life (1940–1941, 1944–1991); church rebuilding ongoing

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Latvia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/latvia/

- Pew Research Center, *Religious Belief and National Belonging in Central and Eastern Europe* (2017) — pewresearch.org/religion/2017/05/10/religious-belief-and-national-belonging-in-central-and-eastern-europe/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Lebanon
TITLE: Lebanon — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/lebanon
META DESCRIPTION: Lebanon is the Arab world’s most Christian country — home to the ancient Maronite Church and a mosaic of Christian communities who helped found the Lebanese state. Decades of war, Iranian-backed Hezbollah dominance, state collapse, and economic catastrophe have driven the Christian community to the edge of demographic crisis.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Lebanon, Middle East, Maronite, Hezbollah, Sectarianism, State Collapse, Christian Minority, Ancient Church, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Lebanon is unlike any other Arab country in its relationship to Christianity. Christians — predominantly Maronite Catholics, Greek Orthodox, Armenian Apostolic, Armenian Catholic, Greek Catholic (Melkite), and a variety of Protestant denominations — constitute approximately 35–40% of Lebanon’s population, making it the most Christian Arab nation in the world. The Lebanese state itself was constructed around Christian political identity: the 1943 National Pact allocated the presidency to a Maronite Christian. That architecture is under existential pressure. Christian emigration — driven by the 15-year civil war (1975–1990), repeated Israeli-Hezbollah conflicts, Syrian interference, and the catastrophic economic collapse that accelerated after 2019 — has been relentless. The Lebanese Christian community faces an uncertain demographic and institutional future in a country that is itself disintegrating.

## The Ancient Maronite Church

The Maronite Church traces its origins to St. Maron, a 4th-century Syrian monk whose followers settled in the mountains of Lebanon and developed a distinctly Lebanese Christian identity. When the Arab Islamic conquest swept through the Levant in the 7th century, the Maronites retreated to the mountains of northern Lebanon — where their rugged terrain gave them protection that their lowland neighbors lacked. For over a thousand years, the Lebanese mountains were a Christian refuge in a Muslim-majority region. The Maronite Church entered into full communion with Rome by the 12th century — making it the oldest Eastern Catholic church in continuous communion with Rome.

## The Lebanese Civil War and Christian Militias

Lebanon’s 15-year civil war (1975–1990) was one of the most complex and devastating conflicts in Middle Eastern history. For the Christian community, the war was both a period of intense self-defense and a period of moral catastrophe. In September 1982, Phalangist militiamen — with Israeli forces controlling the surrounding area — entered the Sabra and Shatila Palestinian refugee camps and massacred between 800 and 3,500 Palestinian civilians over three days. This massacre is one of the defining moral legacies of Lebanese Christian nationalism.

## Hezbollah, Syrian Influence, and Christian Marginalization

Hezbollah — the Iran-backed Shia Islamist organization and political party — has grown to become the most powerful military and political force in Lebanon. For ordinary Lebanese Christians, Hezbollah’s dominance means living in a country whose most powerful actor is funded by Iran and whose military adventures repeatedly expose Lebanon to devastating conflict.

## Economic Collapse and the Emigration Crisis

Lebanon’s economic collapse — which accelerated dramatically after 2019, with the lira losing over 90% of its value — has driven unprecedented emigration across all Lebanese communities. Educated Christians leave at the highest rates because they have the most international options. The August 2020 Beirut port explosion killed over 200 people, injured 6,000, and caused catastrophic damage to much of Beirut, including the historic Christian neighborhoods of Gemmayzeh and Mar Mikhael. The explosion — attributed to the criminal negligence of a political system dominated by sectarian interests — concentrated in its devastation everything wrong about Lebanon’s governance.

## Current Status

Lebanon’s Christian community today maintains its institutions but is demographically anxious and politically weakened. Christians are not persecuted by the Lebanese state. But the state barely functions, and in that vacuum, Hezbollah governs the south, the economy continues to collapse, and Lebanese Christians debate whether their country’s future can include them.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~35–40%
- **Primary Concerns:** Hezbollah dominance, economic collapse driving emigration, displacement from conflict, demographic decline
- **Current Status:** No state persecution; profound existential pressure from political collapse, economic catastrophe, and conflict
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; mid-tier
- **Historical Significance:** Most Christian Arab nation; Maronite Church in continuous communion with Rome since 12th century

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Lesotho
TITLE: Lesotho — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/lesotho
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Lesotho is a small, landlocked kingdom entirely surrounded by South Africa, with approximately 90% of its population identifying as Christian. The Catholic Church arrived through French missionaries (the Oblates of Mary Immaculate) in the 1860s at the invitation of King Moshoeshoe I. The Paris Evangelical Missionary Society established the Lesotho Evangelical Church around the same time. Both institutions remain deeply embedded in Lesotho’s social fabric, running the majority of the country’s schools and hospitals. Christianity is woven into the national identity; the country’s constitution declares it a Christian nation. There is no persecution; Lesotho is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; constitutionally Christian nation; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Lesotho* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/lesotho/
- CIA World Factbook: Lesotho — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/lesotho/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Liberia
TITLE: Liberia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/liberia
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Liberia is approximately 85% Christian — with Baptist, Methodist, Pentecostal, and Catholic communities all significant — and has a unique founding story in the Christian world. Established in 1822 by the American Colonization Society as a settlement for freed American slaves, Liberia was built by people who carried their American Protestant Christian faith back to Africa. The republic these settlers founded in 1847 was explicitly Christian in its cultural orientation. The relationship between the Americo-Liberian settler population and the indigenous African population was historically hierarchical and at times exploitative — a painful irony given the settlers’ own experience of slavery. This tension contributed to the conditions that produced two devastating civil wars (1989–1997, 1999–2003) that killed approximately 250,000 people. The church was a significant institution of survival and reconstruction during the war years. Liberia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List; Christians practice freely.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~85%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Liberia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/liberia/
- CIA World Factbook: Liberia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/liberia/
