# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Libya
TITLE: Libya — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/libya
META DESCRIPTION: Libya has no indigenous Christian community and no functioning state. Since Gaddafi’s fall in 2011, the country has been a theater of militia warfare in which Christians — almost entirely sub-Saharan African migrants — have been enslaved, tortured, and killed. ISIS beheaded 21 Coptic Christian migrants on a Libyan beach in 2015.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Libya, North Africa, Militia Violence, ISIS, 21 Martyrs, Coptic Christians, Migrant Persecution, WWL Top 25, State Collapse
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #27 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Libya has no indigenous Christian community and no functioning state. Since the fall of Muammar Gaddafi in 2011, the country has been a theater of militia warfare in which Christians — almost entirely sub-Saharan African migrants — have been enslaved, tortured, and killed. Libya ranked #27 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List not because of ongoing persecution of an indigenous Christian population (there is none) but because of the catastrophic conditions facing the hundreds of thousands of Christian migrants who transit or are trapped in Libya annually.

## Ancient Christianity and Its Disappearance

The region of modern Libya was thoroughly Christianized during the Roman period. Cyrene is mentioned in the New Testament: Simon of Cyrene carried Jesus’s cross to Golgotha (Mark 15:21). The church in Cyrenaica was significant enough to produce bishops who attended the great councils. The Arab Islamic conquest was swift — completed by 647 AD — and the Christian community did not survive it as it survived in Egypt or Lebanon.

## State Collapse and the Migrant Crisis

The NATO-backed uprising that killed Gaddafi in October 2011 removed the one institution that had maintained central authority in Libya. Criminal networks — particularly human trafficking networks moving sub-Saharan African migrants northward — established themselves with near-total impunity. Sub-Saharan African migrants — predominantly from Nigeria, Chad, Niger, Eritrea, Ethiopia, Sudan, and West African countries — flow through Libya in hundreds of thousands. The majority are Christian. What they encounter in Libya is a predatory system: detention by militias, forced labor, torture, sexual violence, and in some cases execution. The UN has documented these abuses extensively.

## The 21 Martyrs of Libya

On February 15, 2015, the Islamic State’s Libya affiliate released a video showing 21 Coptic Egyptian men in orange jumpsuits being marched along a beach near Sirte, Libya. They had come to Libya as migrant workers seeking employment to support their families. As the blades fell, witnesses reported hearing the men cry out “Ya Rabbi Yasua” — “Lord Jesus Christ” — in their final breath. One of the 21 — Matthew Ayairga, a Ghanaian migrant — reportedly declared himself a Christian after witnessing the faith of the Egyptian men with whom he was imprisoned, and was killed alongside them. All 21 were canonized by the Coptic Orthodox Church as the “Martyrs of Libya.”

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #27
- **Indigenous Christian Population:** Effectively zero
- **Affected Population:** Sub-Saharan African Christian migrants in the hundreds of thousands annually
- **Primary Threats:** Militia violence, human trafficking, torture, killing of Christians in captivity
- **Notable:** 21 Coptic Egyptian martyrs beheaded by ISIS, February 15, 2015 — canonized by the Coptic Church; one Ghanaian migrant embraced Christianity and died alongside them

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Libya* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/libya/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Libya* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/libya/
- CIA World Factbook: Libya — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/libya/
- UN Human Rights Council, *Report on Libya* — ohchr.org
- Human Rights Watch, *Libya: Abuses Against Migrants* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/libya
- Amnesty International, *Libya* — amnesty.org/en/countries/middle-east-and-north-africa/libya/
- International Organization for Migration (IOM), *Libya* — iom.int
- UN Support Mission in Libya (UNSMIL) — unsmil.unmissions.org

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Lithuania
TITLE: Lithuania — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/lithuania
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Lithuania is approximately 77% Roman Catholic — the most Catholic of the three Baltic states — and has full constitutional religious freedom. Christianity arrived in the region in 1387 through the conversion of Grand Duke Jogaila, making Lithuania the last pagan nation in Europe to be Christianized. Soviet repression of the Lithuanian Catholic Church was severe. The response was remarkable: beginning in 1972, an underground publication called The Chronicle of the Catholic Church in Lithuania documented religious persecution for 17 years — 81 issues — produced by priests and laypeople who faced imprisonment for their participation. It was one of the most significant acts of religious civil disobedience of the Cold War era. Since independence in 1990, Lithuania has full religious freedom. Lithuania is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~80% (predominantly Catholic)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Last pagan nation in Europe to convert (1387); Chronicle of the Catholic Church in Lithuania (1972–1989) — major act of religious civil disobedience under Soviet rule

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Lithuania* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/lithuania/
- CIA World Factbook: Lithuania — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/lithuania/
- Pew Research Center, *Religious Belief and National Belonging in Central and Eastern Europe* (2017) — pewresearch.org/religion/2017/05/10/religious-belief-and-national-belonging-in-central-and-eastern-europe/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Madagascar
TITLE: Madagascar — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/madagascar
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Madagascar is a large island nation in the Indian Ocean with approximately 65% of the population identifying as Christian (Catholic and Protestant in roughly equal measure). Christianity arrived primarily through the London Missionary Society in the early 19th century. Queen Ranavalona I expelled missionaries and executed converts in the 1830s–1850s — a short-lived period of anti-Christian persecution that produced the first Malagasy Christian martyrs, canonized by the Catholic Church in 1989. After Ranavalona’s death in 1861, Christianity was fully restored and spread rapidly. Today, Madagascar’s Christian community practices freely. Madagascar is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~65%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Malawi
TITLE: Malawi — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/malawi
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Malawi is a small, landlocked East-Central African nation of approximately 20 million people, of whom approximately 82% identify as Christian. The Presbyterian Church of Central Africa (CCAP) — established through the Livingstonia and Blantyre missions founded by David Livingstone’s legacy — and the Catholic Church are the largest denominations, alongside growing evangelical and Pentecostal communities. Livingstone’s witness catalyzed the missionary movement that established the Christian institutions that built modern Malawi. Christians practice freely; Malawi is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Malaysia
TITLE: Malaysia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/malaysia
META DESCRIPTION: Malaysia is a Muslim-majority constitutional monarchy where Christians face systematic discrimination: bans on the word “Allah” for non-Muslims, restrictions on church construction, deportation of pastors, and severe legal consequences for Muslim converts to Christianity.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Malaysia, Southeast Asia, Islam, Church Restrictions, Allah Ban, Converts, Bumiputera, Church Burning, WWL Top 40
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Malaysia is a constitutional monarchy with a Muslim-majority population — approximately 63% Muslim — in which Islam is the state religion and a parallel dual-track legal system operates: civil law for all Malaysians and Sharia law for Muslim personal and family matters. Christians represent approximately 9–10% of the population — roughly 3 million people — concentrated primarily among the Chinese and Indian ethnic minorities and among the indigenous peoples of Sabah and Sarawak in Malaysian Borneo.

Christians in Malaysia are legal and have churches and active community life. But they operate within a framework that systematically disadvantages them relative to the Muslim majority, uses legal mechanisms to restrict church activity, penalizes conversion from Islam with devastating social and legal consequences, and has tolerated organized Islamist pressure — including church arson.

## The Allah Controversy

The word “Allah” — used by Arab-speaking Christians for centuries and by Malay-speaking Christians in their Bible translations — was banned from use by non-Muslims in Malaysia. The Malaysian Bible (Al-Kitab) uses “Allah” throughout; the ban created a situation in which the government could seize and stamp Bibles imported for Christian use. The Federal Court upheld the ban. The practical effect: Bibles confiscated, Christian literature seized, churches unable to use the standard Malay word for God.

## Church Restrictions and Arson

In January 2010, following a court ruling in favor of the Herald newspaper’s use of “Allah,” eleven churches were attacked in four days, several firebombed. Organized Islamist groups publicly threatened violence before the attacks occurred. The government condemned the attacks, but the organizations that organized the threatening demonstrations faced no legal consequences.

## Converts and the Sharia System

Malaysia’s dual legal system creates severe consequences for Muslims who convert to Christianity. A Muslim who converts faces a legal labyrinth from which exit is effectively blocked in most states. For ethnic Malays — who are constitutionally defined as Muslim — conversion triggers loss of Bumiputera affirmative action benefits in education, employment, housing, and business.

## Sabah and Sarawak

The states of Sabah and Sarawak in Malaysian Borneo have indigenous populations that are predominantly Christian and have historically been more resistant to Islamization pressures from peninsular Malaysia. The Allah ban is acutely felt in communities where “Allah” is simply the Malay word for God in their Christian heritage.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~9–10% (~3 million)
- **Primary Threats:** Allah ban, church construction barriers, Sharia system for converts, deportation of foreign pastors, organized Islamist pressure
- **Open Doors WWL:** Mid-tier ranked
- **Notable:** 2010 church arson wave; Al-Kitab Bible seized and stamped; Sabah and Sarawak indigenous Christians maintain stronger position

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Malaysia* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/malaysia/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Malaysia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/malaysia/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Malaysia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/malaysia/
- Human Rights Watch, *Malaysia country reports* — hrw.org/asia/malaysia
- Amnesty International, *Malaysia* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/malaysia/

COUNTRY: Maldives
TITLE: Maldives — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/maldives
META DESCRIPTION: The Maldives is a 100% Muslim island nation where Christianity is effectively nonexistent and illegal. Any Maldivian who converts faces loss of citizenship.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Maldives, Indian Ocean, Islam, Apostasy, No Christians, WWL Top 10, Citizenship Loss
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #10 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

The Maldives — a chain of coral island atolls in the Indian Ocean, with a population of approximately 530,000 — is one of the most religiously uniform nations on earth. The constitution declares Islam the state religion, requires all citizens to be Muslim, and links citizenship itself to Islamic faith. There are no indigenous Maldivian Christians. There are no churches. A Maldivian who converts from Islam to Christianity faces the formal loss of their citizenship, rendering them stateless in their own country. The Maldives ranked #10 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List. The Maldives converted to Islam in 1153 AD; since then, Islam has been the exclusive religion of the Maldivian people — an unbroken 870-year religious history. Open Doors believes a small number of Maldivian nationals have converted to Christianity — the number is believed to be in the dozens to low hundreds. These individuals exist in extreme isolation.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #10
- **Indigenous Christian Population:** Effectively zero; possibly dozens of secret converts
- **Legal Framework:** Constitution requires all citizens to be Muslim; conversion means loss of citizenship
- **Churches:** Zero

- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Mali
TITLE: Mali — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/mali
META DESCRIPTION: Mali’s jihadist insurgency has made it one of West Africa’s most dangerous countries for Christians. Churches have been burned, pastors beheaded, and entire Christian communities displaced from the north and center of the country.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Mali, West Africa, Sahel, JNIM, Jihadism, Church Attacks, Pastoral Martyrdom, WWL Top 25, State Collapse
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #20 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Mali is a vast, landlocked West African nation of approximately 23 million people. Approximately 90% of Malians are Muslim; Christians represent roughly 5%, concentrated almost entirely in the south. For decades, Mali’s predominantly southern Christian community existed in relative tolerance within the broader Muslim-majority society. Since 2012, a jihadist insurgency — dominated by JNIM (Jama’at Nusrat al-Islam wal-Muslimin), al-Qaeda’s regional affiliate — has spread from the north through the center and is pushing into the south. JNIM has conducted systematic campaigns against Christian communities: burning churches, beheading pastors, massacring Christian villages, and demanding that communities choose between conversion and death. Mali ranked #20 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List and is trending worse.

## The Jihadist Insurgency and Its Assault on Christians

The pattern is the same as in Burkina Faso: militants enter a village, identify Christian residents or leaders, and conduct executions designed to eliminate Christian presence and terrorize remaining communities. Pastors are among the first targeted. Churches are burned as deliberate acts of religious erasure. Documented attacks include the killing of multiple pastors in the Mopti and Ségou regions since 2019, the burning of dozens of churches, and the forced displacement of tens of thousands of Christians from central and southern Mali.

## Government Response

Mali’s government has undergone two military coups (2020 and 2021). The junta expelled French forces and turned to the Wagner Group for security assistance. The military government’s capacity to protect civilian communities remains deeply limited.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #20
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~5% (~1.1 million)
- **Primary Threats:** JNIM and ISIS-affiliated jihadist groups; church burnings, pastoral assassinations, village massacres, forced displacement
- **Notable:** Dozens of churches burned since 2019; multiple pastors beheaded; tens of thousands displaced

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Mali* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/mali/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Mali* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/mali/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Mali — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/mali/

- International Christian Concern, *Mali* — persecution.org/country/mali/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Mauritania
TITLE: Mauritania — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/mauritania
META DESCRIPTION: Mauritania is a 100% Muslim nation in West Africa where apostasy carries the death penalty, Christianity is effectively nonexistent, and even private possession of non-Islamic religious materials can result in arrest.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Mauritania, West Africa, Islam, Apostasy Death Penalty, No Christians, WWL Top 25, Converts
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #24 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Mauritania is a vast, sparsely populated Saharan and Sahelian nation of approximately 4.7 million people and one of the most closed nations on earth to Christian faith. The constitution declares Mauritania an Islamic republic; all citizens are Muslim by law; apostasy is punishable by death under the Mauritanian Penal Code revised in 2017; proselytism is a criminal offense; and Christian literature — including Bibles — is illegal to import or possess in quantity. There are no public churches, no registered Christian organizations, and no indigenous Mauritanian Christian community of any visible scale. Mauritania ranked #24 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

The 2014 case of blogger Mohamed Cheikh Ould Mkhaitir — sentenced to death for apostasy — drew significant international attention. He was held for years and eventually released in 2019 under international pressure, illustrating both the existence of the death penalty and the political pressure that prevents its consistent application.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #24
- **Indigenous Christian Population:** Effectively zero
- **Legal Framework:** Apostasy death penalty codified in 2017; proselytism criminal; Christian literature illegal
- **Churches:** Zero
- **Notable:** Mohamed Cheikh Ould Mkhaitir — sentenced to death for apostasy (2014); released 2019

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Mexico
TITLE: Mexico — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/mexico
META DESCRIPTION: Mexico is overwhelmingly Christian, but indigenous evangelical converts in Chiapas and Oaxaca face expulsion from communities enforcing traditional Catholic-animist customs, while pastors across the country face targeted violence from drug cartels.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Mexico, North America, Catholic, Evangelical, Cartel Violence, Indigenous Persecution, Chiapas, WWL Top 50
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Mexico is approximately 83% Christian — roughly 68% Catholic and 15% evangelical Protestant — and one of the most Christian countries in North America. The state guarantees religious freedom, and no national-level government persecution exists. Yet Mexico appears on the Open Doors World Watch List because Christians face two distinct and documented threats: targeted cartel and criminal organization violence against pastors who challenge criminal authority in their communities; and the persecution of indigenous evangelical converts by community authorities who enforce traditional religious customs — a syncretic blend of Catholic and pre-Columbian spiritual practice — and expel converts from their villages, confiscate their property, and sometimes physically attack them.

## Christianity in Mexico

Spanish colonization brought Catholicism with enormous force from 1519 onward. The Virgin of Guadalupe — whose apparition to the indigenous peasant Juan Diego in 1531 created one of the most important Marian traditions in the world — reflects the complex synthesis of Catholic and indigenous religious identity that developed. The Cristero War (1926–1929), in which Mexican Catholics took up arms against anti-clerical provisions of the revolutionary constitution, produced canonized martyrs including Blessed Miguel Pro S.J., executed by firing squad in 1927.

## Indigenous Community Persecution

The most distinctive form of Christian persecution in Mexico is the expulsion of evangelical converts from indigenous communities in Chiapas, Oaxaca, and other southern states. The usos y costumbres governance system enforces traditional religious customs. Evangelical converts who refuse to participate in traditional festivals face escalating sanctions: fines, formal demands to sign recantation documents, and ultimately expulsion from the village — losing their homes, land, and their children’s enrollment in village schools. The state of Chiapas has seen thousands of evangelical converts expelled from their communities across decades.

## Cartel Violence Against Pastors

Mexico’s drug trafficking organizations treat organized community leadership that challenges their authority as a threat. Pastors who preach against drug trafficking, run rehabilitation programs, or provide community moral authority in cartel-controlled areas are identified as threats and targeted. Mexico’s pastor-killing rate is among the highest in Latin America.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~83%
- **Primary Threats:** Cartel violence against pastors; indigenous community expulsion of evangelical converts in Chiapas and Oaxaca
- **Current Status:** Full national religious freedom; regional and community-level persecution serious and documented
- **Open Doors WWL:** Lower tier ranked

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Mexico* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/mexico/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Mexico* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/mexico/
- CIA World Factbook: Mexico — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/mexico/
- Human Rights Watch, *Mexico country reports* — hrw.org/americas/mexico
- International Christian Concern, *Mexico* — persecution.org/country/mexico/
- Pew Research Center, *Religion in Latin America* (2014) — pewresearch.org/religion/2014/11/13/religion-in-latin-america/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Moldova
TITLE: Moldova — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/moldova
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Moldova is a small, landlocked Eastern European nation of approximately 2.6 million people — the poorest country in Europe by GDP per capita — where approximately 90% of the population identifies as Eastern Orthodox Christian. The intra-Orthodox jurisdictional dispute between the Moscow Patriarchate and the Romanian Orthodox Church reflects political and national identity tensions rather than religious persecution. Protestant and evangelical communities that grew after 1991 have faced some bureaucratic friction in registration and social hostility in rural areas, but these are not systematic persecution issues. Moldova is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~93% (predominantly Orthodox)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Moldova* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/moldova/
- CIA World Factbook: Moldova — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/moldova/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Mongolia
TITLE: Mongolia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/mongolia
META DESCRIPTION: Mongolia had virtually no Christians in 1990. Today it has approximately 60,000. The church grew from nothing after communism fell, and now navigates a government increasingly concerned about “foreign religious influence.”
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Mongolia, East Asia, Buddhism, Shamanism, Church Growth, Post-Communist, Foreign Religion, Missionary, Registration
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Mongolia is one of the most remarkable stories of modern Christian growth in the world. In 1990, Mongolia had an estimated zero to a few dozen indigenous Christians. By 2023, Mongolia is estimated to have approximately 60,000 Christians — almost all first- or second-generation converts, the children of a church that did not exist a generation ago. The church grew from nothing through the work of foreign missionaries who entered Mongolia in the early 1990s and found a population spiritually depleted by communism and open to something new. Today, Mongolia’s Christian community navigates a government increasingly concerned about “foreign religious influence” and tightening registration requirements. Mongolia sits in the moderate tier of monitoring concern.

## The Post-Communist Opening

Mongolia’s peaceful democratic revolution in 1990 opened the country to the outside world. South Korean missionaries came in the largest numbers, finding a population that had lost its traditional religious framework through communist suppression — the monastic Buddhist institutions had been destroyed; an estimated 30,000 Buddhist monks were killed in communist purges of the 1930s.

## Government Religious Control and Indigenization

Mongolia’s Religion Law, revised multiple times and significantly tightened in 2020, requires all religious organizations to register with the government. A 2020 Law on Combating Foreign Influence gave the government expanded powers to monitor and restrict foreign-funded religious activity. Foreign missionaries require special visas that are increasingly difficult to obtain; several have been deported in recent years. The Bible was translated into modern Mongolian in 1990 — almost exactly when the country opened. Mongolian Christian worship has developed its own musical idiom; Mongolian churches are sending their own missionaries. This indigenization is the church’s most important long-term asset.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~60,000 (~1.8%)
- **Primary Threats:** Registration requirements, restrictions on foreign missionaries, Buddhist nationalist pressure, government monitoring of foreign-funded organizations
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; moderate tier
- **Growth Story:** From effectively zero Christians in 1990 to 60,000 by 2023

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Mongolia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/mongolia/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Mongolia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/mongolia/
- Joshua Project, *Mongolia* — joshuaproject.net
- Open Doors USA, *Mongolia Country Profile* — opendoorsusa.org

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Morocco
TITLE: Morocco — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/morocco
META DESCRIPTION: Morocco has no legal indigenous Christian community. Moroccan Muslims who convert face severe social and legal consequences. Foreigners can practice privately, but any outreach to Moroccans is criminal. The church exists only underground — and it is growing.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Morocco, North Africa, Islam, Converts, Underground Church, Apostasy, Church Closure, WWL Top 25, Proselytism Ban
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #28 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Morocco is a constitutional monarchy in which Islam is the state religion, the king bears the title “Commander of the Faithful,” and religious identity is so thoroughly intertwined with national law and social expectation that to be Moroccan is, by legal and social default, to be Muslim. Christianity has no legal indigenous presence in Morocco. The small number of Moroccan nationals who have come to faith in Christ — believed to number between 2,000 and 8,000 — practice in absolute secrecy, with no churches, no community structures they can maintain openly, no legal protection from the consequences of their faith being discovered. Morocco ranked #28 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Ancient Christianity and Its Disappearance

The region of modern Morocco was part of the Roman province of Mauretania Tingitana, and Christianity was present from the early centuries of the church. The martyrs of Tangier — killed under Diocletian in the 3rd century — are among the early North African church’s honored dead. By the Fatimid period, organized Christianity in Morocco had ceased to exist. French colonial rule (1912–1956) brought back a small Catholic presence for European settlers; at independence it departed with them.

## The Legal Framework Against Conversion

Article 220 of the Moroccan Penal Code criminalizes evangelism directed at Muslims. Proselytism toward Moroccan nationals carries penalties including imprisonment and fines. Foreigners who engage in outreach to Moroccan nationals are arrested, detained, and expelled. Moroccan converts cannot legally change their religion on their national identity documents; they continue to be classified as Muslim for all legal purposes including marriage, divorce, inheritance, and child custody.

## The Underground Church and Its Growth

The Moroccan underground church is small, scattered, and operating under extreme pressure, but it is real and — by the testimony of those who have left the country — growing. Communities of Moroccan believers meet in small groups in private homes. No records are kept that could identify members. The growth of the underground church is connected to satellite television broadcasting of Christian content in Arabic and Moroccan Arabic (Darija), the internet and social media, and the testimony of Moroccan workers who encountered Christianity while in Europe and returned with their faith.

Morocco’s Amazigh (Berber) communities — who have a distinct ethnic identity from Arab Moroccans — have been among those most receptive to Christianity in some reports, for reasons connected to their distinct cultural identity from Arab-Islamic identity politics.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #28
- **Estimated Indigenous Christian Population:** 2,000–8,000 (all secret believers; unverifiable)
- **Primary Threats:** Criminalization of proselytism, apostasy consequences (family rejection, legal jeopardy, loss of civil status), deportation of foreign missionaries
- **Legal Status:** Article 220 Penal Code criminalizes evangelism; apostasy not explicitly criminal but devastating in practice
- **Churches:** None for Moroccan nationals; expatriate churches exist for foreign nationals only
- **Historical Note:** North Africa was once the intellectual heartland of Christianity (Tertullian, Cyprian, Augustine’s tradition); today has essentially zero indigenous Christian presence

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Morocco* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/morocco/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Morocco* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/morocco/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Morocco — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/morocco/
- Human Rights Watch, *Morocco country reports* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/morocco
- Amnesty International, *Morocco* — amnesty.org/en/countries/middle-east-and-north-africa/morocco/
- Jenkins, Philip, *The Lost History of Christianity* (HarperOne, 2008) — for ancient North African church history

COUNTRY: Mozambique
TITLE: Mozambique — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/mozambique
META DESCRIPTION: Mozambique is a majority-Christian nation, but its northern Cabo Delgado province has become one of Africa’s most dangerous jihadist conflict zones. An ISIS-affiliated insurgency has burned dozens of churches, killed hundreds of Christians, and displaced hundreds of thousands.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Mozambique, Southern Africa, Jihadism, ISCAP, ISIS, Cabo Delgado, Church Attacks, WWL Top 50, Christian Majority
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Mozambique is approximately 57% Christian — the majority belonging to Zionist and Apostolic African Independent Churches, Catholic, and Protestant denominations — with approximately 18% Muslim and the remainder following traditional religions. In the south and center of the country, Christians practice freely. But in Cabo Delgado — the remote, gas-rich northern province bordering Tanzania — an Islamic State-affiliated insurgency that local people call Al-Shabaab (unrelated to the Somali group) has since 2017 conducted a sustained campaign of violence that has killed thousands, displaced over a million people, and specifically targeted Christian communities and churches.

## The Cabo Delgado Crisis

The insurgency began in 2017 when a group known as Ansar al-Sunna Wa-Jamma attacked a police station. The group has since been formally affiliated with the Islamic State (as ISCAP, Islamic State Central Africa Province) and has conducted a sustained campaign of atrocity. For Christian communities in Cabo Delgado, the violence has been explicitly religiously targeted. Churches have been burned — dozens in the affected region. Pastors have been killed. Christians have been beheaded in public executions. In some cases, militants have entered villages, separated Christians from Muslims, and executed the Christians.

The fall of the port town of Mocímboa da Praia to the insurgents in 2020 was a major escalation. Rwandan military forces and Mozambican army operations drove the insurgents from Mocímboa da Praia in 2021, but the insurgency dispersed into the interior and has continued its attacks. An estimated 1 million people have been displaced from Cabo Delgado.

## Current Status

Mozambique sits in the lower tier of the Open Doors World Watch List. The overwhelming majority of Mozambican Christians worship freely and are not under threat. The Cabo Delgado crisis is geographically concentrated but catastrophic within its geography, and the pattern of jihadist southward expansion — seen in Mali, Burkina Faso, and now Mozambique — gives grounds for serious concern about what the next decade brings.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~57%
- **Primary Threat:** ISIS-affiliated ISCAP insurgency in Cabo Delgado — church burnings, pastoral killings, village massacres, Christian displacement
- **Displacement:** ~1 million from Cabo Delgado region
- **Current Status:** South and center fully free; Cabo Delgado in acute crisis
- **Open Doors WWL:** Lower tier ranked

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Mozambique* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/mozambique/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Mozambique* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/mozambique/
- CIA World Factbook: Mozambique — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/mozambique/
- UN OCHA, *Mozambique Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/mozambique
- Human Rights Watch, *Mozambique: Cabo Delgado* — hrw.org/africa/mozambique
- Amnesty International, *Mozambique* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/mozambique/
- International Christian Concern, *Mozambique* — persecution.org/country/mozambique/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Myanmar (Burma)
TITLE: Myanmar — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/myanmar
META DESCRIPTION: Myanmar’s military junta has bombed churches, burned Christian villages, raped Christian women as a weapon of war, and killed pastors across predominantly Christian ethnic minority regions. The 2021 coup transformed the country into a war zone — and the Christian communities of the Chin, Kachin, Karen, and Kayah states are paying in blood.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Myanmar, Burma, Southeast Asia, Military Junta, Tatmadaw, Chin Christians, Kachin, Karen, Kayah, WWL Top 15, Church Bombing, Ethnic Persecution
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #13 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Myanmar is a nation of profound ethnic diversity whose majority Bamar Buddhist population has never fully accepted the existence of ethnic minority peoples who maintain distinct identities, languages, cultures — and, for several of the largest minorities, Christian faith. The Chin, Kachin, Karen (Kayin), Kayah (Karenni), and Zomi peoples are predominantly Christian, converted through 19th and 20th-century Baptist and Catholic missionary work. Since the military coup of February 1, 2021 — which removed the civilian government of Aung San Suu Kyi — Myanmar has been in a state of multi-front civil war, and the military has conducted systematic atrocities against civilian populations in these Christian ethnic minority regions: burning churches, bombing villages, killing pastors, and using sexual violence against women and girls as a deliberate weapon of war. Myanmar ranked #13 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## The Ancient Majority and Its Religious Identity

The Bamar — the dominant ethnic group, approximately 65–70% of the population — are Theravada Buddhist in a way that integrates spirituality, ethnicity, and national identity as thoroughly as any society in the world. The ethnic minority peoples of the country’s periphery — the hills and jungle regions of the Chin, Kachin, Karen, Kayah, and Shan states — were never fully incorporated into this Bamar Buddhist world.

## American Baptist Missions and the Christian Minorities

Adoniram Judson, an American Baptist missionary, arrived in Burma in 1813 and worked primarily among the Burman population with limited success. His breakthrough came among the Karen people — who had a prophetic tradition some interpreted as anticipating the arrival of a teacher bearing a sacred text. Karen converts became fervent evangelists among their own people; through the 19th and early 20th centuries, the Karen, Kachin, Chin, Kayah, and Zomi peoples converted to Christianity in large numbers. By Myanmar’s independence in 1948, the Christian minorities had a distinctive identity: literate (missionaries had created written scripts for previously unwritten languages and translated the Bible), educated, and organized around churches that served as community institutions.

## Independence, Military Rule, and Ethnic Conflict

Burma’s independence was followed by almost immediate ethnic conflict. The Tatmadaw — the military — became the institution most committed to Bamar Buddhist national unity and most hostile to ethnic minority self-determination. Decades of low-intensity warfare in the ethnic minority areas continued without resolution. Peace agreements have been partial, temporary, or unimplemented.

## The 2021 Coup and Escalation

The February 2021 coup triggered a national uprising. The civil war that followed has meant, for Christian ethnic minority areas:

**Church bombings:** The Tatmadaw has bombed churches in Chin, Kachin, Karen, and Kayah states. The Cathedral of the Immaculate Conception in Mindat, Chin State, was burned. Dozens of churches across the Christian minority regions have been bombed, burned, or shelled. In some cases, civilians who had taken refuge inside churches were killed in airstrikes.

**Village burnings:** Military forces have systematically burned villages in Chin, Kayah, and Karen states. Entire Christian communities have been driven from their homes. Some villages have been burned multiple times.

**Killing of pastors and religious leaders:** Multiple pastors have been killed by the Tatmadaw — arrested, tortured, and executed, or killed in airstrikes and ground assaults. Christian community leaders are specifically identified as threats because they are trusted authorities in communities the military is trying to control.

**Sexual violence:** The Tatmadaw’s use of rape and sexual violence as a weapon of war against ethnic minority women has continued and intensified since 2021. Reports from Chin, Kayah, and Karen states describe gang rape by soldiers used to terrorize communities into displacement. This is systematic, not incidental.

**Displacement:** Over 2 million people have been internally displaced in Myanmar since the 2021 coup. A significant proportion are from Christian ethnic minority communities.

## The Church Under Fire

The Myanmar church — particularly the Baptist conventions that serve the Chin, Kachin, Karen, and other communities — is one of the most deeply tried Christian communities in the world today. Pastors who have stayed with their congregations face the real possibility of arrest, torture, and death. Some have been killed. The Kachin Baptist Convention, the Karen Baptist Convention, and the Chin Christian organizations maintain contact with international partners and advocate for their communities’ survival.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #13
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~6–9% of 54 million (~3–5 million, concentrated in ethnic minority regions)
- **Primary Threats:** Military (Tatmadaw) airstrikes on churches, village burnings, killing of pastors, sexual violence as a weapon of war, mass displacement
- **Most Affected Communities:** Chin (predominantly Christian), Kachin (~85% Christian), Karen and Kayah (significant Christian populations)
- **Displacement:** 2+ million since 2021 coup; significant proportion from Christian minority communities
- **Historical Foundation:** American Baptist missionary Adoniram Judson arrived 1813; Karenic, Chin, Kachin peoples converted in 19th–20th centuries through indigenous evangelism following missionary education

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Myanmar* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/myanmar/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Burma* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/burma/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Myanmar* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Burma — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/burma/
- Human Rights Watch, *Burma/Myanmar country reports* — hrw.org/asia/burma-myanmar
- Amnesty International, *Myanmar* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/myanmar/
- UN Human Rights Office, *Myanmar reporting* — ohchr.org
- UN OCHA, *Myanmar Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/myanmar
- Kachin Baptist Convention — kbc.org.mm
- International Christian Concern, *Myanmar* — persecution.org/country/myanmar/
- Judson, Edward, *The Life of Adoniram Judson* (Anson D.F. Randolph and Company, 1883)

COUNTRY: Namibia
TITLE: Namibia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/namibia
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Namibia is approximately 90% Christian — with the Evangelical Lutheran Church in Namibia (ELCIN) the largest single denomination, followed by Catholic, Dutch Reformed, Anglican, and growing evangelical and Pentecostal communities. The country has full constitutional religious freedom; Christians practice without restriction or persecution. German colonization (1884–1915) brought Lutheran missions and brutal colonial violence — the Herero and Nama genocides of 1904–1908 killed an estimated 65,000–80,000 indigenous people in what historians recognize as one of the 20th century’s first genocides. The Rhenish Missionary Society established the foundation of Namibian Lutheranism; the church that grew from their work became a voice of conscience and resistance during the subsequent South African administration period. Namibia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~90%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Lutheran church central to independence movement; German colonial genocide (1904–1908) against Herero and Nama peoples is a defining national trauma

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Namibia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/namibia/
- CIA World Factbook: Namibia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/namibia/

COUNTRY: Nepal
TITLE: Nepal — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/nepal
META DESCRIPTION: Nepal was the world’s only Hindu kingdom until 2008. Today it is a secular state — but its new constitution criminalizes conversion and proselytism, Hindu nationalism is rising, and Christians face arrests, imprisonment, and violent attacks in a country where the church has grown from almost nothing to over half a million believers in two generations.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Nepal, South Asia, Hindu Nationalism, Anti-Conversion Law, Persecution, House Church, WWL Top 30, Converts, Secular State
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #27 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Nepal occupies a unique position in the global Christian story: until 2008, it was the world’s only constitutionally Hindu kingdom — a nation that formally defined itself as Hindu and had criminalized conversion away from Hinduism for most of its modern history. The democratic revolution that ended the monarchy in 2008 and established a federal democratic republic brought the formal secularization of the state — but also, paradoxically, a constitutional provision that criminalizes religious conversion more explicitly than the old monarchy’s restrictions. Article 26 of Nepal’s 2015 constitution prohibits “converting a person from one religion to another” and bans “disturbing the religion of others” — language broad enough to encompass ordinary Christian witness and that is being actively used to arrest and imprison pastors, Bible distributors, and converts. Nepal ranked #27 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

Nepal’s Christian community — which numbered virtually zero in 1950 and has grown to an estimated 500,000–800,000 today — is one of the most remarkable stories of modern church growth in Asia. It has grown despite the legal framework, not because of it.

## From Hindu Kingdom to Secular State

Nepal was sealed to outside religious influence for much of its modern history. The political upheaval of 1990 brought Nepal’s first democratic constitution and relaxed some restrictions, though proselytism remained illegal. The Maoist insurgency (1996–2006) and the peace process led to the abolition of the monarchy in 2008. The Christian community, which had grown cautiously during the 1990s and 2000s, had hoped that secularism would bring genuine religious freedom.

## The 2015 Constitution’s Conversion Ban

The 2015 constitution disappointed those hopes. Article 26(3) prohibits converting another person from one religion to another. The implementing legislation criminalizes conversion with penalties of up to five years in prison for the person who facilitates conversion and up to two years for the person who converts. This makes Nepal’s anti-conversion law among the most explicit in the world. Pastors have been arrested for baptizing converts. Bible distributors have been arrested for possessing and distributing scripture. Christians have been arrested for sharing their faith with neighbors or relatives.

## Hindu Nationalist Violence

Nepal’s Christian community faces organized violence from Hindu nationalist groups — particularly the Vishwa Hindu Parishad (VHP), which operates across the open border with India. Houses of worship have been vandalized. Prayer meetings have been disrupted by mobs. Individual Christians have been beaten. Pastors in rural areas face organized opposition from village authorities who coordinate with nationalist groups.

## Church Growth Against the Odds

Nepal’s Christian community has grown from an estimated 1,000 believers in 1960 to somewhere between 500,000 and 800,000 today. The growth has been primarily through indigenous witness: Nepali Christians evangelizing their own families, neighbors, and communities. The church is predominantly house-church-based. The Nepal Christian community’s center of gravity is among the lower castes and Dalit communities, among whom Christianity’s message of equal dignity before God has particular resonance.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #27
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 500,000–800,000 (~2–3%)
- **Primary Threats:** Constitutional anti-conversion law (Article 26), Penal Code criminalization of conversion and proselytism, Hindu nationalist mob violence, arrests of pastors and Bible distributors
- **Legal Status:** Secular state but conversion criminalized; up to 5 years imprisonment for facilitating conversion
- **Growth:** From ~1,000 Christians (1960) to 500,000–800,000 (present)
- **Notable:** World’s only Hindu kingdom until 2008; new secular constitution simultaneously eliminated the monarchy and criminalized conversion

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Nepal* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/nepal/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Nepal* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/nepal/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Nepal* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Nepal — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/nepal/
- Human Rights Watch, *Nepal country reports* — hrw.org/asia/nepal
- International Christian Concern, *Nepal Country Profile* — persecution.org/country/nepal/

COUNTRY: Netherlands
TITLE: Netherlands — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/netherlands
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

The Netherlands is a small, densely populated Western European democracy with full constitutional religious freedom and one of the highest rates of religious non-affiliation in the world — approximately 55–60% of Dutch people identify as having no religion. Historically, the Netherlands was the cradle of the Dutch Reformed tradition, the home of major Calvinist theologians and political thinkers, and a significant force in the spread of Protestant Christianity through its colonial empire. Today, active Christian practice is a minority position in Dutch society. Christians of all backgrounds practice without restriction. The Netherlands is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~40% (nominal; active practice much lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

COUNTRY: New Zealand
TITLE: New Zealand — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/new-zealand
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

New Zealand is a Pacific island nation of approximately 5 million people with full constitutional religious freedom and a rapidly secularizing population — approximately 37% of New Zealanders identified as Christian in the 2018 census, down from 57% in 2006. No persecution exists. Christianity arrived through the Church Missionary Society (Anglican) beginning in 1814, when Samuel Marsden conducted the first Christian service on New Zealand soil. The Māori — the indigenous Polynesian people of New Zealand — developed distinctive Māori Christian expressions including the Rātana movement (founded 1918) and Ringatū (founded by the prophet Te Kooti Arikirangi in the 19th century), which integrated Christian faith with Māori spiritual and cultural traditions. New Zealand is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~37% (declining; active practice much lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Nicaragua
TITLE: Nicaragua — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/nicaragua
META DESCRIPTION: Nicaragua under Daniel Ortega has become one of the Western Hemisphere’s most dangerous countries for the church. Bishops have been imprisoned and exiled, Catholic schools nationalized, the Papal Nuncio expelled, and pastors jailed. The assault on the church is the most comprehensive in Latin American history since Mexico’s Cristero era.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Nicaragua, Central America, Catholic, Evangelical, Ortega, Persecution, Bishop Rolando Álvarez, WWL Top 50, State Persecution
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Nicaragua is approximately 55% Catholic and 33% evangelical Protestant — a deeply Christian country undergoing one of the most systematic assaults on the church by a government in modern Latin American history. President Daniel Ortega — who has ruled Nicaragua since 2007 — launched a comprehensive campaign against the Catholic Church beginning in 2018, when bishops and priests sided with protesters during a mass uprising against his government. What followed has included the imprisonment and exile of a bishop, the expulsion of the Vatican’s ambassador, the nationalization of Catholic schools and media, the arrest of dozens of priests and lay leaders, and the revocation of the legal status of hundreds of religious organizations. Nicaragua ranked in the Open Doors top 50 in 2024, and the trajectory is sharply deteriorating.

## Christianity in Nicaragua

Spanish colonization established Catholicism as Nicaragua’s foundational religious institution from the 16th century. Evangelical and Pentecostal Christianity grew rapidly in Nicaragua during the 1980s and 1990s. The Catholic Church’s position in the Ortega era’s first term (2007–2011) was relatively cooperative; the dramatic deterioration began after the 2018 protests.

## The 2018 Turning Point

The April 2018 protests — triggered by social security reform and expanding into a broad pro-democracy uprising — were a watershed moment. The Catholic Church offered churches as sanctuaries for protesters and its bishops as mediators. When Ortega’s forces and government-aligned paramilitaries killed over 300 people, the Catholic hierarchy condemned the violence unambiguously. Ortega blamed the church for the protests and characterized it as a counter-revolutionary force.

## Bishop Rolando Álvarez: The Most Prominent Case

Bishop Rolando Álvarez Lagos of Matagalpa — one of Nicaragua’s most respected Catholic prelates — was arrested in 2022 on charges of “undermining national integrity.” He was sentenced in February 2023 to 26 years in prison. Under intense Vatican and international diplomatic pressure, Ortega released Álvarez in January 2024 — and immediately deported him to Rome, stripping him of his Nicaraguan citizenship. He became one of 19 Catholic priests expelled to Rome in a single day. The deportations were the largest single expulsion of Catholic clergy from any country since the Mexican Cristero era of the 1920s.

## The Scale of the Assault

The full scope of Ortega’s assault on the church includes: the expulsion of the Apostolic Nuncio (the Vatican’s ambassador) in 2022; the nationalization and closure of the Jesuit-run Universidad Centroamericana (UCA) in 2023; the revocation of legal status for over 1,500 civil society organizations, including Catholic Caritas and over 100 other religious organizations; the closure of Catholic radio stations; the arrest of several dozen priests on various pretexts; and the seizure of Catholic properties including retreat centers, charity offices, and educational facilities.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~88%
- **Primary Threats:** State persecution — imprisonment and exile of bishops and priests, nationalization of church institutions, revocation of legal status for religious organizations, expulsion of Vatican ambassador
- **Current Status:** Most comprehensive state assault on the Catholic Church in Latin America since Mexico’s Cristero era
- **Open Doors WWL:** Top 50 ranked and rising
- **Notable:** Bishop Rolando Álvarez sentenced to 26 years, subsequently deported; 19 priests expelled to Rome in single day (January 2024); UCA nationalized

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Nicaragua* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/nicaragua/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Nicaragua* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/nicaragua/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Nicaragua* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Nicaragua — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/nicaragua/
- Human Rights Watch, *Nicaragua country reports* — hrw.org/americas/nicaragua
- Amnesty International, *Nicaragua* — amnesty.org/en/countries/americas/nicaragua/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Niger
TITLE: Niger — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/niger
META DESCRIPTION: Niger is a 99% Muslim Sahelian nation where Christians — barely 0.5% of the population — face jihadist violence from multiple armed groups. The military coup of 2023 removed the Western partnerships that provided the most security. The church is tiny, isolated, and endangered.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Niger, Sahel, West Africa, Jihadism, JNIM, ISIS Sahel, Christian Minority, Coup, WWL Top 25
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #29 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Niger is one of the world’s poorest and most vulnerable nations — a vast, landlocked Sahelian country of 26 million people, of whom approximately 99% are Muslim. Christians represent approximately 0.5% of the population — roughly 130,000 people, concentrated almost entirely in the capital Niamey and in the southwest border region. Since approximately 2015, jihadist armed groups — primarily JNIM and the Islamic State in the Greater Sahara (ISGS) — have conducted attacks across Niger’s regions. The July 2023 military coup that removed democratically elected President Mohamed Bazoum expelled French forces and severed security cooperation with Western partners, dramatically worsening the security environment. Niger ranked #29 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Christianity in Niger

The French colonial administration brought Catholic missionaries who worked primarily among non-Muslim ethnic groups, settling small communities in Niamey and surrounding areas. The Evangelical Church of Niger established work in the mid-20th century. At independence in 1960, Niger’s Christian community was estimated at a few tens of thousands; it has grown modestly to approximately 130,000. Interreligious relations in Niger’s history were characterized by a degree of coexistence rooted in the country’s Sufi-influenced Islam and the traditional tolerance of West African religious pluralism — tradition disrupted by Wahhabi and Salafi-influenced ideologies and the jihadist insurgency.

## Jihadist Violence

In March 2021, rioters in Niamey attacked and burned at least five Christian churches following protests triggered by international publications of cartoons depicting the Prophet Muhammad. Rural Christians in areas with jihadist presence face a more sustained threat: villages with known Christian residents have been targeted; Christians have been killed in attacks that explicitly separated them from Muslim residents. Pastors working in the interior have been killed.

## Post-Coup Vulnerability

The July 2023 coup brought the military junta (CNSP) to power. France withdrew its forces; the US military presence was significantly reduced. The immediate security consequence was a reduction in counterterrorism pressure on JNIM and ISGS.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #29
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~130,000 (~0.5%)
- **Primary Threats:** Jihadist violence (JNIM, ISGS), community attacks, church burnings, killing of pastors; post-coup security deterioration
- **Government:** Military junta since July 2023; Western security partnerships severed

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Niger* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/niger/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Niger* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/niger/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Niger — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/niger/
- UN OCHA, *Niger Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/niger
- International Crisis Group, *Niger/Sahel reporting* — crisisgroup.org

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Nigeria
TITLE: Nigeria — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/nigeria
META DESCRIPTION: Nigeria is the world’s most populated Black nation — and one of its most dangerous places to be a Christian. Boko Haram and ISWAP have killed tens of thousands. Fulani militant attacks have emptied the Middle Belt of Christian communities. Sharia law governs twelve northern states. And yet the Nigerian church is one of the largest, most vibrant, and most globally mission-minded on earth.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Nigeria, West Africa, Boko Haram, ISWAP, Fulani Militants, Middle Belt, Sharia, WWL Top 10, Leah Sharibu, Chibok Girls, Kaduna
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #6 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Nigeria is simultaneously one of the most Christian and one of the most dangerous countries on earth for Christians. It is the most populous country in Africa — approximately 220 million people — roughly divided between a predominantly Muslim north and a predominantly Christian south, with a contested and bleeding Middle Belt region caught between them. Nigeria has more Christians than most European countries have citizens: approximately 95–100 million. It also has Boko Haram, the Islamic State West Africa Province (ISWAP), Fulani militant herdsmen who have systematically attacked and emptied hundreds of Christian farming villages across the Middle Belt, twelve northern states that have implemented Sharia law, and a federal government whose response to anti-Christian violence has been — in the assessment of virtually every monitoring organization — catastrophically inadequate. Nigeria ranked #6 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List. Depending on the metric used, Nigeria accounts for the highest or second-highest number of Christians killed for their faith anywhere in the world in any given recent year.

## Christianity’s Deep Nigerian Roots

Christianity arrived in Nigeria through multiple channels in the 19th century. The freed slave Samuel Ajayi Crowther — who became the first African Anglican bishop in 1864, consecrated in Canterbury Cathedral — was himself Nigerian (Yoruba) and translated the Bible into Yoruba. By independence in 1960, Nigeria had a complex religious map: the predominantly Muslim north under the influence of the historic Sokoto Caliphate; a largely Christian and animist south; and a contested Middle Belt.

The post-independence church has grown with extraordinary vigor. Nigeria is home to some of the largest individual Christian congregations in the world — Enoch Adeboye’s Redeemed Christian Church of God, David Oyedepo’s Winners’ Chapel, and others whose memberships number in the millions. Nigerian missionaries serve across Africa and on every continent.

## The North: Sharia, Boko Haram, and ISWAP

Twelve northern states implemented Sharia law beginning in 2000 — Zamfara State first. Christians living in the north found themselves under governance frameworks that discriminated against them in courts, employment, and public life. Boko Haram launched an armed insurgency in 2009 with the goal of establishing an Islamic state in northern Nigeria governed by strict Sharia. Churches have been bombed — most dramatically at Christmas and Easter. The Christmas Day bombing of St. Theresa’s Catholic Church in Madalla, Niger State, in 2011 killed 44 people. The dominant surviving Boko Haram faction — ISWAP, affiliated with the Islamic State since 2016 — has maintained a significant presence in the Lake Chad basin and surrounding areas of Borno, Yobe, and Adamawa states, conducting sustained anti-Christian campaigns.

## The Chibok Girls and Leah Sharibu

The April 2014 kidnapping of 276 girls from the Government Secondary School in Chibok, Borno State — the “Chibok Girls” — became an international symbol. The girls were predominantly Christian, attending a school in a predominantly Christian community. The case of Leah Sharibu is distinct: Leah, 14 years old at the time of her February 2018 kidnapping with 109 other schoolgirls from Dapchi, Yobe State, was the only girl not released when the others were freed. Her captors’ stated reason: she refused to renounce her Christian faith and convert to Islam. Leah Sharibu has been held by ISWAP ever since. She remains, as of early 2025, the longest-held Boko Haram/ISWAP hostage and the most internationally recognized symbol of ISWAP’s anti-Christian theology.

## The Middle Belt: Fulani Militant Violence

Separate from — and in some ways more deadly than — the Boko Haram/ISWAP insurgency is the wave of Fulani militant attacks on Christian farming communities across the Middle Belt states. The conflict has a resource competition dimension — encroachment of farmland on traditional grazing routes — but also a clear religious dimension: the attackers are predominantly Muslim; the victims are predominantly Christian; churches are not attacked while mosques are; and the attacks follow a pattern — night raids on farming villages, mass killings, burning of homes and churches, and seizure of land — that goes beyond resource competition.

In some of the worst years — 2018, 2019, 2021 — Open Doors documented that more Christians were killed in Nigeria alone than in all other countries on the World Watch List combined. The Middle Belt states of Plateau and Kaduna have been particularly devastated.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #6
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~95–100 million (~45–50%)
- **Primary Threats:** Boko Haram/ISWAP insurgency (northeast), Fulani militant attacks on farming communities (Middle Belt), Sharia law in twelve northern states, inadequate government protection
- **Scale of Killing:** Nigeria accounts for the majority of Christians killed for their faith globally in recent years by most monitoring assessments
- **Notable:** Leah Sharibu — held by ISWAP since 2018 for refusing to renounce Christianity; Chibok Girls kidnapping (2014)

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Nigeria* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/nigeria/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Nigeria* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/nigeria/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Nigeria* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Nigeria — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/nigeria/
- Human Rights Watch, *Nigeria country reports* — hrw.org/africa/nigeria
- Amnesty International, *Nigeria* — amnesty.org/en/countries/africa/nigeria/
- International Christian Concern, *Nigeria Country Profile* — persecution.org/country/nigeria/
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Nigeria* — csw.org.uk
- Aid to the Church in Need, *Nigeria reporting* — acn-intl.org
- UN Security Council, *Lake Chad Basin/Nigeria reporting*

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: North Korea
TITLE: North Korea — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/north-korea
META DESCRIPTION: North Korea is the world’s most dangerous country for Christians — ranked #1 on the Open Doors World Watch List for 22 consecutive years. In a country where Kim Jong Un is worshiped as a deity, practicing Christianity means labor camps, torture, and death. An estimated 50,000–70,000 Christians are in political prison camps right now.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: North Korea, East Asia, Kim Jong Un, Juche, Political Prison Camps, Kwanliso, WWL #1, Underground Church, Labor Camps, Totalitarian
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #1 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

North Korea has been ranked #1 on the Open Doors World Watch List for twenty-two consecutive years. It is, by every measure available to the outside world, the most dangerous country on earth in which to be a Christian. Under the Kim dynasty — Kim Il Sung, Kim Jong Il, and now Kim Jong Un — North Korea has constructed the most complete totalitarian state in human history, a system in which the ruling family is literally worshiped as divine, in which the state ideology of Juche (self-reliance) functions as a substitute religion, and in which any loyalty to any being above the Supreme Leader is treated as an act of treason punishable by imprisonment, torture, and death. An estimated 50,000 to 70,000 Christians are currently held in North Korea’s political prison camp system enduring conditions that United Nations investigators have compared to the Nazi concentration camps in their systematic brutality.

## Christianity Before the Division

Pyongyang, now the capital of the North Korean state, was known in the early 20th century as the “Jerusalem of the East.” American Presbyterian and Methodist missionaries arrived in Korea in the 1880s and found extraordinary receptivity. The Great Revival of 1907 — which began in Pyongyang — was one of the most significant religious revivals in Asian Christian history. By the time of Japanese colonial annexation in 1910, Korea had a significant and growing Protestant church. After Japan’s defeat in 1945, the Korean peninsula was divided along the 38th parallel. Kim Il Sung moved systematically to eliminate Christianity along with every other source of loyalty outside the state.

## The Construction of the Juche Religion

Kim Il Sung developed Juche into a total worldview that fulfilled the functions of religion while claiming to be its scientific replacement. Kim Il Sung is called “Eternal President” and is worshiped at shrines across the country. In this framework, Christianity is not merely a foreign ideology — it is a competing religion that claims a God above the Kims, a Kingdom not of this world, a loyalty that cannot be subordinated to the Supreme Leader.

## The Political Prison Camps: Kwanliso

North Korea operates six known political prison camps — kwanliso — that together hold an estimated 100,000–120,000 prisoners, of whom Christians are believed to comprise approximately 50,000–70,000. Prisoners are worked in mines, farms, and factories under conditions that produce high mortality rates. Beatings are systematic; torture is common as a means of extracting information about underground church networks. The “three generations” punishment policy — under which the arrest of a Christian results in the imprisonment of their children and grandchildren — is one of the most savage features of the system. The UN Commission of Inquiry on Human Rights in North Korea (2014) concluded that the crimes committed in these camps constitute crimes against humanity, specifically documenting the targeting of Christians.

## The Underground Church

Despite the totality of the repression, an underground Christian community exists in North Korea. Its scale is unknowable with any precision; estimates range from a few thousand to several hundred thousand. It is organized in the smallest possible units — individuals, couples, families — who maintain faith in almost complete isolation. The church has no buildings, no institutions, no published names, no public existence of any kind. Some North Koreans encounter Christianity through Chinese-manufactured radios that can pick up South Korean Christian broadcasting; others through contact with Korean-Chinese Christians in China; others through the testimony of family members who were Christians before the Kim era.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #1 (22 consecutive years at #1)
- **Estimated Christian Population:** Unknown; estimates range from 200,000 to 400,000 underground believers
- **Christians in Prison Camps:** Estimated 50,000–70,000
- **Primary Mechanism:** Juche state religion treats Christianity as treason; three-generations punishment policy; total surveillance state
- **Legal Status:** Christianity entirely illegal; no legal religious expression permitted
- **UN Finding (2014):** Crimes against humanity including extermination and torture of Christians and others in political prison camps

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: North Korea* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/north-korea/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: North Korea* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/north-korea/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: North Korea* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: North Korea — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/korea-north/
- UN Commission of Inquiry on Human Rights in the Democratic People’s Republic of Korea, *Final Report* (2014) — ohchr.org/en/hr-bodies/hrc/co-idprk/commission-inquiry-report-dprk
- Human Rights Watch, *North Korea country reports* — hrw.org/asia/north-korea
- Amnesty International, *North Korea* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/north-korea/
- Database Center for North Korean Human Rights (NKDB) — nkdb.org
- Korea Future Initiative — koreafuture.org

COUNTRY: North Macedonia
TITLE: North Macedonia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/north-macedonia
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

North Macedonia is a small Balkan nation of approximately 2 million people — approximately 65% Eastern Orthodox Christian and 30% Muslim (predominantly ethnic Albanian) — with full constitutional religious freedom. The country has been a stable democratic state since independence from Yugoslavia in 1991. The primary institutional challenge facing the Macedonian Orthodox Church has been its canonical status — it declared autocephaly (independence) from the Serbian Orthodox Church in 1967, a declaration not recognized by the broader Orthodox communion until 2022, when the Ecumenical Patriarchate granted it recognition. This decades-long canonical dispute affected the church’s international relationships but not its domestic freedom to function. North Macedonia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~65% (predominantly Macedonian Orthodox)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: North Macedonia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/north-macedonia/

COUNTRY: Norway
TITLE: Norway — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/norway
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Norway is a Nordic welfare state of approximately 5.5 million people with full religious freedom and one of the world’s lowest rates of active religious practice. Approximately 68% of Norwegians are nominally members of the Church of Norway (Evangelical Lutheran), but active weekly attendance is estimated at under 5%. The Church of Norway’s formal state church status was significantly reduced by a 2017 constitutional amendment that removed language making Lutheranism the state religion while maintaining the church’s public role. Norway is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. Norwegian Church Aid is among the most significant Christian humanitarian organizations in the world.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~75% (nominal; active practice very low)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Norway* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/norway/
