=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Suriname
TITLE: Suriname — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/suriname
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Suriname is a small South American nation of approximately 600,000 people with one of the most demographically diverse religious landscapes in the Western Hemisphere. Approximately 48% of Surinamese are Christian — Catholic, Dutch Reformed, Moravian (a particularly significant historical presence), evangelical, and Pentecostal — alongside 22% Hindu, 14% Muslim, and 5% Winti (an Afro-Surinamese traditional religion). The Moravian Brethren played a particularly remarkable role in Suriname’s Christian history: they established missions in 1735 specifically among the enslaved African population — at a time when most colonial Christianity was entirely complicit with slavery. Suriname’s interreligious relations are generally positive. Christians practice freely; there is no persecution. Suriname is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~48%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; remarkable interreligious coexistence
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Moravian Brethren established mission to enslaved Africans in Suriname (1735)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Suriname* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/suriname/
- CIA World Factbook: Suriname — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/suriname/

COUNTRY: Sweden
TITLE: Sweden — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/sweden
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Sweden is one of the world’s most secular nations — approximately 53% of Swedes are nominal members of the Church of Sweden (Lutheran), though active weekly attendance is estimated under 5%. The Church of Sweden was the official state church until 2000, when the formal tie was severed. Full religious freedom exists; Christians face no persecution. Sweden was Christianized primarily through the work of Ansgar of Bremen (“Apostle of the North”) in the 9th century and through subsequent royal conversions. Sweden is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

A notable recent development: several Swedish Christians — including a Pentecostal pastor — have faced legal proceedings under hate speech laws for sermons on sexual ethics, raising religious expression debates common to Nordic countries. Courts have generally protected the right to preach traditional Christian teaching, though the legal landscape remains contested. This is not persecution; it is a legal framework debate common across Western Europe.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~55% (nominal; active practice very low)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Sweden* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/sweden/
- CIA World Factbook: Sweden — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/sweden/
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Switzerland
TITLE: Switzerland — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/switzerland
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Switzerland is approximately 65% Christian — roughly equally divided between Roman Catholic (34%) and Protestant Reformed (24%), with a growing evangelical minority — and has full constitutional religious freedom. Switzerland was the cradle of the Reformed tradition: Ulrich Zwingli launched Reformation in Zurich in 1519, and John Calvin established his influential theocratic experiment in Geneva from 1541. The Geneva Conventions — the foundational documents of international humanitarian law — were developed in Geneva in 1864, with the International Committee of the Red Cross (ICRC) inspired by Henry Dunant’s Christian humanitarian convictions. The ICRC’s symbol — a red cross on white — is the Swiss flag reversed, a tribute to Dunant’s nationality. Switzerland is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~65%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Significance:** Birthplace of the Reformed tradition (Zwingli, Zurich 1519; Calvin, Geneva 1541); Geneva Conventions (1864) inspired by Christian humanitarian conviction

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Switzerland* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/switzerland/
- CIA World Factbook: Switzerland — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/switzerland/

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Syria
TITLE: Syria — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/syria
META DESCRIPTION: Syria was home to one of the oldest Christian communities in the world — Damascus was where Saul of Tarsus became Paul; Antioch was where followers of Jesus were first called Christians. The civil war killed half a million people and drove Syria’s Christians from over 1.5 million to perhaps 400,000, their communities shattered by ISIS, Assad, and years of indiscriminate war. The fall of Assad in December 2024 opens an era of radical uncertainty.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Syria, Middle East, Ancient Church, Civil War, ISIS, Antioch, Damascus, WWL Top 25, Assad Fall, Displacement, HTS
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #24 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Syria is one of the most ancient Christian homelands in the world. Damascus — among the world’s oldest continuously inhabited cities — is where Saul of Tarsus had his encounter with the risen Christ and became Paul the Apostle. Antioch, in what is now southern Turkey but was historically part of the Syrian cultural sphere, was where followers of Jesus were first called “Christians” (Acts 11:26) and was the base of Paul’s missionary journeys. The Syriac Christian tradition — worshipping in a dialect of Aramaic closely related to what Jesus spoke — is among the most ancient in the world. From over 1.5 million Christians before Syria’s civil war began in 2011, the Christian population has fallen to an estimated 300,000–500,000 — a catastrophic demographic collapse. The December 2024 fall of Bashar al-Assad’s government creates an era of radical uncertainty for Syria’s already devastated Christian communities. Syria ranked #24 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## The Ancient Christian Foundation

The New Testament’s account of Paul’s conversion on the road to Damascus (Acts 9) makes Syria one of the originating sites of Christian history. Antioch — the city from which Paul and Barnabas launched their missionary journeys — was one of the great cities of the ancient world and the first major center of Christianity outside Jerusalem. The church of Antioch gave rise to the Syriac Christian tradition, including the Church of the East, the Syriac Orthodox Church, and the Maronite Church. The ancient city of Maaloula, in the mountains northwest of Damascus, maintains an Aramaic-speaking Christian community that has preserved the liturgical language of the early church into the 21st century — arguably the closest surviving linguistic connection to the language Jesus spoke.

## The Civil War and Its Devastation of the Church

The Syrian civil war began in March 2011 with mass protests against Assad’s government and escalated into a multi-sided catastrophe. For Christians, the war created a compound crisis. The Assad government — which had provided a degree of protection for religious minorities as part of its multi-sectarian political coalition — was fighting for its survival against opposition groups of widely varying ideology. ISIS — which controlled significant territory from 2013 to 2019 — was explicitly and violently anti-Christian. Christians in ISIS-held areas faced the same ultimatum as everywhere ISIS governed: convert, pay the jizya, leave, or be killed. The ancient Christian communities of the Khabur River valley in northeast Syria were attacked and abducted en masse by ISIS in 2015 — hundreds of Assyrian Christians were taken hostage. The historic Christian town of Qaraqosh (also known in the Syrian context) was among those devastated.

## The Post-Assad Moment

In December 2024, the Assad government fell with stunning speed when the rebel coalition led by Hayat Tahrir al-Sham (HTS) swept through Aleppo and then Damascus. HTS is the dominant group in the new Syrian governance structure. HTS has roots in Jabhat al-Nusra, al-Qaeda’s Syrian affiliate, though its current leadership has publicly sought to distance the organization from jihadist ideology and has made public statements promising protection for minorities. Syrian Christians approach these assurances with cautious skepticism born from bitter experience. The first months of the post-Assad era will be determinative for whether Syria’s remnant Christian community has a future in the country.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #24
- **Estimated Current Christian Population:** 300,000–500,000 (down from 1.5+ million pre-war)
- **Primary Historical Threats:** Civil war displacement, ISIS ethnic and religious cleansing, indiscriminate bombing of Christian neighborhoods
- **Current Uncertainty:** Assad fell December 2024; HTS-led governance; Christian community monitoring HTS minority protection commitments
- **Ancient Heritage:** Damascus — Paul’s conversion; Antioch — first called Christians; Maaloula — Aramaic-speaking Christians; oldest Christian communities in the world

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Syria* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/syria/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Syria* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/syria/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Syria — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/syria/
- Human Rights Watch, *Syria country reports* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/syria
- Amnesty International, *Syria* — amnesty.org/en/countries/middle-east-and-north-africa/syria/
- UN OCHA, *Syria Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/syria
- Aid to the Church in Need, *Syria reporting* — acn-intl.org
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003) — for Antioch and Syriac church history

COUNTRY: Taiwan
TITLE: Taiwan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/taiwan
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Taiwan is a democratic island of approximately 23.5 million people with full constitutional religious freedom. Approximately 5–6% of the population identifies as Christian — a small but active community including Presbyterian, Catholic, evangelical, and charismatic congregations. The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan has a long history of advocacy for Taiwanese democratic identity against authoritarian governance, both during the Japanese colonial period and under KMT rule. Christianity has had particular success among Taiwan’s indigenous Austronesian-speaking peoples, several of whose communities are majority Christian. Taiwan is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. Christians face no persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~5–6%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; vibrant church community
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable:** Presbyterian Church in Taiwan — historically significant advocate for democratic identity and indigenous rights

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Tajikistan
TITLE: Tajikistan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/tajikistan
META DESCRIPTION: Tajikistan is a secular authoritarian state where the government controls all religious life, Christianity is barely tolerated, unregistered churches are raided and closed, foreign missionaries are expelled, and religious education of children is effectively banned. Christians navigate one of Central Asia’s most restrictive religious environments.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Tajikistan, Central Asia, Islam, Post-Soviet, Registration Restrictions, House Churches, Raids, WWL Top 30, Authoritarian
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #30 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Tajikistan is a landlocked, mountainous Central Asian nation of approximately 10 million people — the poorest country in the former Soviet Union — governed by President Emomali Rahmon, who has ruled since 1992. The population is approximately 96–98% Muslim (predominantly Sunni). Christians number approximately 1–2% — largely ethnic Russians and other Soviet-era settlers, with a small but growing community of Tajik converts. The government controls religious life comprehensively: all religious organizations must register with the state; unregistered religious activity is illegal; religious education of minors outside the home is banned; the distribution of religious literature requires state approval; foreign missionaries are banned. Christianity is legal only within narrowly constrained official structures; the church that operates outside those structures — the growing community of Tajik converts who meet in house groups — does so illegally and at risk of raids, fines, arrests, and deportation of foreign members. Tajikistan ranked #30 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Government Religious Control

Tajikistan’s Religion Law, revised multiple times, requires all religious organizations to register with the Committee on Religious Affairs and Regulation of Traditions, Holidays, and Ceremonies. Registration requirements include minimum membership thresholds, approved premises, and financial documentation. For small evangelical and Protestant congregations — particularly those meeting informally — these requirements function as an effective ban. Unregistered religious meetings are subject to police raids. Participants are identified, fined, and sometimes detained. Foreign nationals participating in unregistered religious activity face deportation.

Religious education of minors is restricted to official channels. Parents can provide religious education within the home; any organized religious instruction of children outside state-approved religious schools is prohibited. Bibles and other religious literature can be imported in limited quantities for personal use; bulk importation requires approval.

## Tajik Converts and House Churches

The most vulnerable community is the growing number of ethnic Tajik Muslims who have converted to Christianity — primarily through evangelical and Pentecostal influence that reached Tajikistan through missionary activity in the early 1990s and through contact with the international community. These converts face family rejection and social exclusion in addition to legal vulnerability. House churches serving these converts operate illegally and are subject to raids.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #30
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~1–2% (~100,000–200,000)
- **Primary Threats:** Mandatory registration (functional ban on independent churches), raids on house churches, ban on religious education of children, deportation of foreign missionaries, social consequences for Tajik converts
- **Legal Status:** Christianity technically legal within registered structures; unregistered church activity criminal

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Tajikistan* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/tajikistan/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Tajikistan* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/tajikistan/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Tajikistan* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- Forum 18 News Service, *Tajikistan* — forum18.org/archive.php?country=31
- CIA World Factbook: Tajikistan — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/tajikistan/
- Human Rights Watch, *Tajikistan* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/tajikistan

COUNTRY: Tanzania
TITLE: Tanzania — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/tanzania
META DESCRIPTION: Tanzania is a religiously balanced East African nation where Christians and Muslims have historically coexisted. But in Zanzibar, Christians face significant discrimination, and on the mainland, Islamist attacks against churches and pastors represent a growing and documented threat.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Tanzania, East Africa, Christian Majority, Islam, Zanzibar, Church Attacks, Islamist Violence, Religious Discrimination
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Tanzania is approximately 63% Christian and 34% Muslim — one of the most religiously balanced large nations in sub-Saharan Africa — and for much of its post-independence history has maintained a formal interreligious harmony through the political framework of Chama Cha Mapinduzi governance. The mainland (Tanganyika) and the islands (Zanzibar) united in 1964, but Zanzibar retains substantial autonomy and is approximately 99% Muslim. For Tanzanian Christians, the situation varies dramatically by geography: mainland Christians practice largely freely; Christians in Zanzibar face systematic discrimination and pressure.

## Mainland Tanzania: Growing Islamist Violence

On the mainland, a growing pattern of Islamist attacks against churches and pastors has emerged, particularly in the coastal regions of Dar es Salaam, Mtwara, and the coast more broadly. Pastors have been killed with knives or machetes in targeted assassinations. Church buildings have been attacked. These attacks are carried out by radical Islamist cells, not by the government. The Tanzanian government has responded with security operations, but the pattern of attacks continues and intensifies. Open Doors documents Tanzania in its monitoring tier for exactly this pattern.

## Zanzibar: Systematic Discrimination

In Zanzibar, the small Christian community — approximately 1% of the population — faces significant structural discrimination. Building permits for churches are extremely difficult to obtain; Christians are effectively excluded from senior government positions; Christian schools face administrative obstacles; and the social and family pressure on any Zanzibari who converts from Islam is intense and potentially dangerous.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~63%
- **Primary Concerns:** Mainland — Islamist attacks on churches and pastors (coastal regions); Zanzibar — systematic discrimination against Christian minority
- **Current Status:** Mainland majority practices largely freely; coastal and Zanzibar Christians face documented threats
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; moderate tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Tanzania* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/tanzania/
- CIA World Factbook: Tanzania — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/tanzania/
- Open Doors USA, *Tanzania Country Profile* — opendoorsusa.org
- Human Rights Watch, *Tanzania* — hrw.org/africa/tanzania
- International Christian Concern, *Tanzania* — persecution.org/country/tanzania/

COUNTRY: Thailand
TITLE: Thailand — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/thailand
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Thailand is approximately 94% Theravada Buddhist and approximately 1% Christian — roughly 700,000 people in a nation of 70 million. Thailand is a constitutional monarchy in which the king is the protector of Buddhism and Buddhism is the dominant institutional and cultural force. Christianity is a small minority with a long history in Thailand: Catholic missionaries arrived in the 17th century; Protestant missions (primarily American Presbyterians and Baptists) established work in the 19th century. Christian communities are concentrated in Chiang Mai and the northern highlands (where hill tribe peoples have converted in significant numbers), in Bangkok, and in the south. Christians practice freely in Thailand with no systematic persecution. Some evangelical and Pentecostal groups have encountered bureaucratic friction with registration, but no pattern of state oppression exists. Thailand is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~1%
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

COUNTRY: Timor-Leste
TITLE: Timor-Leste — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/timor-leste
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Timor-Leste (East Timor) is one of the most Catholic countries in Asia — approximately 97% of the population identifies as Catholic. The Catholic Church became the defining institution of Timorese resistance identity during the Indonesian occupation (1975–1999), when the Church was the only institution the occupiers could not fully suppress. Bishop Carlos Filipe Ximenes Belo — awarded the Nobel Peace Prize in 1996 — was the most prominent voice for Timorese rights during the occupation. Independence was achieved in 2002. Christians practice entirely freely in Timor-Leste. The country is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~97% (overwhelmingly Catholic)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; Catholic identity synonymous with Timorese national identity
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** Catholic Church was the primary institutional defender of Timorese identity during Indonesian occupation; Bishop Belo received Nobel Peace Prize (1996)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Timor-Leste* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/timor-leste/
- CIA World Factbook: Timor-Leste — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/timor-leste/

## Overview

Trinidad and Tobago is a twin-island Caribbean nation of approximately 1.4 million people with a diverse religious landscape reflecting its multi-ethnic history: approximately 57% Christian (Catholic, Anglican, Pentecostal, and others), 22% Hindu, and 5% Muslim — all descended from different waves of colonial and post-colonial migration. =================================================================================

COUNTRY: Tunisia
TITLE: Tunisia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/tunisia
META DESCRIPTION: Tunisia was the homeland of Tertullian and Cyprian — pillars of the ancient church. Today it is 99% Muslim, Christianity has no legal indigenous presence, and converts face severe consequences. The country’s post-Arab Spring political evolution has not improved conditions for the tiny Christian minority.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Tunisia, North Africa, Islam, Converts, Ancient Church, Apostasy, Tertullian, Carthage, WWL Top 40
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Tunisia — ancient Carthage — was one of the most important centers of early Christianity. Tertullian, who wrote in Latin what would become the theological vocabulary of Western Christianity, was from Carthage. The martyrdom of Perpetua and Felicity — among the most celebrated early Christian martyrs — occurred in Carthage in 203 AD. Augustine’s North African world shaped by this tradition produced the greatest theologian of the Western church. Today, Tunisia is approximately 99% Muslim, the church that produced these giants is long gone, and the tiny number of Tunisian converts to Christianity — estimated at a few hundred to a few thousand — practice in secret at serious personal risk.

Tunisia’s post-Arab Spring government — and particularly the increasingly authoritarian governance of President Kais Saied since his 2021 consolidation of power — has not shown any indication of improving conditions for religious minorities. The primary concern for Tunisian Christians is not state prosecution but severe social and family consequences for those who convert from Islam. Proselytism is criminalized. Apostasy, while not explicitly listed as a crime in the Penal Code, can be prosecuted under other provisions.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~22,000 (mostly expatriate); estimated several hundred to a few thousand secret indigenous converts
- **Primary Concerns:** Proselytism criminal; severe social consequences for converts; ancient Christian heritage entirely gone
- **Historical Significance:** Birthplace of Tertullian, Cyprian; location of Perpetua and Felicity martyrdoms; Carthaginian church was one of the most important in the ancient world
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; moderate tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Tunisia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/tunisia/
- CIA World Factbook: Tunisia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/tunisia/
- Open Doors USA, *Tunisia monitoring* — opendoorsusa.org
- Jenkins, Philip, *The Lost History of Christianity* (HarperOne, 2008)

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Turkey
TITLE: Turkey — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/turkey
META DESCRIPTION: Turkey was once the heartland of Christianity — the seven churches of Revelation, the great councils of Nicaea and Chalcedon, the birthplace of Paul, the ministry of John and Mary. Today it is 99% Muslim, the Christian population is a fraction of a percent, churches face constant legal barriers, and Christians are murdered by nationalists. Hagia Sophia is a mosque again.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Turkey, Middle East, Ancient Church, Seven Churches, Hagia Sophia, Armenian Genocide, Greek Orthodox, WWL Top 40, Nationalist Violence
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Turkey is the heartland of some of the most important history in Christianity. The seven churches addressed in the Book of Revelation — Ephesus, Smyrna, Pergamum, Thyatira, Sardis, Philadelphia, and Laodicea — were all in what is now western Turkey. Paul the Apostle was born in Tarsus, in what is now southeastern Turkey. The councils that defined Christian doctrine — Nicaea (325 AD), Constantinople (381 AD), Ephesus (431 AD), Chalcedon (451 AD) — all met on what is now Turkish soil. The Hagia Sophia in Istanbul was for nearly a thousand years the greatest church building in the world. Today, Turkey is approximately 99% Muslim. The Christian population is estimated at 0.2% — roughly 170,000 people in a nation of 84 million — and declining. In July 2020, President Erdoğan converted Hagia Sophia from a museum back to a mosque, completing its symbolic reconversion first performed by Mehmed II in 1453.

## The Ancient Church

The Christian history embedded in Anatolian soil is among the densest in the world. Paul’s missionary journeys — described in the Acts of the Apostles — established churches across Galatia, Asia, Macedonia, and Achaia. Ephesus was one of the greatest Christian cities of the ancient world, where Paul spent three years, where tradition holds that John the Apostle lived with Mary the mother of Jesus, and where the Council of Ephesus (431 AD) affirmed Mary as Theotokos (Mother of God) against Nestorius. The Apostle Peter established the church at Antioch (now Antakya) before moving to Rome.

## The Dissolution of Christian Communities

The Armenian Genocide of 1915–1923 killed an estimated 600,000 to 1.5 million Armenians — eliminating the largest Christian community in Anatolia. The Greek-Turkish War (1919–1922) and the subsequent population exchange (1923) removed virtually the entire Greek Orthodox population from Anatolia. The Syriac Christian communities of southeastern Turkey were devastated in the same period. By the 1920s, the multi-confessional Christian presence that had existed in Anatolia for two thousand years had been essentially eliminated.

## Current Situation

The Ecumenical Patriarchate — the seat of the Eastern Orthodox Church’s highest prestige, located in the Phanar district of Istanbul — continues to operate under significant constraints. Turkey refuses to recognize the Ecumenical Patriarch’s ecumenical role beyond Turkey itself, demands that the Patriarch be a Turkish citizen (a near-impossible requirement given the size of the Turkish Orthodox community), and has kept the Halki Seminary — the Orthodox theological school — closed since 1971.

Catholic, Protestant, and evangelical churches operate in Turkey on a legal basis that requires them to hold services only in officially designated spaces. Church construction is effectively impossible. Churches can purchase or rent existing properties but face extreme bureaucratic obstacles. Pastors — particularly those of evangelical and Protestant communities — have faced violence from Turkish nationalists. Italian Catholic priest Andrea Santoro was murdered in Trabzon in 2006. Three workers at a Christian publishing house — the Malatya massacre — were tortured and killed in 2007. An American missionary was stabbed in Ankara in 2023.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~170,000 (~0.2%)
- **Primary Concerns:** Legal obstacles to church property and construction, Ecumenical Patriarchate under restriction, nationalist violence against church leaders and workers, Hagia Sophia conversion to mosque
- **Current Status:** Legal but highly restricted; nationalist violence documented; Ecumenical Patriarchate constrained
- **Open Doors WWL:** Mid-tier ranked
- **Ancient Heritage:** Paul born in Tarsus; seven churches of Revelation; four great ecumenical councils; Hagia Sophia; Ecumenical Patriarchate

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Turkey* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/turkey/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Turkey* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/turkey/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Turkey — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/turkey/
- Human Rights Watch, *Turkey country reports* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/turkey
- Aid to the Church in Need, *Turkey reporting* — acn-intl.org
- International Crisis Group, *Turkey reporting* — crisisgroup.org
- Moffett, Samuel Hugh, *A History of Christianity in Asia, Vol. I* (Orbis Books, 2003)

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Turkmenistan
TITLE: Turkmenistan — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/turkmenistan
META DESCRIPTION: Turkmenistan is one of the world’s most closed and repressive states — a cult-of-personality dictatorship where all religious activity outside state-controlled structures is illegal, churches are raided, Christians are fined and imprisoned, and any contact with foreign religious organizations is treated as a security threat.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Turkmenistan, Central Asia, Post-Soviet, Authoritarian, Religious Control, Raids, Fines, House Churches, WWL Top 20
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #19 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Turkmenistan is one of the world’s most isolated and repressive states — a Central Asian nation of approximately 6 million people governed under a cult-of-personality dictatorship that is among the most comprehensive in the world. Under President Gurbanguly Berdimuhamedov (who ruled until 2022) and now his son Serdar Berdimuhamedov, Turkmenistan has maintained an elaborate system of state control over every dimension of society including religion. All religious organizations must register with the government; unregistered religious activity is illegal; all religious meetings require state approval; religious literature must be approved before distribution; any contact with foreign religious organizations requires government permission. Christians — an estimated 5–7% of the population, predominantly ethnic Russians and other non-Turkmen minorities, with a small Turkmen convert community — face regular raids on their home worship meetings, fines, confiscation of religious materials, and in some documented cases arrest and imprisonment. Turkmenistan ranked #19 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## Government Control of Christianity

The 5,000-person minimum registration requirement — among the highest in the world — functions as an effective ban on most Christian denominations in Turkmenistan. Only the Russian Orthodox Church meets the threshold. Baptist, Seventh-day Adventist, and evangelical congregations that cannot meet the threshold operate illegally. Raids on home prayer meetings are documented regularly by Forum 18 News Service. Fines are levied on participants and leaders. Bibles and Christian literature are confiscated and destroyed. Contacts with foreign Christians or Christian organizations require government permission and are monitored.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #19
- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~5–7% (predominantly ethnic Russians; small Turkmen convert community)
- **Primary Threats:** 5,000-person registration threshold (functional ban), raids on unregistered churches, fines, confiscation of religious literature, monitoring of foreign contacts
- **Legal Status:** Christianity legal only in state-registered frameworks; unregistered practice criminal

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Turkmenistan* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/turkmenistan/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Turkmenistan* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/turkmenistan/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Turkmenistan* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- Forum 18 News Service, *Turkmenistan* — forum18.org/archive.php?country=32
- CIA World Factbook: Turkmenistan — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/turkmenistan/
- Human Rights Watch, *Turkmenistan* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/turkmenistan

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Uganda
TITLE: Uganda — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/uganda
META DESCRIPTION: Uganda is a majority-Christian nation with deep church roots — but in the north, the Lord’s Resistance Army’s 30-year campaign of terror against Christian communities remains a defining trauma. Today, the primary concern is the government’s use of anti-LGBTQ+ laws to target churches aligned with Western positions, and the continued Muslim-majority north’s social pressure on Christians.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Uganda, East Africa, Catholic, Anglican, Evangelical, LRA, Museveni, Anti-Homosexuality Act, Religious Freedom
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Uganda is approximately 84% Christian — Catholic, Anglican (Church of Uganda), evangelical, Pentecostal, and Seventh-day Adventist among the major traditions — with a genuine depth of faith embedded in Ugandan life, culture, and national history. The Uganda Martyrs — 22 Catholic converts killed on the orders of Kabaka Mwanga II in 1885–1887 for refusing to renounce Christianity — were canonized by Pope Paul VI in 1964 and remain among Uganda’s most revered figures. The Church of Uganda provided critical moral leadership through the Amin dictatorship (1971–1979). Uganda is not a country that systematically persecutes Christians by the state; it is a country with real and complex challenges in the space between religion, power, and social freedom.

## The Lord’s Resistance Army

The Lord’s Resistance Army — led by Joseph Kony, who claimed Christian and spiritual authority for his movement while conducting one of the most savage insurgencies in African history — terrorized northern Uganda from the late 1980s through the mid-2000s. The LRA abducted an estimated 30,000 children, forced girls into sexual slavery, and forced boys to kill as child soldiers. The LRA specifically targeted Christian communities, church buildings, and aid organizations in northern Uganda. While the LRA has been largely driven from Uganda, its crimes remain a profound wound in the Acholi and Langi communities of the north.

## Current Political Dynamics

Uganda’s 2023 Anti-Homosexuality Act — signed by President Museveni — includes provisions that affect religious organizations: churches that are perceived as insufficiently opposed to homosexuality or that receive Western funding that might be connected to LGBT advocacy face potential scrutiny. Some Western-aligned evangelical churches in Uganda have found themselves navigating pressure from both directions. This is a complex church-state dynamic but does not constitute systematic persecution of Christians.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~84%
- **Primary Concerns:** LRA historical trauma in northern Uganda; complex church-state dynamics around Anti-Homosexuality Act; Muslim-majority north with social pressure on converts
- **Current Status:** Majority of Christians practice freely; no state anti-Christian policy
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; moderate tier
- **Historical Significance:** Uganda Martyrs (1885–1887) — 22 Catholic converts killed by Kabaka Mwanga II; canonized 1964

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Uganda* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/uganda/
- CIA World Factbook: Uganda — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/uganda/
- Human Rights Watch, *Uganda country reports* — hrw.org/africa/uganda
- Open Doors USA, *Uganda monitoring* — opendoorsusa.org
- International Criminal Court, *Joseph Kony / LRA case documentation* — icc-cpi.int

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Ukraine
TITLE: Ukraine — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/ukraine
META DESCRIPTION: Ukraine is a deeply Orthodox Christian nation whose churches have been bombed by Russia, whose priests and pastors have been killed, and whose Christian communities in occupied territories face torture, deportation, and forced ecclesiastical submission to the Moscow Patriarchate. The war against Ukraine has a specifically Christian dimension.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Ukraine, Eastern Europe, Eastern Orthodox, Russian Invasion, Church Bombings, Moscow Patriarchate, UOC-USA, Greek Catholic, WWL
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Ukraine is a majority-Christian nation — approximately 79% Orthodox, 10% Greek Catholic (Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church), and smaller Catholic, Protestant, and evangelical communities — in the midst of a full-scale Russian invasion that has specifically targeted Christian institutions and communities. Since Russia’s February 2022 full-scale invasion, churches have been bombed, priests and pastors killed, and Christian communities in Russian-occupied territory subjected to forced ecclesiastical submission to the Moscow Patriarchate — the Russian Orthodox Church’s structure that has provided theological justification for the war.

## Ukraine’s Christian Identity

Ukraine’s Christianity is ancient — Kievan Rus received Christianity in 988 AD, a moment commemorated in the mass baptism in the Dnieper River at Kyiv (Kyiv was the original baptismal site of Eastern Slavic Christianity). The Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church — which maintains Eastern liturgical traditions while in full communion with Rome — is one of the largest Eastern Catholic churches in the world. Ukraine’s Orthodox landscape is complex: the Orthodox Church of Ukraine (OCU), granted autocephaly (ecclesiastical independence) by the Ecumenical Patriarchate of Constantinople in 2019, is now the dominant Orthodox body; the Ukrainian Orthodox Church (UOC-MP) — historically under Moscow — declared its independence from Moscow in 2022 but its canonical relationship remains contested.

## The War’s Christian Dimension

Russia’s war against Ukraine has a specifically Christian dimension that cannot be ignored. Patriarch Kirill of Moscow has provided sustained theological justification for the invasion, framing it as a struggle against Western decadence and secularism — a framing that makes the war, in his theology, a holy war. The Russian military has bombed Ukrainian churches across multiple denominations: Orthodox churches, Greek Catholic churches, Baptist churches, and evangelical congregations have all been struck. Several priests and pastors have been killed. In Russian-occupied territory, the UOC-MP — the Moscow-affiliated church — is the only Orthodox structure permitted to function, and pressure has been applied to local communities to sever their ties with the OCU.

Documented in occupied territories: the detention and torture of Protestant and Baptist pastors; the closure of OCU parishes; seizure of church buildings; deportation of clergy who refused to transfer to the ROC structure; coercion of Greek Catholics to convert to Orthodoxy.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~89%
- **Primary Concerns:** Russian military targeting of churches; killing of clergy; forced ecclesiastical submission to Moscow Patriarchate in occupied territory; detention and torture of Protestant pastors in occupied areas
- **Current Status:** Free Ukraine fully maintains religious freedom; occupied territories systematically violate it
- **Open Doors WWL:** Ranked (war context); rising
- **Historical Significance:** Kievan Rus baptism (988 AD) — original baptismal moment of Eastern Slavic Christianity; Ukrainian Greek Catholic Church — largest Eastern Catholic church; OCU received autocephaly 2019

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Ukraine* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/ukraine/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Ukraine* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Ukraine — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/ukraine/
- Human Rights Watch, *Ukraine: Russian Attacks on Religious Sites* — hrw.org/europe/central-asia/ukraine
- Amnesty International, *Ukraine* — amnesty.org/en/countries/europe-and-central-asia/ukraine/
- Forum 18 News Service, *Ukraine* — forum18.org
- Open Doors USA, *Ukraine Country Profile* — opendoorsusa.org
- UN Human Rights Monitoring Mission in Ukraine — ohchr.org

COUNTRY: United Arab Emirates
TITLE: United Arab Emirates — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/united-arab-emirates
META DESCRIPTION: The UAE is the Gulf’s most religiously tolerant state — expatriate Christians can worship in purpose-built churches, the Pope has visited, and the Abu Dhabi Declaration on Human Fraternity was signed there. But no Emirati Muslim can legally convert, apostasy has severe consequences, and no church can be built without royal permission.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: UAE, Gulf States, Islam, Expat Christians, Religious Tolerance, Apostasy, Abu Dhabi Declaration, Pope Francis Visit
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

The United Arab Emirates presents the most open face of Gulf Islam toward Christianity. The UAE — a federation of seven emirates, most prominently Abu Dhabi and Dubai — has a large expatriate Christian population: approximately 13% of its residents are Christian, representing over a million people from the Philippines, India, South Korea, Lebanon, Europe, and the West. Purpose-built churches operate in Abu Dhabi and Dubai. Pope Francis visited Abu Dhabi in February 2019 — the first papal visit to the Arabian Peninsula — and signed the Document on Human Fraternity with the Grand Imam of Al-Azhar. The Abrahamic Family House — a complex containing a mosque, a church, and a synagogue — opened in Abu Dhabi in 2023.

Yet the framework within which this openness operates is clearly bounded: no Emirati national can legally convert from Islam; apostasy carries social and legal consequences; all churches require royal permission to exist; no construction of a church can occur outside government-sanctioned structures; and Christian workers are permitted to practice but may not engage any Emirati in evangelism.

## The Limits of Tolerance

The UAE’s tolerance is a tolerance of a specific kind: it welcomes the Christianity of expatriate workers who contribute to the economy, provides them with worship facilities, and manages the optics of religious diversity in a cosmopolitan global hub. It does not extend to the conversion of Emiratis from Islam, the public proselytizing of Muslims, or the independent construction of churches outside the royal patronage framework. Every church in the UAE exists at the pleasure of the ruling families.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~13% (almost entirely expatriate)
- **Primary Restrictions:** No Emirati converts permitted; proselytism to Muslims criminal; all churches exist by royal permission; no church construction without state authorization
- **Current Status:** Among the most open Gulf states for expatriate Christians; no indigenous Emirati Christian community
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; not top tier by physical violence measure
- **Notable:** Pope Francis visit (2019) — first papal visit to Arabian Peninsula; Abrahamic Family House (2023)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: United Arab Emirates* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/united-arab-emirates/
- CIA World Factbook: United Arab Emirates — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/united-arab-emirates/
- Vatican News, *Pope Francis visit to Abu Dhabi* (February 2019) — vaticannews.va

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: United Kingdom
TITLE: United Kingdom — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/united-kingdom
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

The United Kingdom is a majority-Christian nation — approximately 46% identified as Christian in the 2021 census — undergoing rapid secularization, particularly in England and Wales. The Church of England is the established church in England; the Church of Scotland is established in Scotland; Northern Ireland has a complex relationship with Protestantism, Catholicism, and the sectarian conflict that defined its 20th-century history (the Troubles, 1968–1998). Full religious freedom exists; Christians practice without persecution. The UK is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

The UK’s global Christian history is among the most consequential of any nation: the British and Foreign Bible Society (1804) and the Religious Tract Society drove the global Bible translation movement; William Wilberforce and the Clapham Sect provided the evangelical moral energy behind the abolition of the slave trade (1807); the London Missionary Society, the Church Missionary Society, and other British mission agencies drove the 19th-century missionary expansion that established the church across Africa and Asia. Contemporary legal debates over religious expression — hate speech laws, public preaching, sex education in church schools — continue.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~46% (nominal; active practice much lower)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; ongoing legal debates over religious expression in public life
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Significance:** Major driver of global Bible translation movement and 19th-century missions; abolition of slave trade (1807) driven by evangelical Christians

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: United Kingdom* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/united-kingdom/
- CIA World Factbook: United Kingdom — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/united-kingdom/
- Office for National Statistics (ONS), *2021 Census: Religion* — ons.gov.uk

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: United States
TITLE: United States — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/united-states
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

The United States is approximately 63% Christian — approximately 24% Catholic, 15% mainline Protestant, 14% evangelical Protestant, and 7% other Christian traditions — with the remainder non-religious (26%), religiously unaffiliated, or of other faiths. Christians practice fully freely under the First Amendment’s robust protection of religious freedom. The United States is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. Americans are not persecuted for their Christianity.

The United States has been the single most significant driver of global Christian missions in the modern era — sending and funding more missionaries, Bible translation projects, and Christian relief organizations than any other nation. American evangelical and Pentecostal Christianity has shaped global Christianity profoundly. The global Open Doors World Watch List that this database references is published by Open Doors USA, headquartered in Santa Ana, California.

Domestically, legal debates over the First Amendment’s religion clauses continue: battles over religious exemptions, public religious expression, chaplaincy programs, and the limits of secular public institutions’ authority over religious institutions. These are legal and cultural debates in one of the world’s most robust frameworks for religious liberty — not persecution.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~63% (declining from ~75% in 2000)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom — among the strongest constitutional protections for religious liberty in the world
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Global Impact:** The world’s largest single source of missionary activity and Christian humanitarian funding

### Sources & References

- Pew Research Center, *America’s Changing Religious Landscape* — pewresearch.org/religion/

- CIA World Factbook: United States — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/united-states/

COUNTRY: Uruguay
TITLE: Uruguay — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/uruguay
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Uruguay is one of Latin America’s most secular nations — approximately 43% identify as Christian, 40% as non-religious, and the remainder as other. Uruguay was the first Latin American country to formally separate church and state (1918), and its secular liberal tradition is among the most established in the region. Christians practice entirely freely; there is no persecution. Uruguay is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. The country’s challenges for the church are not persecution but secularism — ministering in a culture where religious indifference is perhaps more common than hostility.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~43% (lowest in Latin America)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution; highly secular society
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

COUNTRY: Venezuela
TITLE: Venezuela — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/venezuela
META DESCRIPTION: Venezuela is a nominally Christian nation in political and economic collapse, where the Maduro government has increasingly pressured the Catholic Church and evangelical communities that challenge its authority. Pastors face threats; the church provides humanitarian services the state cannot.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Venezuela, South America, Catholic, Evangelical, Maduro, Church-State Tension, Persecution, Humanitarian Crisis
PERSECUTION TIER: Moderate

## Overview

Venezuela is approximately 88% Christian — predominantly Catholic, with a significant and growing evangelical Protestant community. The country has been in a state of political and economic collapse since approximately 2015, with GDP falling by more than half, hyperinflation destroying savings, and over seven million Venezuelans emigrating in one of the largest displacement crises in Western Hemisphere history. The church — Catholic and evangelical — has stepped into the humanitarian vacuum, providing food, medicine, and social services that the collapsed state cannot deliver. This social role has made the church a target of government suspicion and occasional explicit pressure.

The Catholic Church in Venezuela has been one of the most consistent institutional critics of the Maduro government. Cardinal Baltazar Porras and other bishops have issued public statements denouncing political repression, human rights violations, and the humanitarian crisis. The government has responded with accusations of church interference in politics, surveillance of church activities, and verbal attacks on bishops. Evangelical pastors who have spoken publicly about justice, democracy, or humanitarian needs have in some cases received threats or been monitored by government-aligned organizations.

Venezuela is on the Open Doors World Watch List monitoring tier. This is not systematic state persecution on the scale of Cuba or Nicaragua — but the directional trend is toward more hostility.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~88%
- **Primary Concerns:** Government pressure on Catholic Church leadership for political speech; surveillance; threats to evangelical pastors; humanitarian crisis creating pressure on church as service provider
- **Current Status:** Most Christians practice freely; political speech by church leaders brings government retaliation
- **Open Doors WWL:** Monitored; lower tier

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Venezuela* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/venezuela/
- CIA World Factbook: Venezuela — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/venezuela/
- Human Rights Watch, *Venezuela country reports* — hrw.org/americas/venezuela
- Open Doors USA, *Venezuela monitoring* — opendoorsusa.org

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Vietnam
TITLE: Vietnam — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/vietnam
META DESCRIPTION: Vietnam is a one-party communist state where Christianity has grown to over 8 million despite — and because of — decades of persecution. Unregistered house churches, ethnic minority Christians, and independent Catholic communities face raids, arrests, land seizures, and forced recantation. The church grows in the underground; the government tightens its grip.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Vietnam, Southeast Asia, Communism, CPV, House Church, Hmong Christians, Montagnards, Forced Recantation, WWL Top 20, Catholic, Evangelical
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #19 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Vietnam is a one-party communist state governed by the Communist Party of Vietnam (CPV), which controls all religious life through mandatory registration, surveillance, and the consistent suppression of religious activity outside state-approved frameworks. Christianity — both Catholic and Protestant — has grown significantly despite this, with an estimated 8–9 million Christians today representing roughly 8% of the population. The Vietnamese church is one of the most remarkable growth stories in Asian Christianity — a church that endured the fall of Saigon, the re-education camps, the confiscation of church properties, decades of suppression, and has come out on the other side numerically stronger. Vietnam ranked #19 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List.

## The Catholic Church in Vietnam

Catholicism arrived in Vietnam through Portuguese and later French missionaries from the 16th century. By the time of Vietnamese independence, the Catholic Church had been present for over three centuries and had built an enormous network of schools, hospitals, and social institutions. The fall of South Vietnam in 1975 brought immediate suppression: church properties were confiscated, foreign missionaries expelled, priests and bishops monitored, seminaries closed or strictly limited, and religious education of youth forbidden. The church withdrew to its pastoral core — Mass, sacraments, catechesis — and survived. The government has progressively allowed greater Catholic institutional life since the 1990s, though control remains tight.

## The Protestant and Evangelical Church

Protestant Christianity in Vietnam is both older (through French colonial-era missions) and dramatically newer — the Evangelical Church of Vietnam (ECVN), established through the Christian and Missionary Alliance, grew rapidly in the 20th century and established the primary registered Protestant body in Vietnam. Since the late 1980s and especially since the 2000s, independent Pentecostal and evangelical house churches have grown explosively. The Vietnamese government requires all religious bodies to register; unregistered church activity is illegal. Many house churches operate without registration and face raids.

## Ethnic Minority Christians: Hmong and Montagnards

The most acute persecution in Vietnam is directed at ethnic minority Christians. Two communities stand out:

The Hmong people — a highland ethnic minority concentrated in the northern provinces of Ha Giang, Lao Cai, Son La, and others — converted to Christianity in extraordinary numbers beginning in the 1980s and 1990s, primarily through Radio Free Asia and Voice of America Christian broadcasting in the Hmong language, and then through personal testimony. Hundreds of thousands of Hmong have become Christians. Village authorities have responded with a documented pattern of persecution: demands that converts sign recantation documents, destruction of church buildings, confiscation of Bibles, expulsion from villages, denial of official documents to those who refuse to sign, and in some cases physical violence.

The Montagnard peoples of the Central Highlands — predominantly Protestant, many converted through the Christian and Missionary Alliance’s work in the 20th century — have faced sustained suppression connected both to their religious identity and to their history of support for the United States during the Vietnam War. Their churches have been bulldozed; their pastors arrested; their community lands confiscated by the state for economic development. Many Montagnard Christians have fled to Cambodia and sought refugee status.

## Current Status

Vietnam’s government has progressively expanded the formal space for registered religious activity while maintaining tight control. Registered churches can operate; unregistered churches cannot. The suppression falls most heavily on three groups: unregistered house churches in urban areas, Hmong converts in northern highlands, and Montagnard Christians in the Central Highlands.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #19
- **Estimated Christian Population:** 8–9 million (~8%)
- **Primary Threats:** Registration requirements (functional ban on independent churches), raids on house churches, forced recantation of Hmong Christians, Montagnard persecution, land seizures from church communities
- **Most Affected:** Hmong highland Christians (north), Montagnard Christians (Central Highlands), unregistered urban house churches
- **Growth:** From near-zero Protestant (1900) to 8–9 million Christians (2024) — despite sustained persecution

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Vietnam* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/vietnam/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Vietnam* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/vietnam/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Vietnam* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Vietnam — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/vietnam/
- Human Rights Watch, *Vietnam country reports* — hrw.org/asia/vietnam
- Amnesty International, *Vietnam* — amnesty.org/en/countries/asia-and-the-pacific/vietnam/
- Christian Solidarity Worldwide, *Vietnam* — csw.org.uk
- Forum 18 News Service, *Vietnam monitoring* — forum18.org
- International Christian Concern, *Vietnam* — persecution.org/country/vietnam/

# ================================================================================

=================================================================================

COUNTRY: Yemen
TITLE: Yemen — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/yemen
META DESCRIPTION: Yemen is the Arab world’s most catastrophic humanitarian disaster and one of the world’s most dangerous countries for the handful of Christians who exist within it. With no indigenous Christian community of any scale, conversion is apostasy, apostasy is death, and the few known believers are isolated, endangered, and invisible.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Yemen, Middle East, Islam, Apostasy, Houthi, Civil War, No Christians, WWL Top 5, Humanitarian Crisis
PERSECUTION TIER: High
WWL RANKING: #4 (Open Doors 2024)

## Overview

Yemen ranked #4 on the 2024 Open Doors World Watch List. It is the Arab world’s most catastrophic humanitarian emergency — a multi-sided civil war that has killed over 150,000 people in direct violence and an estimated 377,000 through war-related famine, disease, and displacement since 2015. Into this catastrophe, add the fact that Yemen is approximately 99.9% Muslim, that Christianity has no indigenous presence of any meaningful scale, that apostasy from Islam is a capital offense under the prevailing legal frameworks of all major Yemeni factions, and that the handful of known Yemeni Christians — estimated at a few hundred at most — live in conditions of absolute concealment, cut off from one another, with no institutional support and no community, surrounded by a conflict that makes normal danger feel remote. Yemen is not merely a dangerous country for Christians; it is a country where Christianity’s invisibility is the primary condition of survival.

## Historical Background

Yemen has no significant ancient Christian heritage. Christianity was present briefly in the pre-Islamic period — the Aksumite kingdom of Ethiopia colonized Yemen in the 6th century and brought Christianity; the king of Yemen at the time of Muhammad’s birth is historically associated with events connected to the Christian tradition. But the Islamic conquest was rapid and complete. Islam has been Yemen’s faith for fourteen centuries.

## The Houthi Factor

The Houthi movement — formally Ansar Allah — which controls northwest Yemen including the capital Sanaa, operates under an explicitly Islamic framework in which proselytism to Muslims and apostasy from Islam are capital offenses. Houthi theology draws on Zaidi Shia traditions and has been significantly influenced by Iranian revolutionary Islam. In Houthi-controlled territory, the enforcement of Islamic practice is comprehensive.

## Key Facts

- **Open Doors WWL Ranking (2024):** #4
- **Indigenous Christian Population:** Estimated a few hundred at most; effectively zero as a community
- **Primary Threats:** Apostasy death penalty under all major Yemeni factions; civil war making already-dangerous conditions catastrophic; Houthi enforcement of strict Islamic law in controlled areas
- **Legal Status:** Christianity entirely illegal in any organized form; apostasy capital offense
- **Humanitarian Context:** 377,000+ war-related deaths; 21 million in need of humanitarian assistance

### Sources & References

- Open Doors USA, *World Watch List 2024: Yemen* — opendoorsusa.org/christian-persecution/world-watch-list/yemen/
- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Yemen* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/yemen/
- U.S. Commission on International Religious Freedom (USCIRF), *2024 Annual Report: Yemen* — uscirf.gov/reports-briefs/uscirf-annual-report
- CIA World Factbook: Yemen — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/yemen/
- UN OCHA, *Yemen Humanitarian Situation Reports* — unocha.org/yemen
- Human Rights Watch, *Yemen country reports* — hrw.org/middle-east/north-africa/yemen

COUNTRY: Zambia
TITLE: Zambia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/zambia
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Zambia is approximately 95% Christian and is formally declared a Christian nation — the December 1991 constitutional declaration by President Frederick Chiluba made Zambia one of the few countries in the world to formally declare itself a Christian state. The declaration is more symbolic than restrictive — all religious groups practice freely, and minority faiths are not oppressed. Christianity arrived primarily through the influence of David Livingstone, who died in Zambia (in what was then Northern Rhodesia) in 1873 while continuing his explorations and advocacy against the slave trade. The Zambia the missionaries established — through schools, hospitals, and churches that became the foundation of the country’s social infrastructure — reflects that legacy. Zambia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~95%
- **Current Status:** Declared Christian nation (1991); full religious freedom for all; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** David Livingstone died in Zambia (1873) while engaged in missionary and anti-slavery work

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Zambia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/zambia/
- CIA World Factbook: Zambia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/zambia/

COUNTRY: Zimbabwe
TITLE: Zimbabwe — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/zimbabwe
META DESCRIPTION: Zimbabwe is a majority-Christian nation where most believers practice freely, but the government has a documented pattern of using registration laws against apostolic and independent churches, and church leaders who engage in political advocacy face monitoring and harassment.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Zimbabwe, Southern Africa, Christian Majority, African Independent Churches, Apostolic Churches, Mugabe, Mnangagwa, Church Registration, Political Advocacy
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Zimbabwe is approximately 85% Christian — with the Catholic, Anglican, Methodist, and evangelical traditions all present, alongside a uniquely large community of African Apostolic and Independent Churches (AICs) that blend Christian theology with African spiritual practice and prophetic tradition. Christianity arrived through the London Missionary Society (Robert Moffat established the Kuruman mission in the 1820s; David Livingstone worked from that base) and subsequently through Catholic and Anglican missions. Under Robert Mugabe (1980–2017), the government had a complex relationship with the church: Mugabe himself was Catholic-educated, and the church maintained significant institutional life, but church leaders who spoke about human rights, election violence, or land seizure faced monitoring and harassment.

Under President Emmerson Mnangagwa (2017–present), the overall relationship between state and church remains complex. The government has used registration requirements to pressure some independent Apostolic Churches. Church leaders who engage publicly in political commentary or human rights advocacy face government attention. The violence of Operation Restore Order (2005) and election-related violence in 2008 and 2018 — in which church buildings were at times used as shelters for the displaced — illustrates the church’s humanitarian role in a state that sometimes uses force against its own people.

Zimbabwe is not on the Open Doors World Watch List. The majority of Zimbabwean Christians practice freely.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~85%
- **Current Status:** Majority practice freely; church leaders who engage in political advocacy face harassment; registration requirements used against some AICs
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Historical Note:** David Livingstone and Robert Moffat established foundational missionary work in the region

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Zimbabwe* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/zimbabwe/
- CIA World Factbook: Zimbabwe — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/zimbabwe/
- Human Rights Watch, *Zimbabwe country reports* — hrw.org/africa/zimbabwe

## Overview

Andorra is a tiny landlocked principality in the Pyrenees between France and Spain, with a population of approximately 77,000. It holds a constitutional curiosity: its two co-princes are the Bishop of Urgell (a Catholic bishop in Spain) and the President of the French Republic — making Catholicism structurally embedded in its governance in a way unlike any other state. Approximately 90% of Andorrans identify as Catholic. Christians practice entirely freely; there is no persecution. Andorra is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

COUNTRY: Australia
TITLE: Australia — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/australia
META DESCRIPTION: Australia is a majority-Christian nation with full religious freedom. The church’s role in Indigenous Australian history carries deep wounds, and the Catholic clergy abuse crisis produced a major royal commission. Christians practice freely while navigating a rapidly secularizing society.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Australia, Oceania, Catholic, Anglican, Evangelical, Religious Freedom, Indigenous Christianity, Abuse Scandal, Secularization
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Australia is approximately 44% Christian — a figure that has dropped significantly from 74% in 1991, reflecting one of the steeper secularizations in the Western world — with Anglican, Catholic, Uniting Church, and evangelical and Pentecostal communities all present. Christians practice entirely freely under full constitutional protection. Australia is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

## Christianity in Australia

Christianity arrived with British colonization in 1788. The Church of England was effectively established through the colonial period. Catholic missionaries followed, primarily serving Irish convict and immigrant communities. Pentecostal and charismatic Christianity has grown substantially since the late 20th century; Hillsong Church — founded in Sydney in 1983 — became one of the most globally influential evangelical worship movements of the 21st century.

## Indigenous Christianity and Colonial Wounds

The relationship between Christianity and Australia’s Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islander peoples is complex and painful. Missionaries were often among those who participated in the removal of Indigenous children from their families — the “Stolen Generations” — a policy that caused generational trauma and is a source of ongoing national reckoning. At the same time, Indigenous Australian Christianity is deeply rooted in many communities. The Uniting Church in Australia has been a significant institutional voice for Indigenous rights.

## The Royal Commission into Institutional Child Sexual Abuse

Australia’s Royal Commission into Institutional Responses to Child Sexual Abuse (2013–2017) was one of the most comprehensive investigations of church-related abuse in any country. It received over 8,000 personal accounts of abuse and examined the response of institutions including the Catholic Church, Anglican Church, and others. The Commission made 409 recommendations. The findings significantly accelerated secularization and fundamentally changed the Catholic Church’s approach to child protection in Australia.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~44% (declining from 74% in 1991)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked
- **Notable:** Hillsong Church (1983, Sydney) — globally influential worship movement; Royal Commission into Institutional Child Sexual Abuse (2013–2017)

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Australia* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/australia/
- CIA World Factbook: Australia — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/australia/
- Australian Bureau of Statistics, *2021 Census: Religion* — abs.gov.au
- Royal Commission into Institutional Responses to Child Sexual Abuse, *Final Report* (2017) — childabuseroyalcommission.gov.au

COUNTRY: Austria
TITLE: Austria — Christianity & Persecution Profile
SLUG: country-profiles/austria
META DESCRIPTION: Austria is a historically Catholic Central European nation with full religious freedom. The church played a defining role in Habsburg empire-building and in Central European Christian civilization for centuries. Today Austria is secularizing but Christians practice without restriction.
CATEGORY: Country Profiles
TAGS: Austria, Central Europe, Catholic, Lutheran, Religious Freedom, Habsburg, Secularization
PERSECUTION TIER: Low

## Overview

Austria is approximately 57% Roman Catholic — though active practice is declining — with Lutheran, evangelical, and Orthodox minorities. The country has full constitutional religious freedom; Christians face no persecution. Austria is not on the Open Doors World Watch List.

Austria’s Christian history is inseparable from the history of the Habsburg dynasty, which ruled the Holy Roman Empire and its successor states for centuries and positioned itself as the defender of Catholic Christianity in Central Europe. Vienna was besieged by Ottoman forces twice — in 1529 and again in 1683 — and the successful defense of 1683 (in which Polish King Jan III Sobieski’s forces were decisive) is celebrated in Catholic tradition as a providential deliverance. The composers Haydn, Mozart, Schubert, and Bruckner all wrote profoundly within the Catholic sacred music tradition.

## Key Facts

- **Estimated Christian Population:** ~62% (Catholic majority; declining active practice)
- **Current Status:** Full religious freedom; no persecution
- **Open Doors WWL:** Not ranked

### Sources & References

- U.S. Department of State, *2023 Report on International Religious Freedom: Austria* — state.gov/reports/2023-report-on-international-religious-freedom/austria/
- CIA World Factbook: Austria — cia.gov/the-world-factbook/countries/austria/
- Pew Research Center, *Being Christian in Western Europe* (2018) — pewresearch.org/religion/2018/05/29/being-christian-in-western-europe/

COUNTRY: Kiribati
TITLE: Kiribati — Christianity & Persecution Profile

CATEGORY: Country Profiles

## Overview

Kiribati is a remote Pacific nation of approximately 120,000 people spread across 33 atolls in the central Pacific Ocean. Approximately 96% of the population is Christian — Catholic (57%) and Protestant (Kiribati Protestant Church, 34%) — with Christianity having arrived through London Missionary Society and Sacred Heart Catholic missionaries in the 19th century.
